THE RED PAGE.
James Brunton Stephens.
L
JAMES BRUNTON STEPHENS was born
at Bo’ness, on the Firth of Forth, in Linlithgow-
I shire, on the 17th June, 1335. He was the
youngest son in a family of six—two brothers
'and three sisters. His father was the master of the
parish school. His mother’s family traced itself back to
George Buchanan (tutor to Janies VI of Scotland), a
writer with a European reputation, whom Camden has
spoken of as “easily the first poet of his age ; whom
Dryden acknowledges as “comparable to any of the
moderns and excelled by few of the ancients, and whom
Hr. Johnson has referred to as “ a great poetical genius,”
and “ the only man of genius whom his country ever
produced.” In the Bo’ness parish school was housed the
town library, to which young Stephens had free access.
Fielding, Smollett, Shakespeare, and the English classics
were more to the boy’s taste than athletics, for which he
never showed any inclination.
At 11 his father died, and a friend secured him
a presentation to a Foundation School in Edinburgh, not
far away. A delicate, shy, nervous lad, the tyranny, im-
morality and brutality of his surroundings were torture
to him. He loathed the place, in which he remained for
four years. He left it to join the University classes,
taking pupils to pay his fees. When the classes were in
vacation, he wandered off to earn enough to pay his way
(in the familiar Scotch*student fashion), and, when work
was resumed, he took his classes in Greek, moral
philosophy and mathematics at the University, with
Latin, logic and mental philosophy at the New College,
Edinburgh. In each of these branches of study he secured
an honourable place, but was nevertheless forced to
abandon his course ere proceeding to a degree, or, rather,
having got the groundwork of the culture he desired,
he was tempted away from the mere diploma by an
invitation to become a travelling tutor to the family of a
Massey-Pawson. The position was secured for him
through the influence of a fellow student named Simp-
son, whose father later on became the discoverer of
chloroform as an anaesthetic —and a knight. With the
Massey-Dawsons young Stephens visited Paris, where
they stayed many months, and where he learned French.
The Mas'sey-Dawgons had entertained Napoleon 111 in his
poorer days in London, and in turn were entertained by
that potentate during their visit to Paris. They wintered
in Pisa, returned to Paris, and thence to London, where
Stephens left them and again became a travelling tutor
to the family of Captain Leyland of the Guards.
&
The Leylands were very wealthy. They
“ literally rolled in riches.” Their private yacht carried
a doctor, an honorary captain, as well as a practical sail-
ing master, and a crew of thirty. They visited Gibraltar,
Marseilles, Genoa, Palermo, Athens, Constantinople,
Sebastopol, Jaffa, Damascus and Cairo, making many
excursions inland. They called in at the Crimea while
the troops were vacating the place (March, 1856). From
Jaffa they travelled through Palestine-; and from
Cairo they sailed up the Nile, spending in all eighteen
months in the Mediterranean. During this period
Stephens’s first attempts at poetry are recorded. In an
old pocket-book are preserved two sets of verses remini-
scent of the voyage, viz., “Loose Leaves from Olivet,”
dated “Jerusalem, 1857,” and “Nile Glances.” “Nile
Glances” is purely descriptive of the Nile trip. It is
interesting description, but crude poetry, disclosing no
indication of the latent genius of its author :
A glance on the past, when we wandered awhile,
Borne on the breast of the sunbright Nile,_
Leaving Winter behind for a Summer fair,
Drinking new life from the balmy air.
It consists of eighty lines, and remains unfinished.
“Loose Leaves from Olivet” comprises nine verses of
haphazard metre savouring of the influence of Tennyson
and disclosing an orthodox religious earnestness. The
opening verse runs—
Not the mere name of it, —
Not the mere fame of it,
No more a mere story or vision of air,
But Olivet’s self , parched, solid, and bare,—
Olivet, mountain of iveeping and pray’r ;
Bethpage—Bethany—
Kedron—Gethsemane —
Eastward and westward behold them all there l
J*
When the Leylands returned to London and
settled down, Stephens left them, and in 1861 became a
teacher in the Greenock Academy. A student in the
Academy at the time supplies the following : “ Well do I
remember his tall, thin figure, clear-cut and almost
feminine cast of features, his genial smile and winning
ways.” He took an interest in the boys not only as a
pedagogue and taskmaster, but as a private friend.
The senior class used to meet at the house of the
headmaster on Friday evenings, where Stephens joined
in reading Shakespeare’s plays, each of us taking a char-
acter. Private theatricals were arranged, and Stephens
used to drill the boys in their parts, and train their
elocutionary powers. On one occasion he wrote a song
to be introduced in “ Bombastes Furioso,” which we
were rehearsing. On a later occasion he himself took
the part of Sally Blunt, the Irish maid of all work, in a
piece, when his long figure and rollicking acting caused
the whole play to go as an immense success.”
By this time he had developed into a writer of
verses for Sharpe’s Magazine and Chambers’s Journal.
He wrote also for newspapers, and printed two short
stories entitled “Rutson Morley” and “Virtue Lemoyne.”
Some of these Greenock verses which have been preserved
are serious, and some are written in the lighter vein that
was later to reach its highest development in “ The
Godolphin Arabian” and “My Chinee Cook.” But he
suffered from rheumatics and decided to emigrate to a
warmer climate. On the 28th December, 1865, he left
Gravesend in the Blackball liner Flying Cloud, bound for
Brisbane. Aboard the same vessel was Horace Earle, the
present secretary of the Johnsonian Club (Brisbane). In
the “ settling down ” process these two came together and
formed a close friendship that existed up to the day of
Stephens’s death. Mr. Earle describes him as having a
middle-age appearance at that time, “ looking, in fact,
very little younger than when he died,” although he was
then only‘3o years old. “He made very few friends
during the voyage, was well-liked by the passengers—a
good conversationalist, and very entertaining."
The Flying Cloud arrived in Moreton Bay on
the 30th April, 1866. Landing in Brisbane, Stephens
went to stay at Kangaroo Point with a cousin—a Mrs.
Hill, wife o‘f Walter Hill, first director of the Brisbane
Botanical Gardens. Within a week he delivered a lecture
on “ The Antiquities of Egypt ” in the Brisbane School of
Arts, which occupied at that time the present site of the
Queensland National Bank. The lecture is described by
one of the audience as “interesting, but not well
delivered.” He tried for various positions without suc-
cess, and meanwhile .took pupils at evening classes at
Kangaroo Point. One of his pupils was Robert Philp, a
lad destined afterwards to become Premier of Queens-
land, with Stephens as his Under-Secretary. For a little
while he held the post of proof reader on the Guardian
newspaper, and later, published a MS. Pmich in con-
junction with J. A. Clarke, artist, etcher, etc., which ran
into three issues.
In 1869 he went to Tamrookum as tutor to the
Barker family. Tamrookum was a cattle-station on the
Logan, 6C miles from Brisbane by road. He was allotted
a bed and sitting room in the bachelors’ quarters. “ I
am by a jollv log fire,” he writes to his Kangaroo Point
friends. .. “ repeatedlv interrupted. One of the inter-
ruptions waS not * hor-e ’ hut ‘ dog ’ —a family affair. ‘At
Tamrookum on the 10th ins(., under Mr. Stephens’ bed,
Tiny, of five pups—mother and children doing a great
deal better than they deserve.’ ” The bush depressed
him, and although the Barkers were kind to
him he was lonely and miserable. His heart was
back at Kangaroo Point with the Hills and Earles,
and the Donaldsons and Clarke, and the genial com-
pany and the warm hospitality that he had become
accustomed to. “I feel as tender and homesick as I
used to do in my school-days when the holidays were
over,” he wrote to Horace Earle ; “ I picture you all
sitting to-night under the bright lamps . . . and Don
Quixote and the ‘ Pussy, pussy, come to baby,’ and all the
rest of it, and I wish, I wish I were with you in body as 1
am in spirit.” Again : “ Oh, how sick I was at dinner
to-day with horse, horse, horse, and it will be horse,
horse, horse day after day for six long months.” And
again : “I am still fretting against the bush. What has
roused me to-day is seeing M and B going off to
Brisbane. I am in the land of monotony ... deep in
Euripides. W was here on Saturday and Sunday,
and all was horse, horse, horse. • —— is here to-night,
and I literally fled from the drawingroom to avoid the
eternal horse, horse, horse.” His Tamrookum letters
contain commissions of all sorts, ranging from boots and
troupers to Adams’A iniquities and Homer. In onehe relates
how he tendered his resignation as a protest against the
repeated removal of his charges to help in the station
work, and of how his pretest prevailed, and his resigna-
tion was withdrawn. In another occurs this significant
item : “In case anything goes wrong about posts pre-
venting you from hearing from me ere the beginning of
the August mail for England, please send registered a
draft for £l5 on the Koyal Bank of Scotland to Jane
Stephens, 32 Ann-street, Greenock.” He was always a
good son and brother, and to the day of his death con-
tinued to help his people.
Jt
It was at Tamrookum that “Convict Once”
was produced. It was written in three weeks. As was
his custom, the MS. was first submitted to his Kan-
garoo Point friends. Horace Earle sent it to Professor
Masson, then Professor of Literature in the Edin-
burgh University, afterwards first editor of Macmillan’s
Magazine. Masson kept the MS. for six months before
reading it. Afterwards, he wrote to Stephens’s sister
statibg that when he started to read it he could not leave
it down until he had finished it. He adds : “ That young
man, a tutor in the Australian bush, is a remarkable
poet.” Stephens was elated at the verdict. In a letter to
Earle he writes : “ Oh, my dear old boy, read the accom-
panying copy of a letter from Professor Masson, and, by
your gratification, judge of mine. I give yon my word
of honour lam taken by surprise. I really and truly had
given the thing up as halt, maimed and rotten.” But he
continues: “My head isn’t turned, boys. The tug of
war is to come yet. Macmillan the Rhadamanthushas yet
to bring the commercial judgment to bear. But even if
nothing else comes of it I am exceedingly gratified.”
Macmillan was slow to move, and many weary months
went by before his decision was received. “ Macmillan is
still silent,” Stephens from Tamrookum; “My
sisters have n’t had a single line from him. As you may
suppose, lam in a devil of a rage about it. As I have
told you before, I could stand a flat rejection with perfect
complacency. But to have one’s hopes stirred up to
boiling point and then allowed to flicker month after
month —why I’d much rather not have had them kindled
at all, or else have them trampled out at once: ... If I
were faithfully to put on paper my feelings with
reference to the matter, you would see expressed
in black upon white my fervent wish that Macmillan
would go to the devil and take the MSS. with
him. . . . But it’s always the way, Horace, whenever I
go beyond my plain school-mastering vocation I merely
lay up for myself stores of disappointment. Therefore I
say once more, Vive la birch ! Poetry he blowed ! W ould-
be authorship confounded !—and may everybody go to the
deuce except ourselves and our fellow-creatures.”
Ultimately, Macmillan undertook to publish Convict
Once. Professor Masson read and corrected the proofs.
It was most favourably received by the English critics,
but was not a financial' success. Freni Tamrookum fol-
lowed verses in lighter vein— “My Chinese Cook,” “My
Other Chinese Cook," “ Ode to a Black Gin,”—and a year
later “ The Godolphin Arabian ” appeared. Well-meaning
friends had said to him after the appearance of Convict
Once, “Yes, it’s very fine, Stephens; but why don’t
you give us something racy of the soil —something
horsey?” “So I sat down,” he once told a friend, “and
crammed myself with Stonehenge’s Rural Sports and
other works on the horse, and produced ‘ The Godolphin
Arabian.’ My horsey friends were delighted. “That’s
the sort of thing that will take,” they used to tell me.
In May, 1870, he wrote to Earle, telling him of an
offer he had received to contribute to a new paper which
was starting in Ipswich (Q.) “I think I’ll have a shy at it,”
he says. “It may lead to summit. In fact, if I only had
subjects I’d keep plying the newspapers in all directions
till I come down, so as to get a chance at some occu-
pation by and bye.” Three months later he excuses
himself for not writing “ owing to the barrenness of the
Tamrookum soil. This week it is as unpregnant as ever.
Mr. B will be home in a month, and if he has any
other arrangement for the boys, I may be going adrift
altogether. Whichever way it is decided I shall n’t be
sorry. I think I’d rather be cut adrift on the whole and
compelled to make a push, for this here life is mere
barren ease and unprogressive monotony.”
At times he rode into Brisbane from the station.
Returning once, he left the track and passed a wet night
lost in the bush. Finally he left Tamrookum and secured
an appointment as a teacher in the Normal school,
Brisbane. He held the position a while, and left it
to return to the bush as tutor to the family of
Captain Sherwood at Unumgai station, on the Richmond
River. A while later he was back in Brisbane again, and
secured a prize of £lOO offered by the Queenslander, then
edited by Gresley Lukin, for a serial story entitled “ .A 100.”
It was his first and only novel, atid he was never very
proud of it. It appeared afterwards in book form. Later
he went to Stanthorpe as a teacher in the State school.
The local colour of the tin-field inspired his “Quart-pot
Creek,” “A Lost Chance;” and a fork of Byron’s, the
property of Colonel Pitts, a resident of Stanthorpe, gave
rise to the verses on that theme.
Jt
In Brisbane, in 1876, he married Rosalie
Donaldson, whose family formed one of the Kangaroo
Point circle, and whose sister married his old friend J.
A. Clarke. Men of Stephens’s temperament too often have
made mistakes in marrying—it is sufficient to say that
Stephens married the predestined woman. Shortly after
his marriage he was appointed to the Ashgrove State
school, near Brisbane. Here he did much writing
for the press. The Melbourne Argus, Punch, and
Australasian published many of his pieces ; to the
Brisbane Courier he became a regular contributor of
light matter; while writings such as “ Marsupial Bill,”
“ Roley Poley,” and “ Christmas Night in the Museum,”
illustrated by his friend Clarke, popularised the Christ-
mas numbers of the Queenslander. At Ashgrove three of
his children were born, and there he remained for six
years.
Jt
During this period Sir Arthur Kennedy was
Governor of Queensland. His daughter, Miss Kennedy,
had a great admiration for Stephens as a writer, and
interested herself in him. “ Cannot you find something
better for such a genius as Brunton Stephens,” she said
to Thos. Mcllwraith, then Premier, “ than a country
school—some position in which a man of his gifts would
have better opportunities of developing his great
talents ? ” The result was that Stephens was shortly after
transferred to ,the Colonial Secretary’s office as corres-
ponding clerk. At a later period he became chief clerk
and ultimately Under-Secretary in the Chief Secretary’s
Department. In his altered capacity he served the State
for 21 years. He died on the 29th June, 1902, from a
heart trouble, and lies buried in the picturesque South
Brisbane cemetery. His widow and'’five children—four
girls and a boy—survive him.
F.K.
TEARS.
These are the blown spindrift that is lashed from the face
of the waters
That cover the Soul with Care;
These are the Children of Sorrow, these are the sons and
the daughters
Sped forth from thy house, Despair !
Spray that is flung on the desolate cliffs from the deeps
of sea-sources
To lie, like a veil, on our biers ;
Children that follow the plumes and the step of the
stately black horses ;
Slow mourners, sure comforters —Tears !
Will H. Ogilvik, in The Spectator.
SETTING AUSTRALIA TO MUSIC
“ Puck ” complains that “L.B.’s” article, “An Aus-
tralian Pastoral Symphony” (21/2/3) described nothing
distinctively Australian beyond the black swans and the
mopoke ; and suggests a symphony based on “ the big
plains of the interior” or “fern gullies,” “gum-trees,”
and “the death-like silence of Aust. mountain scenery.”
“ L. 8.” replies at length, and points out that “Puck ” “for-
gets that a symphony is a work of sound, not of silence. His
‘ silent-plain-and-wind-through-she-oaks symphony ’ is
the easiest thing in the world. Here it is : Orchestra of
eighty performers, two thousand bars rest. Sole per-
former, the drummer, who is producing the 1 wind
through the she-oaks ’ by' placing a large sheet of paper
upon one of the tympani and rubbing it with a brush.
Would such a performance fill Melbourne Town Hull ?
Even Saint-Saens’ Desert has a charming oasis in it.”
The case is judged in “L.B.’s” favour, of course:
England (which “ Puck ” compares) has not the charac-
teristic components of such a musical scene as “ L. 8.”
described. And “ L. 8.” adds these further notations of
Australian bird and insect cries :
WHEN SCOT MAULED SCOT
Thomas Carlyle’s family has smarted for twenty
years over the picture of Carlyle left by Froude’s pen ;
and two volumes of New Letters and Memorials of Jane
Welsh Carlyle have just been published to set down the
wife’s share of the Carlyle household strife. In truth she
was the “ bitter little woman ” that Rossetti called her :
her pen ran vitriol. This passage from one of her letters
of 1845 will interest our Gov.-General—
Did you know that Alfred Tennyson is to have a
pension of a year after all ? Peel has stated his
intention of recommending him to Her Gracious
Majesty, and that is considered final : “ A ehaqu’un
selon sa capaciU!” Lady Harriet told me that he
wanted to marry ; “must have a woman to live beside ;
would prefer d lady, but—cannot afford one ; .and so
must marry a maid-servant.” Mrs Henry Taylor said
she was about to write him on behalf of their house-
maid, who was quite a superior character in her way.
The venom of it! Yet conceivably Jane Carlyle might
have been happy and kindly. Her life went awry
simply for want of a Highland family of sixteen or so ;
and the barren woman’s hysteria permanently clouded her
br jas. Crichton-Browne, w’ho introduces the New Letters,
refers to Froude’s treatment of his hero thus : “He ex-
ceeded all editorial license in his manipulation of the
documents placed in his hands, suppressing what seemed
incompatible with his own views, and even sometimes,
it is difficult to believe inadvertently, altering the text in
a manner favourable to them.”
“ Carlyle and his wife,” says justly a Lond. Book-
man reviewer, “ resembled each other much too
closely. They were both neurotic, irritable, fussy, and
to the' last degree cruel and uncharitable in their judg-
ments. Both had an extraordinary gift of expression.
Both were essentially Scottish peasants. They were
childless, and for many years of their life they were poor.
The chief sting of their poverty was that it indicated the
world’s coldness and lack of appreciation. However
much they affected to despise these, they were in reality
very sensitive. The wonder is not that they quarrelled,
but that they did not separate for ever. As it was, their
married life, all things considered, was an amazing
success. There can be no doubt that they re-
tained through all a strong affection for one
another. Of the two, Carlyle was by far the
nobler, and it is only fair to Fronde to say that he
fully recognised this. Mrs. Carlyle was altogether on
a lower plane intellectually, morally, and spiritually.
There is even reason to think that Carlyle’s hateful
habit of disparaging everyone was derived from his wife.
Whether or no it is undeniable, and must permanently
affect the general estimate of his character. As for her,
no one was ever safe from her tongue.”
Bayard Taylor, the distinguished American who
englished Faust , sent a copy of his version to Car-
lyle,' and presently called at Chelsea \ th a friend.
They found Carlyle in a cab, and Froude was just getting
in. There were recognitions all around. All who knew
Carlyle’s ways were aware that a cab ride with him was a
great functidn, and must not be interfered with, but a
little talk before the ride, with Froude at the cab door
and Carlyle inside, promised a delightful moment.
Taylor said to Carlyle he hoped the copy sent of his
translation bf Faust had been duly delivered. “Ay,”
was the answer ; “yon’s the eighteenth presentation
copy of translations of Faust they’ve sent me.” Then
the cab drove off.
BOOKFELLOW’S MIXTURE
A paragraph of penance. Lionel Straehey’a
complaint of Mrs. Ward’s new novel was headed “Lady
Bose’s Dullness;” and Lady Rose's Daughter is not
merely not dull : she is absorbing. The tedious
moralities, the pulpit discourses, the conscientious
platitudes of the Mrs. Ward we knew cast hardly a
shadow over her. The author of Lady Rose’s Daughter
is a new author entirely.
But. . . she is not Mrs. Humphry Ward. Three-fourths
of the plot and five-sixths of the characters of Lady
Rose’s Daughter are conveyed straight from the letters
of Julie de Lespinasse, an 18th century Parisian with
wit and a history. Julie Le Breton, Mrs. Ward’s heroine,
is Julie de Lespinasse writ large in English ; and the lady
of fiction is surrounded by precisely the same people
that played havoc with the life of the lady of fact. Mrs.
Ward has furbished old phrases and emotions, transferred
characters to a London society where they do not belong,
and added a typical Ward ending. That is how the book
made; and, for_ convenient comparison, publisher
Heinetnann has supplied a new edition of the Lespinasse
Letters, so that paternity can be traced point by point.
The New York Tribune reviewed the facts, ranged the
Ijarailels, and declared that Mrs. Ward should have
icknovrjedged the paternity, and accounted for her
Hidden doffing of the dull manner. Mrs. Ward cabled at
ength that her sources were well-known, and that
she had made the clue cle>r by calling her heroine Julie,
and bv quoting.a passage from Mdlle. Lespinasse.'" The
Tribune retorted that, precisely because the debt was so
glaring, it should have been specifically paid.
Endorsed. Mrs. Ward’s way is a legitimate way of
novei-building; but since she has transfused fact into
fiction, not merely founded fiction on fact, she owed her
original definite thanks.
Lady Rose's Daughter is'uncommonly interesting,’ and
is commended to readers. But the interest is due first of
all to Julie de and only secondly to Mrs.
Humphry Ward. The blots that Strachey complained of
are the marks of tiie joiner’s chisel.
The biographical account of Brunton Stephens
on this page is the first complete smmmary to be pub-
lished. The writer, Francis Henna, author of Songs of a
Season, now member of Q. Legislative Assembly, was
among the number of Stephens’s personal friends. In a
following article will be given some account of Stephens’s
character and’ temperament, literary, political, and
religious opinions, as they appeared to one of his
intimate Brisbane circle..
In quitting his post on the editorial staff of
Sydney Telegraph, John Farrell closes a period of some
fourteen years valuable service with one proprietary.
During the greater part of that time he has been em-
ployed principally as leader-writer, and in addition has
contributed most of the Saturday notices of books. His
place will be taken by John Sandes, “Oriel” of Melb.
Argus staff. Both 'Farrell and Sandes have won reputa-
tion as writers of verse. Farrell’s Houi He Died, and
Other Poems was published in 188 V ; Sandes’ Rhymes of
the Times in 1898.
John Bull is an admirable brute; it is as a
canting brute that he disgusts us. Remember the tales
of “ the treacherous Boers ” who used soft-nosed bullets,
in retaliation for the Eng. soft-nosed bullets which John
Bull was silent about, but which even Conan Doyle had
to admit. Now we have the hypocrite’s cloak off in St.
James’s Budget of May 1, discussing the annihilation of
Col. Plunkett’s column by African Somalis defending
their country against the same old invader- Thus the
unabashed Tory—
We are all for humanity, of course ; but we do not
quite see where the humanity comes in of permitting
riddled and probably dying fanatics to hack our own
soldiers to pieces with spears before they collapse. The
point of exit of a dum-dum bullet is an ugly spectacle,
no doubt; but so is the wound of a shovel-spear ; and,
after all, we owe our first duty to the lives of our own
soldiers. Furthermore, if the native troops get to dis-
trust the power of their weapons to stop the enemy
they are likely to become unreliable. It is highly in-
I convenient, no doubt, to be obliged to keep two
separate supplies of small-arm ammunition ; and the
Boer contingent may possibly snigger to see us “slosh-
ing ” the 'Somalis with the kind of bullet over which we
raised such a virtuous howl when they were used against
us. But no considerations of this kind can stand against
the paramount importance of providing the troops in
Somaliland with that kind of “ gun-medicine,” to use
the expressive Malay idiom, which will enable them to
do their work most quickly and effectively.
'..'rue; and just as true in the mouth of John Bulls
pponents. Then why the “virtuous howl”? Because
ant is the Briton’s birthright : and canting pays.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The Riot of the Roada.
How l tire of rangeless reaches I how I loathe
these shapeless wastes —
Their sandhills, scrubs and arid waterways !
And my ears are sick of silence, and my eyes are
sick of Death,
Of the dry bones and the dust-wrack and the haze.
Ay ! I shrink from stricken Nature in her garni-
ture of woe,
And languish ’neath the wrath that all corrodes,
Till my heart is in a fever, and my senses all
combine
In a craving for the riot of the roads !
Let those who crave for Nature go and seek her
bony front
By the perished creek and dried-up billabong ;
Let them fight the lonely battle in her wilderness
of pain—
I d rather chance the pity of the throng
That crowds the busy highways of the biggest
city known :
At least no sable crow there broods and bodes :
O ! tis truly martial music to the ear that loves it
well—
The rattling, roaring rumble of the roads !
Hearts of stone within the city; trust and
honesty betrayed ;
Our daring dreams dispelled and bright hopes
vanished.
Yes, I know, and knowing choose it, for I ken the
bush land too,
And its baleful ban that never may be banished.
For the savage, silent contest ’gainst The Un-
known chills the heart
While the struggle ’gainst the tangible is meet—
”lis a faint heart falters surely when its beatings
beat in time
To the rushing, rolling music of the street.
Thus peace comes only to me when the day’s dull
toil is o’er
And Night the blighted bushland holds in thrall;
I hen in sleep, my soul, unfettered, takes in ecstasy
its flight,
And speeds to where the distant cities call.
And I thread the teeming highways and view the
busy marts,
The crowded quays, the harbors with their fleets,
The while my heart is pulsing—full and happy—
keeping time
To the rolling, rumbling music of the streets.
N.s.w.
LOUIS M.
Disillusioned.
To see the Maori in his home he came across the
sea;
He read him up, he learned his myths, he learned
them carefully.
Now first he saw an old brown hag who sat and
smoked a pipe,
A blackened clay. “I’ll speak to her, this old
’un, fully ripe.”
He hummed and hawed—but, hang it all! a man
must have a try.
“E pou/” he said (“Old woman,” that) "Epou!’’
he caught her eye.
“ Term koe I” —she removed her pipe—“Tena
koe: very dhry ! ”
She lifted up a skinny hand and pinched a
withered throat
(An intimation, very like, that smoke doth thirst
promote),
“Tena koe” means “good-day,” he thought—
again he caught her eye ;
“Tena koe” still she said and grinned; “Tena
koe—very dhry ! ”
He met a young man (Maori too, though dressed
in pantaloons);
Thinks he, “This fellow is not parched by half a
hundred moons.”
(They reckon time by nights, not days, in Maui’s
Fish, you know.)
Then once again he hummed and hawed, and
“ Here’s another go.”
“ Tena koe/” “Oh ! good-day; good-day,” the
Maori youth replied.
“ He’s up to date,” thought he ; and then another
tack he tried :
“Do you remember Maui’s tricks and how at
last he died ?
How first he held the burning sun and made him
run more slow,
And then he hauled a mighty fish from ocean’s
ebb and flow ! ”
“No fear! the parson says it’s lies—all lies and
fairy-tales.
I b’lieve that Josher held the sun, and Joner
swallered whales! ”
He went. He met an aged man who bore a staff.
“ A chief,
I’ll bet my hat,” thought our raw youth; “I’ll
tackle him.” In brief,
He tackled him. Was he a toa?—a famous old-
day hacker ?
“I’m civilised /” the old man said; “have you
got any ’baccer ? ”
He had no ’baccer, and no breath to tell the old
man so.
He went, and with a heavy heart he murmured,
‘ ‘ ’Taint a go ;
There are no Maoris here, I think, in all the
bloomin’ show.”
The aged men, the youths, the squaws, past all
he hurried by :
“Tena koe, pakeha! ” they cried; continuing,
“ very dhry ! ”
Z.
English hangman Billington is a barber by
trade, and carries on business at Bolton ; he is
said to be a good tradesman, too, except for his
habit of stopping to muse reflectively upon a cus-
tomer’s neck in the middle of a shave.

SATURDAY, JUNE 20, 1903.
The Landlord and the Federation.
Practically the only objection urged against
the Judicature Bill in the House of Repre-
sentatives was on the score of economy.
Those Tories who, in the absence of a
Federal High Court, see an impunity for
such lawless offenders as Victorian Premier
Irvine ; those agents of the importers who,
in their bitter unpatriotism, object to a High
Court as removing the last chance of the
customs swindler escaping scot-free ; those
Conservative enemies of Federation who
still prattle about State rights and dodder
over the “ tyranny of the union ” —all com-
bined to put forward the plea of economy as a
reason why the Federation should be left in-
complete, without its most necessary member
—the Judicial Executive of its Acts of
Parliament. There were divers things
in their hearts but a single argument
on their tongues —that the financial position
of the Federation is at the present time
somewhat embarrassed, hampered by the
Braddox clause of the Constitution and by
the disinclination of the State Governments
to re-cast their taxation and expenditure
systems to suit the new condition of affairs ;
that Australia cannot afford a Supreme
Court of its own, and should be con-
tent with any sort of an undignified
and inefficient makeshift. Economy is
a truly admirable thing in its proper
sense, but it is not “ economy ” for
a miserly person to hoard up gold
whilst denying t£> himself the necessaries of
life, or for a municipal government to
let filth accumulate in its streets to save
the cost of scavenging. Nor is it economy
for a nation to allow its most important
power to be exercised in a slovenly, undig-
nified and faulty fashion for the sake of a
small saving. It is folly, and folly of such a
kind as must inevitably end in worse results
than would follow a course of reckless prodi-
gality.
* * * *
Whilst not admitting, then, the smallest
degree of validity to the plea that, for
economy’s sake, the Federation should dis-
pense with a High Court, or set up a Court
insufficient in numbers or badly paid, it
must bb recognised that there is a certain
claim ta attention in the statement that the
Australian- Government is within measur-
able distance of financial embarrassment. The
cost of Federal services has already exceeded
the early estimates, and seems likely in
the near future to very much exceed them.
This is not because of any prodigality in
Federal administration ; so far there has
been rather a tendency to parsimony ; the
salaries attached to many responsible
positions are too low, though they may be
too high for some of the persons filling
those positions. But there has been, since
the framing of the Australian Constitu-
tion, a marked growth in the Federal idea
and a consequent increase in the functions
of the Australian Government. This
growth is a matter for congratulation, and its
continuance will be the best possible augury
of national w r ell-being. The more the
governing power centres in the one Govern-
ment which is truly representative of the
Australian people, and which is able to
legislate for the whole continent, thus avoid-
ing injurious differentiations as between
State and State, the better for the Common-
wealth. Nevertheless the financial difficulty
is becoming a very real one. The Federa-
tion does not, as yet, use the full proportion
of customs taxation to which it is entitled by
the Constitution ; but it is approaching to
that limit. And the present tariff probably
marks the extreme limit of revenue-raising
capacity. An increase in its rates (which
increase should, and must, come in the near
future as a better protection to native
industries) would mean less revenue, because
it would curtail imports and customs revenue.
Further, even supposing that a little more
tariff revenue could be gathered in, the
Federation would be able to use a fourth of
it only —the other three-fourths going to the
State Governments. Under all the circum-
stances, a betterment of the customs revenue
as a means towards putting the Federation
into a sounder financial position is not worth
discussing.
* * ♦ *
Yet the Federation must have more reve-
nue or neglect solemn duties, and let slip
out of its hands golden opportunities. In
addition to the expense of the High Court,
it will shortly have to make provision for a
High Commissioner, an Interstate Commis-
sion and an Arbitration Court. Federal old
age pensions must also come ; the Common-
wealth can hardly tolerate a system by which
its citizen in one State is granted a
fair allowance in his old age, in another
State a mean and grudging stipend doled
out in the spirit of Bentism, and in
yet another is left to starve. Nor will the
National Parliament, as it awakens to a
sense of its responsibilities, be content to
stand by whilst the various States run a
wild and reckless race to the pawn shop :
it will find itself compelled to take over the
State debts and also to adopt the hitherto
unheard-of policy in Australia of paying
them off. And all these things, courts,
commissions, pensions, sinking funds, will
require money—more money than is at all
likely to be at the command of the Com-
monwealth Treasurer under present condi-
tions of taxation. The Australian Parlia-
ment may get out of the difficulty by
refusing to undertake the duties which
demand its attention. But that would be
a dishonorable, and, ultimately, a bitterly
prodigal way out. Remaining in Egypt
would, ou the whole, have been a cheaper,
easier method of life for the Israelites than
trekking on an arduous path to the Pro-
mised Land. But they did not adopt it:
they had too good an eye to Business.
* * * *
The only alternative —and the alternative
which must be soon faced—to a policy of
lazy inertia and dishonorable neglect of its
duty, is for Australia to search out other ways
and means of obtaining revenue. It is argued
in Tory circles that, because the Australian
Government has the customs revenue, it
should leave all the other sources to the
State Treasurers. To which there are three
obvious answers : That the Australian Go-
vernment was given power to impose any
sort of tax, presumably so that it might
exercise that power ; that the Australian
Government has not the customs revenue
but only one-fcurth of it; and that it is use-
less to leave to the State Treasurers the
right to impose taxes which, so far, most of
them have shown no disposition to impose.
The most needed tax in Australia for
financial and economic reasons, and the most
honest one, is a heavy impost on land values,
graded so as to fall more heavily on the big
landlord and most heavily on the big
absentee landlord. Such a tax commends
itself for its honesty in the first instance,
because the greatest part of the responsi-
bility for the financial embarrassment of
Australia rests with the land-owning class,
which has, at one and the same time,
retarded settlement (and consequently
population), and piled up a huge foreign
debt for the purpose of increasing the
value of its estates by railways, roads, and
other public works. It commends itself as
providing a ready means of collecting a fairly
large revenue, which the Federal Treasurer
could reserve entirely for Australian pur-
poses, free from the operation of theßr addon
clause. But most of all it commends itself
for its economic effect in forcing the land
into use and in breaking down the barriers
which land monopolists have erected around
great holdings, received in the first case as
gifts, brought to enormous value by the
efforts of the community, and held still in
jealous reserve in the expectation that
further unearned increment will accrue, or
else leased out under rack-renting conditions
to poor teriantrfaYmers,
♦ * *
So valuable would be the effects of a land-
tax, exempting the small, poor holding and
making its exactions higher and higher as
the huge land monopolists and rack-renting
abs ;ntee landlords are reached, that, even
were there no financial necessity for extra
revenue, the Federation should take steps
for its imposition. The work cannot be left
to the States. In every State there is a
Second Chamber with powers of veto rang-
ing from the strong to the absolute, and
devoted almost exclusively to the land-
owning classes. The N.S. W. Upper House
is, of all the Australian Second Chambers,
the most Liberal, the most enlightened, the
most representative of the electorates, yet
it still represents in the main the land-
lord element. The Victorian Second
Chamber is at the other end of the scale —a
House absolutely monopolised by the land-
lords, narrow, intolerant, stupid, actuated
by no other consideration than the guardian-
ship of wealth. Their Upper Houses make it
practically impossible for the Australian States
to undertake the radical legislation which the
evil of land monopoly demands. The land-
lords will never consent to legislation w r hich
makes landlords disgorge any appreciable
share of their plunder from the public purse,
or relax to any appreciable extent their
throttling grasp on the throat of the com-
munity. But the Australian Parliament has
no such obstacle. Its Second Chamber is
not a House of Landlords, but a
body elected on as full and demo-
cratic a franchise as the House of
Representatives. The Australian Parlia-
ment alone can effectively grapple with the
problem of landlordism, and is called to the
task not only by the necessity of revenue,
but the necessity of national progress.
♦ * * *
To the Australian citizen who believes in
his own country, who does not agree with
the cowardly, whining cry of the borrowing-
importing gang that Australia is only fit to
be a low-wage country dragging a few rough
products from a reluctant soil, there is no
more sorrowful symptom of national sloth
and carelessness than the growth in this
continent of a landlord system matching in
iniquity that of England, and even Scotland
and Ireland. With manufacturing industries
of its own and easily accessible land,
Australia can grow to be a great country ;
without them —both are vitally necessary
—it must steadily degenerate into a poor,
dishonest, sponging country, irredeemably
pledged to foreign money-lenders, and sunk
in a slough of stagnation. It is the mission
of the National Parliament to open up for
the Commonwealth both these paths to
national greatness, and the means in one
case is a tax on unimproved land values,
graded so as to reach almost the point of
confiscation in the case of huge estates
owned by absentees and locked up against
settlement. In truth, absolute confiscation
of such areas would not strike anyone
fully acquainted with the facts of Australian
landlordism as being at all inequitable,
for when the average big landowner of
the Commonwealth does not completely lock
up his lands against settlement, he allows
them to go into cultivation only on condi-
tions which constitute the cruellest “con-
fidence game *’ ever designed to rob the
poor and the unwary.
r * * * *
There is nothing more pitiful than the
sight of the Australian farmer in the grip of
the private landlord staggering with bent
back towards the goal of prosperity and
falling ever into the grave’s deep furrow on
the way. His life may be epitomised :
Man comes, and tills the field, and lies beneath.
Starting, perhaps, as a young and hopeful
man, fresh and vigorous, he takes up a little
piece of tillable land on lease ; the rent
which the landlord charges him is a mere
nothing. True, the land has to be cleared
and fenced and sown, and a dwelling and
outbuildings put up. But the rent is so low
that, with hard economy and labor pushed
to the ultimate point of endurance, he
and his wife can manage to get over the
first few years. Their time of comfort is
ahead, when there is no more clearing
and not so much of “improving” to
be done. Five years pass and the
prospect of prosperous times seems near
enough to be almost grasped, when the
landlord comes to renew the lease— on fresh
terms which leave the toiler in the same hard
position as before, with just a living left
after the rent is paid. The farmer, chained
to the soil into which he has put his best
years of labor, to which he has given its
main value, cannot leave it and must submit
to any exaction. He struggles on, with a
bare subsistence wage, until it is
time to give his last tribute to the
land, and leave a son or a neighbor
to carry on for the profit of the land
monopolist. The mirage of prosperity is
ever alluringly before the victim of land-
lordism. With the stimulus of a low rent
he at first works with a savage hope for the
future, and afterwards, as he finds the land-
lord’s exactions increasing, with a despairing
resolution not’ to be forced off the bit of
earth that he has baptis'd as his own with
the sweat olhis soul.
» » » *
It is a truly pitiful thing this spectacle, in
a] new country, of the agriculturist in the
grip of the landlord, nailed to the same
cross as his unfortunate brother in Ireland
and England. One may see the process
of landlordism in all its stages in Australia—
on the North Coast of N.S.W. where the
men are just taking up the land from the
monopolist and are full of courage and
hope ; on the South Coast of N.S.W.,
where the intermediary stage is being
reached of a tenant peasantry that blesses
the squire and all his relations because the
rents only rack moderately so far ; in the
Bathurst district of N.S.W., and many
Tasmanian and Victorian centres, where
the tenant is sinking to the position
of an absolute serf, or, giving up
the struggle in despair, is trudging
to the poor-house, leaving the land to
be tilled by Chinese. In this country,
barely a century old as yet, settled at a time
when the experience of older lands pointed
a stern finger of warning against landlordism,
there should not be a single land monopolist’
nor a single farmer paying tribute to any land-
lord other than the State. The duty of the
Australian—the National —Parliament is to
retrace the steps of the Commonwealth back
to that ideal. The legislative system inflicted
upon Australia by England allowed the
landlord class a tremendous political
power, and the growth of a system of
rapacious landlordism was the inevitable
consequence. The legislative system de-
signed by Australians for Australia does
away with a controlling House of Landlords,
and sets up a Parliament in which both
Houses represent the people, and the natural,
logical sequence of such a reform is the
destruction of the evils of landlordism. The
Australian Parliament is the only one which
can unlock all the lands of the Common-
wealth ; it is the only one which does not
need to secure the assent of the land-
lords themselves to the destruction of
landlordism. To the Australian Parliament
the people look for the Federal land tax.
As matters stand the State Parliaments—
mere subordinate twopenny legislatures of
little account—are by their land systems
driving away the Commonwealth’s subjects
and the Commonwealth’s taxpayers, and the
sons of the farmers are beginning now to
make for far-off lands in Canada and South
Africa, simply because, even in this empty
region, good soil is not available. If
the Australian Parliament won’t protect its
own people, to w'hom can they look for aid ?
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Customs Administration.
Customs Minister Kingston seems to have be-
come tired, the other day, of the Daily Yabbeb-
graph’s constant wails about the poor, honest
importer brought before an unfeeling police-court
for judgment, and asked that paper to come
and investigate for itself his customs administra-
tion. From a full investigation of the records,
this result was reached by the D.T. reporter:
Action taken by the Customs Department, New South
Wales, from July 1, 1902, to May 31, 1903, in connection
with misdescription of ad valorem goods and other errors
in entries, goods imported but not entered, concealed
goods, and prohibited goods :
Note. —Post entries, representing balance of duty pay-
able, have been allowed in cases where the error has first
been drawn attention to by the importers, where the
error has been in the rate or classification of duty only, or
where, if there have been no previous cases, the error
does not exceed £1 in duty involved.
What on earth possessed the Telegraph to pub-
lish these figures it is difficult to say, for it is not
the “ tradition ” of that newspaper to burst up its
own myths in such an effective way. The table
shows that of 586 cases so far dealt with there
were only 89 prosecutions; of these only six
failed. This will at once strike anyone conversant
with criminal statistics as showing that the pro-
secutions were undertaken very cautiously and
never without due cause. Taking offenders in the
lump, the figures for N.S.W. in 1901 were 58,681
persons brought before magistrates on arrest or
summons and 8457 of these discharged. bus,
roughly, eight in 58 ordinary cases fail, whilst
only six in 89 customs cases have failed.
The inference is that Minister Kingston is
far more reluctant to prosecute the fraudulent
importer than the courts are, in a general
way, to prosecute the general offender, and
doesn’t move anything like so rashly, but
there are other notable features in the return.
In 164 cases of obviously honest error there was
not one prosecution. In only one section of the
return (that of importing prohibited goods) does
it appear that more than 25 per cent, of offenders
were prosecuted. The return proves with un-
questionable certainty that the yarn about the
“honest importer” being dragged to the police
court is a lie. Yet the Telegraph, though it
publishes the figures, does not put on sackclot t
and ashes and bewail its past misguided expressions
of sympathy for the dishonest importer, or its crue
slanders of Kingston. No, it still cries Out against
prosecutions, and suggests some arrangement by
which they can be avoided. But the communit
will support Kingston in not making anysucn
arrangement till the importer leaves off swindling,
and in dragging to the open court importers wno
bring to this country obscene prohibited articles,
or who seek to defraud the customs. If they a
honest men they should not fear investigation.
If they are dishonest, it is well that they thou
get the police court brand.

The Descendants of the Pig-dealer.
Died suddenly last week, the royal family of
Servia. It is now exactly 99 years since t.
Servians rose in insurrection against their grin •
ing tyrants, the Turks, under the leadership o
Black George, a grumpy and morose cattle-
owner, reputed also to be a bushranger in m
spare time. The Servian war never attracted t
same attention as the Greek war of Independence,
mainly because no Byron ever made poetry ab-
it ; nevertheless it was a magnificent struggle
grander than any the Greeks ever put up.
three years Black George had freed Servia.


ten years be had lost Servia again and was a
fugitive. Then arose a still greater man,
Mjlosh Obrenovitch, Pig-dealer, who swept the
Turkish power before him and fairlv started
Servia on its new career as a practically free,
though nominally tributary, kingdom. Later, the
two leaders reached out for the crown and there
wasn’t enough crown for two. Obrenovitch slew
Kara George in a gory squalid fight in a public-
house, and thereby began a feud which has lasted
until now. The mighty Pig-dealer reigned for
26 years, but he drove his subjects as he used to
drive his swine, and at last they rose against him
and ejected him. His son Milan reigned about
nine months, with the Karageorgevitch faction
taking pot shots at him periodically ; and then he
died. The Pig-dealer’s second son Michael lasted
three years, and then the Karageorgevitch
faction (representing Black George’s descen-
dants) drove him out, and put on the
throne Alexander, son of the old man whom
Milosh Obrenovitch slew in the public-house
But after seventeen years the mighty old
Pig-dealer came back to the throne and reigned
till his death in 1861. His career fills nearly 50
years of Servian history. His son Michael took
another turn on the throne ; he was a great and
enlightened man, but the Karageorgevitch
faction shot straighter than usual, and he died.
Next came another Milan, grand-nephew of the
tremendous Pig-dealer who raised Servia from the
dust. Milan was civilised—the worst kind. He
was a cigarette-fiend, a pursuer of ballet-girls
with plump legs, an unlucky and squalid gambler,
a man who made war recklessly and came out of
it badly, an issuer of big proclamations, a person
of shabby domestic squabbles, and a gentleman
whose I O IT was known with disrespect in all the
gambling hells of Europe. Still there was a streak
of the tremendous courage of the old Pig-dealer
about Milan Obrenovitch, and Servia progressed
even jn his time. He was shot at occasionally by
the Karageorgevitch push, but their aim seemed
to deteriorate with years. Milan abdicated in
his old age, and went to gamble with marked
cards and some spare trumps up his sleeve in
various parts of Europe, and to be unfavorably
known as a man who bilked his landlady when-
ever possible. His son, Alexander, who came
next, was promptly shot at by the Karageorge-
vitch faction—which again missed. The marks-
manship of the Karageorgevitch crowd was
execrable. Alexander was a fussy man of
good intentions. He reformed almost everything
in a hurry. He married a widow much older
than himself, and they had rows. His wife,
finding she had no hope of giving birth to another
Obrenovitch, tried to pass an adopted child off
as her own, and made a scandal. Alexander
was suspected of an intention to make his wife’s
obscure brother his heir, and the Karageorge-
vitch faction took fresh hope and oiled its gun.
He suspended the constitution, and quarrelled
with the army, and made more honorable and well-
meaning fuss, and the Karageorgevitch faction
felt glad and loaded its gun. Finally, last week
the army rose and slew the young King and the
middle-aged and ended the Obreno-
vitches. Peter Karageorgevitch, a remote
descendant of the man who was killed in a public-
house nearly 100 years ago, will apparently get
the throne, and the long feud is presumably
ended. King Alexander killed one man in the
scrimmage which ended in his death. The family
had degenerated. His ancestor, the mighty Pig-
dealer, would have wiped out twenty.

The Public Demand for Loot.
The Sydney Telegraph strikes about the bottom
limit of degradation with this notice :
TO COUNTRY READERS.
Owing to the enormous demands made by the Govern-
ment of N.S. W. for the Carriage of Newspapers by Train,
amounting to Eight Times as much as the freight charged
for corresponding merchandise such as milk, the Pro-
prietors of the Daily Telegraph cannot send extra copies
of the paper “ on sale or return ” to railway towns ex-
ceeding 150 miles from Sydney. Readers who may
require the paper are therefore requested to order it from
the Newsagent in Advance, so as to avoid disappointment.
The Proprietors of the Daily Telegraph
have in the past cheerfully borne the losses resulting
from unsold copies of the paper, and would be glad to do
so still, but they cannot in addition undertake to pay
freight charges which, in some cases, amount to more than
a penny per copy.
Considering that the Federal Postal Department
will carry the Telegraph to any part of Australia
and Tasmania for this statement about being
compelled to pay “ more than a penny per copy ”
is specially gorgeous. The Telegraph has now got
down to about the level of the ruffian who tries to
extort money by threats. It makes no disguise of
the fact that the See Government can expect no
quarter from it while the See Government refuses
to subsidise it at the cost of the taxpayer by carry-
ing its wares free. All it is asked to do is to pay
the freight that has long been fixed by law, and
which is only one-fourth of the usual parcel rates,
and that very moderate and strictly legal demand
has roused all the falsehood and slander that is in
the sordid paper’s constitution. Openly and
unashamed it lets it be known that it wants to be
paid, and that it is reviling the See Government
because it isn’t paid—at the country’s expense.
As a rule the man who wants the public money
for his own pocket has the decency to ask for it
privately, but the Telegraph howls at the street
corner and on the house-top.
Wanted, a Dignified High Court.
The Bulletin does not believe in spoiling a ship
for the sake of a ha’porth of tar, or in the economy
of giving a working horse three measures of corn
when its constitution demands five ; and, accord-
ingly, plumps for a Federal High Court of five
Judges. Such a court is necessary for the pre-
servation of the Federal Constitution and the
enforcement of the Federal laws. Since the
Court has to act as one of original as well as of
appellate jurisdiction it does not seem possible
that its work should be carried on efficiently with
less than five Judges. A good deal of circuit
business will have to be done and, probably also,
industrial arbitration will claim at least a part of
the services of one of the members of the court.
Allowing one Judge for circuit work and one for
industrial arbitration, and taking into considera-
tion the fact that, were the Court composed of
only three members, the Full Court, sitting in
appeal on a decision of the Federal Court, could
only consist of two members and might find it
impossible to come to a decision owing to a differ-
ence of opinion between those two, the necessity
for five Judges is at once apparent. It may be
well to briefly outline the functions of the Federal
High Court, to show the mistake of arguing that
it will have probably little work to do. The Court
may supplant the Privy Council in all appeals
from a State Court; it must take the place of the
Privy Council in all appeals on constitutional
questions ; it must act as a court of appeal from
the decisions of any one of its members, and as a
court of appeal on questions of law from the Inter-
State Commission ; it has original jurisdiction
(i.e., it is a court for the first decision) in
matters arising under any treaty, in any
oreign matter, in any case in which the
Commonwealth Government is a party, in all
cases between States or between residents of
dmerent Sta.tes, and in suits of mandamus or pro-
hibition against Commonwealth officers. That is
a wide range of causes ; and the Commonwealth
provides a wide range of territory. A court
which has that amount of business open to it, and
winch may have to send a member to sit at Perth,
xlobart, Broken Hill, or Port Darwin, needs five
members. The objection taken to that number
• S T e . made on the ground of economy, but
in the bitter grudging spirit of the anti-Australian
party, which does not wish to see an effective
Australian court established, but would prefer to
exalt the British Privy Council, and to leave the
r ederal Government hampered by lack of judicial
authority.
“ Naval and Military Experts."
Victorian Gov. Clarke’s lecture on the Imperial
naval subsidy, t’other night, caused Crouch,
M.H.R., to ask Premier Barton if he would
intimate to Chamberlain that the meddling of
State Governors with Federal politics was not
desirable. And Barton declined, adding the
strangely futile explanation that several of the
State Governors, who were naval or military
experts, favored the Imperial naval tribute pro-
posals. No doubt they do, but it is a pity Barton
didn’t explain what possible bearing that has on
the case. A State Governor has a right to favor
anything he pleases, including the abolition of the
Barton Ministry, the abolition of Responsible
Government and a return to Crown Colony rule,
the re-introduction of the convict system, the
gagging of the press, Freetrade, and the Victorian
railway break-downs. But he has no right whatever
to meddle with theself-governing powers of the Aus-
tralian people by advocating any one or other of
these things, and any Governor who begins to
openly favor naval subsidies or other such matters
thereby perpetrates an insolent outrage on all the
traditions of his office, and a grossly unwarranted
interference with things that don’t concern him.
If Barton gives many more explanations like the
above he will begin to get himself disliked; he
won’t begin to get himself despised, for he has
arrived at that stage already. Of course a British
naval expert would be in favor of Australia con-
tributing towards the support of theßritishfleet, of
British officers holding all the dignified and profit-
able billets on the ships that Australia pays for,
and of the people in England running the show—
and even if he wasn’t in favor of it he would have
to say he was if he spoke at all. A Russian naval
expert would approve just as cordially of the
Australian squadron being controlled from St.
Petersburg—and the British fleet also, for that
matter. None of these facts would prove any-
thing whatever. Also, the matter in question has
a political as well as a naval side ; and the fact
that a man has walked fiercely up and down the
quarter-deck of a British ship for many years,
with a brass telescope under his arm, isn’t evi-
dence that he knows anything at all about the
political aspect of the case. It doesn’t go to show,
either, that he knows anything about naval ad-
ministration ; that he knows anything about the
necessities of Australian defence ; it doesn’t even
show that he knows much about war. It only
shows that he is an interested party with a tele-
scope under his arm. That applies to the naval
Governor who interferes with Australian naval
questions ; but if a purely military Governor like
Clarke attempts, however covertly, to advocate
the naval subsidy folly, then he doesn’t even rank
as the most obscure kind of expert. It is difficult
to say what he ranks as—unless it be some kind
of horse-marine.
The Minimum Wage.
The Victorian Factories and Shops Act expires
in October, and, it is announced, “ unionist
leaders fully appreciate the hopelessness of
securing the re-enactment of the measure. ” If
Premier Irvine passes anything at all it will be
“a very greatly modified Bill,” from which the
minimum wage provision will be absent. And if
this happens the event won’t be quite so regret-
table as it would have been a few years ago. In
the ante-Federation days Victoria maintained at
least moderate Protection for its industries ; but
in a moment of folly it sent the late Sargood,
and Fraser and Zeal and other political fools
and horrors to the Commonwealth Legislature to
whittle away that Protection, with the result that
the business of importing foreign goods is booming
again. Many of Victoria’s workmen are only
employed half-time or are not employed at all,
and the competition for bread is more
desperate than it has been for years.
Good wages necessarily add something to the cost
of production, and unless tlsere is sufficient Pro-
tection to make matters even between the well-
paid Australian and the miserably paid Hindu
and Italian, good wages simply mean the
destruction of the industry in which they are
paid. And that means a drop to no wages
at all. Let a minimum wage on the old
scale be introduced, or let any other fair
conditions of industry be re-enacted, and in many
lines successful competition with the foreigner,
under the whittled-down tariff for which certain
Victorian members are partly responsible, be-
comes impossible. In other words, competition
with the cheap foreigner under the low Federal
tariff is, in many lines, only possible by working
in much the same way as the cheap foreigner
himself works. Wherefore, if we insist upon a
minimum wage law for our own people under
present tariff conditions, we simply insist that in
many industries the cheap foreigner shall have
the work to do. And apparently, until wiser coun-
sels prevail, and the cry of the Pumpkin Smiths
and other advocates of the foreign sweater is some-
what allayed, things will remain this way. If the
foreign boss pays his workmen only 20s. a week
and we think our workmen should get 405., then,
as we can’t control the foreign boss, we can only
put them on even terms by seeing that the
foreigner’s goods are, in some way or other,
weighted with the extra 20s. before they reach
us. A customs law is, in itself, the first
half of a minimum wage law; and it is the
only sort of law which will make decent
wages and conditions in Australia possible.
Therefore it doesn’t matter so much just now as
it did a year or two. ago whether the Victorian
minimum-wage law is re-enacted or not. The
first thing that Victoria must do is to eject
every Freetrader and low-tariffist from its bit of
the Commonwealth Parliament and bend its
whole soul towards getting a higher tariff. When
it has got that it should lay its whole soul towards
getting a minimum wage law for all Australia.
In the interval there will be a period of suffering
and low wages, but the State’s industries will
be preserved, and wages will be raised again—
when the new tariff and the all-Australian Fac-
tories Act come into force. If the old minimum
wage law could be enacted while the low tariff
lasts, the result in many lines would almost cer-
tainly be a closing up of industries and no wages
whatever. It is a cruel dilemma, but the world is
full of cruel dilemmas, and nothing is gained by a
refusal to recognise the situation—the only thing
is to start at the beginning by shifting the callous
ruffians who are hi red in our Parliament to
damage Australian industries for the benefit of
foreign manufacturers and traders. At the same
time, two tilings must be recognised : (1) A re-
duced minimum wage, which would still be better
than the old sweating conditions, is possible, and,
if that is not granted, it is so much the more to
the discredit of the present Victorian Govern-
ment. (2) The Irvine-Bent hostility to the
minimum wage is not based on any of the con-
siderations above set forth, but on the grudging
meanness of two petty tyrants, whose sympathies
are with the worst kind of sweater all the time.
Irvine and Bent may be only doing what is, to
some extent, inevitable, but they were just as
anxious to do it when it wasn’t inevitable. Also,
there is a difference between doing a cruel act
from sorrowful necessity and doing it because one
feels that way.

Be Alleged Senator Beid.
The Bulletin has received the following letter
from Senator Higgs, of Queensland :
In re a portion of your last week’s leader : There are
several men in the Senate who would publicly take up
the Robert Reid case if they felt sure of their ground.
There is no question of unworthy fear in the matter. The
most intense disgust at Senator Reid’s entrance was
expressed by several senators privately ; and, during the
swearing-in ceremony, one senator spoke in his usual loud
whisper something about “outrage” and “fraud.”
Another, just. before Reid concluded the oath, said,
loud enough to be heard through the chamber,
"and, pay the customs taxes.” Still, no motion was
tabled for a variety of fair reasons. A statesman whom
you greatly-respect suggested that it would he unwise to
“ make a martyr of him.” What weighed most with me
was the remembrance of the Bradlauoh House of Com-
mons case. The high ground taken by those who, while
not agreeing with Bradlaugii’s views on religion, favored
his admission to the House of Commons, was that he was
the representative of his constituents. Incontestable
ground, to my mind.
We are a Senate composed of six representatives from
each State. Senator Reid presents himself as one of the
representatives of Victoria, and is not disqualified under
any one of the five sub-sections in clause 44 of the Con-
stitution. The sub-clause which more nearly approaches
his case than any other is sub-clause 2 :
“Is attainted of treason or has been convicted or is
under sentence or subject to be sentenced, for any
offence punishable under the laws of the Commonwealth
or of a State by imprisonment for one year or longer.”
But although the Robert Reid firm was convicted of
fraud, its members were not sentenced to imprisonment.
The firm was fined £5O and costs. Can we under
the Constitution expel Senator Reid? Have we any
right to censure the people of Victoria for electing
him their representative ? True, he was elected by the
Victorian State Parliament, but so far not a single public
meeting has been held in Victoria protesting against his
admission to the Senate. Even if public meetings unani-
mously denouncing him had been held throughout coun-
try and town, I do not think we could do otherwise than
allow him to sit as Victoria’s representative so long as he
is not disqualified by any clause of the Constitution or of
the electoral law of the Commonwealth-. No doubt the
reason why there has been no strong public Victorian
agitation in connection with the case is because the
general public are satisfied to wait until December next,
when they will have an opportunity of pronouncing their
verdict at the ballot box.
There is, in all this, a total and most amazing lack
of allusion to the question that is really at issue.
The Commonwealth law provides that no person,
while a member of a State Parliament, is capable
of being elected to the Commonwealth Legislature.
The law doesn’t make any distinction as to
whether he is elected by the people or elected by
the State Parliament. Premier Irvine, by way of
deliberate insult, nominated a disqualified person,
and he was illegally elected—by a majority over
Peacock and Barker. When a man is illegally
on Senator Higgs’s premises, does be lekve him
there so as to avoid “ making a martyr ”'oLhim ?
And does the fact that Robert Reid, belongs to a
firm that has been convicted of swindling its
country give him a right to break the electoral
law which a person with a perfectly clean record
doesn’t possess ? If the Senate is not going to
enforce its electoral laws it had tetter cease
making such laws.
Re that statement by Queensland Premier Philp—
There were some things at Atherton which I did not
like. A number of men who are crying out for a White
Australia have taken land from the Queensland Govern-
ment at 2s. fid. an acre, and they are sub-letting it to
Chinamen at £1 an acre. Now, these men were loudest
in their cries about White Australia, and no black men or
Chinamen in Australia. I think that that country ought
certainly to be reserved for the white race.—
Philp here, without a shadow of evidence,
assumed that the men who sub-let their land to
Chinamen were not his own nigger-loving push,
but the White Australia party. But since making
the above statement he has been reminded that in
1900 the Labor and White Australia party in
Queensland Parliament moved that the sub-letting
of land to Asiatics should be prohibited, and the
motion was defeated by the Philp party, Philp
himself crying out bitterly on behalf of the Chow
sub-lessee. Now he lays the blame of his own
action on his political opponents, which is just
like Philp.
♦
The oareful collection of the Federal rolls reveals
some remarkable electoral anomalies in South
Australia. In Port Adelaide, the most thorough-
going Labor and Radical district in the State,
each member represents 6997 electors ; the
Northern Territory, which sent Sudden Solomon
and other all-wool Tories to politics, and votes
Tory every time, has a member for every 376. In
Torrens, Labor-leader Price and Coneybeer re-
present each 6329 electors; Alexandra, where a
Labor man isn’t possible, has one member for
every 2436. Perhaps it’s only a coincidence,
but it is a curious fact all the same, that in the
districts which Labor men can win, or have a hope
of winning, it takes an average of 5750 people to
return a member, while in the districts 'which
Labor men can’t win it only takes 2200. South
Australia has one person one vote; but some per-
sons’ votes seem still to be worth twice as much
as the votes of other persons. If there was one
vote one value, the Labor element in S.A. Parlia-
ment would be more than twice as strong as it is.

“ Chasselas ” supplies this story, which is backed
up in every particular by newspaper evidence :
The Victorian railways, under the Muddle and Back-
bone triumvirate—the short, thick, ignoYant man who
yells, and the pale, impassive, glassy-eyed man with the
great white bone of a forehead, and the tall man with the
carefully composed epigram that smells of the midnight
oil in his pocket—are in worse confusion than ever. Even
the big dailies have to say things occasionally, and then
cases of gross sweating come to light. The fat stock of
the Kyabramites must be run on a charity basis by an
under-paid, under-manned railway service, whilst men
fall asleep at their posts, and the big smash is only post-
poned by wondrous luck. The station-master at Guild-
ford, during six days, was on duty 128£ hours, and con-
tinuously for a spell of 61 hours. He overslept himself at
last, was responsible for a serious break in the traffic, and
his case was “inquired into.” Stationmaster at Rosedale
put in 106 hours a week or 17J hours a day. Last week
on Ararat to Hamilton line stationmasters were con-
tinuously on duty for four days and nights. One of these
men complained to the Department and was accused of
insubordination, and the usual black mark was put
against his name. The aim of the unholy trinity of
muddlers seems to be to achieve the worst service on
earth, and barring one or two South American lines they
seem to have about got there.
♦
Vic. Lands Minister Taverner, who is making a
few mild efforts to settle people on the land, has
arrived, somewhat late, at the conclusion that all
efforts in this direction are futile unless the
Government has power to compulsorily resume
good land. The State long ago “bust up” its
inheritance by selling or giving away practically
all the good land it possessed, and the only areas
available nowadays are poor blocks in Various
inaccessible places. Taverner says his Govern-
ment is only too willing to buy and cut up estates
on which a genuine agriculturist can make a living,
but directly it suggests a purchase the private
owner runs up his demands to an absolutely
impossible figure. There are ways out, of course,
but the paralysed Irvine Government won’t
move. One way is a general land-tax, graduated
to bear more heavily on big estates ; power to
compulsorily resume big estates for closer settle-
ment ; and, say, half the proceeds of the lar.d-tax
to be set aside to form a fund out of which to pay
for the estates when resumed. If the Irvine
Government doesn’t like these ideas, then its
business is to suggest something else that will
equally meet the case. At present Victoria pre-
sents the ridiculous and insane spectacle of a
State, with only 14 people to the square mile,
from which the young generation of farmers are
flying because land is unobtainable. They musi
go and farm somewhere else, for Victoria can’;
offer them room. And Belgium manages to find
room for 589 people per square mile.

“Alba” to The Bulletin :
T’other day when a band of Russian Jcw-balters
“dealt it out” to a Hebrew community at Kitcherniei
or some such place, old Rabbi Davis, of Sydney, lifted
up a voice that bled the hearts of his listeners. But
the Jew, in his anguish, is singularly impotent; with
both hands full of gold he looks skywards for help.
When writer heard the Rabbi recount how little Rachel
and Rebecca and the tiny Absalom had been hacked and
quartered he left the synagogue in quest of his buccaneer
friends to formulate a plan for the annihilation of the
unspeakable Slav. It is in the blood of the Jew to lose
heart in the presence of calajnity and oppression. Yet a
combine of European and American Hebrews could stiffen
Russian trade. With a man like Waterloo Rothschild
at their head they could chase the last rouble out of
St. Petersburg, and compel the Don Cossack to pawn his
knout for a meal. When the Jew-owned papers started
to manufacture “ Boer atrocities” in the Transvaal they
gripped Bull by the hair and flung him at the Boer, but
in the matter of his own blood-drenched Rachels ai d
Rebeccas he stands out as a man with chalk knee-joints
and a cainel-like under lip. When cash is in question he
is strong enough to hurl two little Republics to the far-
end of Gehenna. Enough said. Still, it seems to writer
that the long arm of Retribution has stretched across tl e
Dutch children’s graves to rest upon the Absaloms ard
Receccas at Kitehernief. Meanwhile Moss Moss and Ji b
Benjamin are shaking tremulous fingers at the Czar
And the bear blinks and licks his paws
When Reid gets into Office.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
. onl y let me get back into office and an immediate
improvement will set in.”— George Reid's familiar yell,
heard at intervals everywhere.
When George gets back to office he will wave his
Dog on high—
It shall be his sign and sceptre, shall that mammal
old and dry.
And there’ll be an alteration in the manners and
the times—
Every bard will find it easy to fix up his awkward
rhymes!
Every kid will be provided with a diamond-
studded hoop
When George gets into office —when the sea turns
into soup !
Ihe ships of the Importer, anchored gross and
anchored score—
They shall spill their many cargoes on our
country’s blessed shore.
We shall cease to manufacture, we shall sit upon
the piers
While the craft of Bill M'Millan line the
wharves in triple tiers.
There will be no need for working, there will be
no cause for toil
When Reid gets back in office—when the snow-
ball starts to boil.
When he waves his rod enchanted —when he
twirls his magic dog
Shall the wheels of State run sweetly, Parlia-
mentary cam and cog.
There will be no jars, no trouble—so the Georgian
Gospel saith—
There will be an end of tooth-ache and finality of
death.
All the stars shall shine far brighter, roses blush
a warmer red
When Reid gets into office —and Job rises from
the dead.
There will be no droughts nor floods then, there’ll
be silken hats for all;
We shall eat in golden inangers, we shall tread
a marble stall.
There’ll be free rides on the railroad, in the cab
and on the tram,
And the poor shall daily banquet on champagne
and fruit and ham !
All the pubs, shall give free drinks then, kine
shall chew a special cud
When Reid gets back to office—when the Tank
Stream is in flood.
There’ll be loans to pay the baker, there’ll be
credit at the store,
And the butcher will cease saying that he’ll call
with meat no more !
Bathurst-burr shall cease to flourish, weeds shall
droop their dying heads ;
There’ll be food for man and woman and the other
quadrupeds.
Earth shall be a perfect Heaven—wondrous things
will come to pass
When Reid gets into office—when the wind turns
into grass !
Crops shall grow a cubit higher; girls will wax
far plumper then,
And six eggs be laid in concert by the ordinary
hen !
Boots will cease to make us suffer ; we shall feel no
grief nor pain ;
Every man shall own a motor and a fine gold
watch and chain.
And Australia shall be filled with joyful songs by
many a bard
When Reid gets back to office —and when Sheol
freezes hard.
