♦ ■
J. C. Williamson concludes his season of
musical comedy at Sydney Royal, this week,
with “The Belle of New York.” As remarked
here some weeks ago, all musical comedy is Girl
of some sort; but the New York sort of girl has
more tune in her than most, and she even
threatens occasionally to have a story. The cast
is much as it was 12 months or so ago. Fred.
Graham is still the astonishing Reformer from
Cohoes, and the crowded house last Saturday
night knew so well where his songs—or chants, or
whatever they are, “Be as like us” and “Per-
haps”—came in that it began to titter before-
hand. Arthur Crane is still a mild young man
for such a devil of a fellow as the Reformer’s son ;
Maurice Dudley, though he only sings two
syllables, adds much to the gaiety as the polite
lunatic; and the other things that, after 48
hours’ reflection, seem worth remembering are
Miss Digges’ pert and vivacious Fifi, Miss
Rose Musgrove’s reasonably satisfactory “ Belle,”
and the shake of Fred. Leslie’s foot. .The
“ Belle” owes part of its popularity to what it is,
and part to what it was. In the old long ago,
when Oscar Girard, now deceased, rose before the
public eye as Ichabod Bronson of Cohoes, he built
a joke round the play that will not soon die.
* * * *
Next Saturday Cuyler Hastings and
return to the Royal to play “Sherlock Holmes”
for a week, and when that coldly impossible man
ceases to leave his burning cigar on the mantel-
piece in the cellar there will be a one-week revival
of “ If l Were King. ’
* * * *
At Sydney Lyceum Wm. Anderson con-
tinues his career as a doubtful reformer ; every
night at something past 9 Robert Inman is on the
verge of d.t’s., and at something to 11—a sober and
a contrite man—he is marrying the girl he ought
to have married shortly after 8. Still, if he had
married her then, “ Man’s Enemy ” wouldn’t have
been possible, and Anderson would probably have
continued to play “The Face at the Window,”
the prize horror of recent years. “Man’s
Enemy,” without being as horrible as that, is still
sensational enough for any ordinary glutton, and
is, besides, less rankly impossible. “Night Birds
of London ” will be the next thrill ; and the
public remembers these birds as very dissipated
fowls indeed. The change takes place on Saturday
night.
# * * *
Rudinoff, who made his first Sydney ap-
pearance at the Tivoli on Saturday, convinced
the audience at once that he was an entertainer of
singular talent. As a lightning sketch artist, his
work is facile and singularly humorous (a pre-
sentment of “his friend, Mr. Cohen,” in which
the letters of the name are made to serve as the
outline of a typical Hebrew face, is an epigram
in crayon); his “smoke pictures” are novel and
entertaining; and his whistling representations of
birds in ulay and love are full of quaint
and skilful mimicry. Since Cinquevalli there
has not been such a clever and amusing
entertainer at the Tivoli. The programme has
been graded up to keep it somewhat in coun-
tenance with Rudinotf’s merits ; Halleu and
Fuller, from America, contribute a comedy sketch
after the manner of the Drews —not as well done,
but still brightly and acceptably; and a “long-
felt want ” is supplied by a picturesque ballet.
Miss Fanny Powers and Edward Ford contribute
other good “ turns.”
* * # *
Some of the star-dust that tenor Lloyd
left behind him has been kneaded into a com-
bination and called by its potter, Mr. J. C.
Williamson, the English Concert Co. It will give
one concert in Sydney, at the Town Hall, next
Saturday. Besides Miss Foxcroft (contralto),
Peterkin (basso) and Miss Mukle (’cellist), the co.
has Miss Nairne (soprano), Arthur Mason
(organist) and Herr Scherek (pianist).
* * *
Miss Maggie Moore’s season at Sydney
Palace proves, by its success in such a familiar
piece as “Struck Oil,” that lady’s strong popu-
larity. On Saturday “A Widow from Japan,”
which is not so familiar as the “Oil” drama, will be
staged. This country has suffered a good deal by
reason of Johnny Sheridan’s widows—the Irish
ones who arrive in a motor car from Chicago—and
it hopes that the one from Japan will be of a
different type.
* * * *
“ Madame Sans Gene,”as presented at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.), last Saturday, reveals no
unsuspected greatness in the Daniel Frawley co.
The “Secret Service ’ performance, though not
at all brilliant, was a fairly interesting treatment
of a Civil War drama in which the stage manage-
ment and the Amurkan accent are half the battle.
“ Madame Sans Gene,” a spectacular comedy of
the “ Sweet Nell ” class, is a more difficult under-
taking. The Broughs formerly staged it in their
best style with several clever artists around them,
and if Melbourne co nplained—as it did complain
—that the bright work of those old favorites
was faulty, what shall be said of the present
inferior revival of the same play by a visi-
tor who trumpets its praises in advance?
This is the original English version of . the
French “Sans Gene,” disfigured by cheap, broad-
comedv elaborations in the name part. The same
lively young laundress becomes a middle-aged
duchess, and sauces Napoleon’s sisters, and strikes
the Imperial tyrant speechless by reminding him
of his unpaid washing bill. But Miss Van Buren
seems a mere exuberant amateur in the character,
as compared with what Mrs. Brough was, or Mrs.
Bland Holt might be. Her acting wants force,
finish, sincerity. Her talk grows tiresome, for
she talks out of tune with her subject, just as
Miss Edith Crane did, bringing out all the defects
of American accentuation with none of its effective
points. She doesn’t even look Sans Gene, for
the duchess in 1811 is younger than the laundress
of 19 years before. And her labored antics to
raise a gallery laugh are sometimes contemptible
and always unconvincing. The play gets a lift in
the third act when Frawley’s figure of Napoleon
comes to light. Frawley doesn’t fulfil the expec-
tation of a startling Napoleonic make-up, but he
easily resembles the portrait of the Emperor in
many respects where Brough didn’t, and he gives
a plain, sturdy performance. Harrington Rey-
nolds (of the American contingent) makes his
local debut as a plump, quiet Lefebre, Duke of
Dantzig, the model of a flunkey in his court suit.
Other men—including Plimmer, who plays a
small part and would be better placed as De
Neipperg—are either passable or otherwise, and
all the company, bar Nap., are temporarily extin-
guished when Miss Susie Vaughan takes the
floor as Queen of Naples. A typical American
play, “Arizona” (by the author of “In Miz-
zoura”), follows “Sans Gene.” The season has
three more weeks to run, and “ Arizona ” can’t
happen too immediately.
** * #
Except in the d.c., where the seats of the
scornful are only moderately well filled, “As You
Like It ” seems to be a pretty solid attraction at
the Princess’s. Another fortnight is all that’s
left for the season of W. Shakspeare’s peaceful
comedy, therefore Melbourne will do well to hurry
up and admire the beauties of stage nature before
they go on tour and lose their early bloom. In
the truthful words of the text, this thing is done
“ on a scale of magnificence never before attempted
in Australia.” Public patronage is now being
weighed in the scale, and, if it is found wanting,
Musgrove will, presumably, reduce his magni-
ficence down to the comprehension of a one-eyed
community in future ventures. Meanwhile, the
mighty fine show continues to put up a Mel-
bourne record for dramatic landscape pictures
with figures in them. The company is enjoying
good healthas usual, and evilly disposed characters
get converted every evening. Venerable Adam,
who blows like a winter wind on the subject of
his green old age, because he never drank any hot
rebellious whisky in his youth—Adam, the lusty
Rechabite, invariably threatens to die of fatigue
when Orlando puts him to the test, but he never
fails to postpone his decease when he reaches the
banished Duke’s luncheon, and hears Kenning-
ham sing. The song puts new life into
Adam seemingly. He is fainting for food
when they dump him down beside the
frugal banquet, but he forgets to eat any-
thing of late. For the first night or two
he used to pick a bit, and then he dropped the
practice and found refreshment in listening to
Kenningham. Athol Forde is an impressive
Adam, armed with the tall clothes-prop of a
blameless life. Good men are addicted to leaning
on clothes-props for support. The early Christians
in the “Sign of the Cross” were the founders of
the custom. These few remarks must not conclude
without a sweet allusion to the improvement in
Miss Rosalind Kerin’s performance. When the
importance of being earnest has impressed itself a
little more deeply upon her fair young soul, Miss
Kerin will be the Rosalind of a Shakspeare
Society’s dream.
** * #
Bland Holt’s “ Breaking of the Drought ”
has come to stay awhile at Melbourne Royal.
Nothing really booms in Melbourne since the
Black Bent Saturday when the engine-drivers
suspended traffic, but the “Drought” drama is
getting all the prosperity that offers. The city in
general warms towards the bush fire, and is
impressed by the symbolical limelight of blue
ruin which falls upon father and mother Styan
and daughter Frances after the dear old home is
burnt out. Somehow, Walter Baker contrives to
be of little importance in this strange, eventful his-
tory. He overhears a conversation in the first
act, but things go on the same as though he had
heard nothing at all, and. although he finds the
money to buy a coffee-stall for his mother-in-law
elect, Baker is but a second-rate hero at the best.
His morals are doubtful. Just when the girl he
loves is drinking champagne and coquetting with
a nigger at the haunt of sin, Baker strolls in
casually and is shocked to find her there, so the
audience is left to suppose that if she had not
been there he would have found another girl, not
so respectable as the one he wasn’t looking
for. Styan, as the elderly burnt-out squatter,
is a conspicuous success; so is Miss Ross,
as the girl who feigns wickedness. Also
Brown is a good sketch of a nigger from the
’alls, and Albert Norman repeats his own familiar
kind of villain, instead of introducing a new
pattern. Why not a bronzed, heavy, hairy-faced
villain for a change? Bland Holt, in the absence
of Mrs. H., plays a lone and lively hand as a
rouseabout and reckless patron of the coffee-stall.
Brunton’s artistic performance of the Sydney
scenery gets great applause, especially the bush
tableaux, Mosman’s Bay, and Coogee, where Miss
Kellermann leads a procession of divers into
sundry feet of water.
** * *
“ The Lady Slavey ” jingles along satis-
factorily at Melbourne Bijou. Some of the music
is distinctly better than the rest of it, which
descends to the comic-song class of composition;
also, the Cinderellian story is more humorous
than the dialogue. Good ideas, borrowed and
original, are crudely worked out in “ The Slavey.”
The chorus of creditors and the two bailiffs, mas-
querading as guests at the major’s carnival,
amounts to a good idea, and the capture of the
millionaire by the decorative daughter who
professes to do the washing up is felt to be a
matter of general interest to the company. That’s
where “ The Slavey ” has the advantage of higher-
toned musical comedies whose choruses are but
intermittent intruders on the gaudy scene. The
diamond in Sheridan’s shirt-front is now shining
as it never shone on the first night, since when,
the show elsewhere has got into better working
order.
** # *
The Boissetts, late of Sydney, started
their Wild West panto, at Melb. Opera House
last Saturday, and the intelligent dumb activity
of those eleven new arrivals seemed to hit the
popular taste in many places at once. Subse-
quently the Boissetts gymnasted with success, the
funny man of the party proving a more subtle
comedian than the average comic athlete. Another
strong addition to the Rickards bill was W. C.
Fields, for the first time in Australia. Fields is a
clever juggler who treats himself as a joke, and
doesn’t treat the joke too seriously. Whitburn
and other people incidental to the first part
contribute songs and gambols to the fund of
entertainment, which is just now in a verv
healthy state.

Miss Gertrude Alger, the young violinist
from Bendigo, makes her first appearance at
Melbourne Town Hall next Saturday (20th), and
on the Saturday after Marshall Hall will give
another orchestral concert at that place of musical

* * * *
At Adelaide Royal, the “ Thieves ” who
call themselves thirty, but are in the flesh twelve,
gave way to the picturesque “Fortune Teller”
for last two nights of the Musgrove season.
Pringle and Mme. Slapoffski had put in a Broken
Hill concert engagement while out of the bill.
It is a somewhat risky experiment to run a six-
weeks season in Adelaide with only three plays.
At present Frank Thornton is “Facing the
Music ” with a quaint play which lasts about two
hours in fiction as well as in fact, and between the
acts of which nothing is “supposed to elapse’’ but
the 10 minutes actually required to elapse for the
purpose of getting a drink. The plot turns on
the bewilderment of one John Smith at finding
there actually is in the world another man of the
same curious name. Meanwhile, the short Tivoli
season of “Mistakes Will Happen” is already
coming to an end. The biggest mistake
which has happened is that Willoughby and
Geach took a house too small for the
crowds that want to see the real four-wheeler,
and the real straw, and the real dirty water
squirted into a gentleman’s eye. The Caledonian
Society (assisted by Maggie M‘Cann from Mel-
bourne) gave a rousing Scotch Night in the Town
Hall on the holiday ; and a somewhat up-country
family of Cunard Entertainers now occupies the
same building.
# * * *
At Hobart Royal, in the last week in May,
the smiling face of Dick Stewart, ex-junior, was
seen again in the manager’s room, his cares this
time being J. C. Williamson’s Hastings-Chevalier
co., with “Secret Service,” “If I were King,”
“The Christian,” and “Sherlock Holmes.” If
J.C. wanted to keep his audiences select at
Hobart the prices were all right, for the playgoer
on the banks of the silver Derwent is jealous of
his silver coin, and looks at two half-crowns a lot
of times before he parts with them to old Tom
Jones at the hole in the wall. Melbourne and
Sydney prices are insulting things to the typical
Vandemonian. Nevertheless, the houses during
the nine days’ wonder of the Williamson
season were good, and meant money. “Secret
Service ” opened the season, and there was a good
influx of playgoers, who had seen Cuyler Hastings
as Sherlock Holmes and wanted to see him in a
less superhuman part. “ Secret Service ” ran for
three nights, as did “If I were King.” J. B.
Atholwood had a good show as the foolish, philo-
sophical, vindictive, humorous old King ; but why
on earth did he wear a red rosary round his neck,
like an order-collar? For two nights the
“Christian” stormed, and then the calm of the
petrified boss detective prevailed for a single
evening.
* * * *
Writes a Melbourne critic :
Joe Blascheck, the little lame joker remembered in
Melbourne as a reciter of “ The Cockney's Seven Ages of
Man,” recent!}' wrote a letter to London Referee pro-
testing that the dearth of Australian actresses for the
British market is not due to their alleged “ objectionable
and horrible colonial accent,” but to the fact that they
get no encouragement from the local managers. In
proof of which Blascheck instances four out of five
successful Australian girls as having owed their oppor-
tunities to “the advantage of being the daughters of
their own managers.” Nellie Stewart, Essie Jenyns,
Lily Dampier, and Carrie Moore are the four
instances, Carrie Moore, according to Blascheck, bein«-
che “adopted daughter of Mr. J. C. Williamson.”
J.C.W., the managerial critic of the twang, has probably
hastened to disclaim the adopted paternity of Carrie
Moore’s peculiar accent. For the rest of Blascheck’s
letter, it is mostly written in ignorance. He says, “A
few Australian girls, despite the discouragement they
received in their own land, have obtained in England the
chance of proving their ability that was denied them at
home.” Yet the fact remains that no successful Aus-
tralian actress abroad, not even excepting Ethel Haydon
(now a back number), can say she didn’t get a good
show in her own country. Pattie Browne got
better parts with Brough and Boucicault than
she has achieved in England. Lilian Wheeler plaved
Desdemona to Rignold’s Othello before she had been two
years on the Australian stage. Alice Crawford was
recommended by J. C. Williamson to M‘Kee Rankin and
Wilson Barrett, who brought her to the fore ere
she went away with Barrett’s co. And Hilda
Spong, who is practically an exported Australian actress,
was the leading lady in Bland Holt’s co. when she was
still a mere girl. None of the three last-named artists
had the least suspicion of that “objectionable colonial
accent ’’ which disqualified so many other bright girls for
similar rapid improvement. They got on because their
elocution was worthy of their talent.
A Letter from Lyell.
George Mack writes from the copper region
North Mount Lyell Railway and Mining Co., owned
mostly by Australia’s creditors, has amalgamated with
Mount Lyell M. and R. Co., whereat is much rejoicing
among the Fatmen of the mining world. But among the
lean men is heard no sound of jubilation, the why of it
being thuswise. When the North Lyell built its much-
bungled railway, and erected its alleged smelters, and
opened flux quarries at Darwin, two townships sprang
up, one at Crotty and one at Darwin. Deluded by the
hope of permanent work, men brought their families
there, and built houses on blocks of land bought at boom
prices from the Tasmanian Misgovernment. All the
money they had, and all that the gross mis-
management of the North allowed them to
earn, they spent in improving their properties.
Stores were opened, hotels of brick and of wood
erected, and the people regarded themselves as
definitely settled. The reports that Crotty would be
abandoned were persistently denied, even after the
amalgamation was certain. Up to the very last Crott.v-
ites and Darwinites firmly believed that the smelters
would be kept going. Now there is weeping and lamen-
tation, for Crotty is not, and Darwin is not. All hands
have been sacked, and men who paid enormous sums for
land blocks on which to build what they fondly imagined
were to be their homes, now find land and homes value-
less. These people have no money. Few of the men
have put in more than half time for months past, and
as the stores are closed, and they must quit or starve,
their straits are sad indeed. It is almost impossible to
get work on the Coast, and the unfortunate have no
means of leaving it. The Tasmanian Misgovernment has
promised aid : but past experience of its methods has
taught the people that to wait for it means starvation.
Hundreds of men are thus thrown on the already over-
stocked labor market, and what the rest of the winter
will be like one shudders to think ; but of course, the
Fat Man will get handsome divs. from the amalgamation,
so what matter a few hundred workmen and their families
left workless and starving !
The bungling which was begun by the Tasmanian Mis-
government surveying and selling blocks of land for a
town site near Mt. Lyell smelters, and then surveying
another site, and selling more blocks, without, of course,
refunding money paid in first case, when the North Lyell
changed what served it as a mind and decided to erect
its smelters elsewhere, has had a fitting finish. Twenty
thousand pounds worth of property, belonging mostly to
workmen, has been rendered absolutely valueless; hun-
dreds of men have been thrown out of work at a few
days’ notice, and scores of men, women, and children are
actually starving through the shutting down of Crotty
reduction works. Little else could have been
expected. North Lyell management never exer-
cised the least amount of foresight over anything,
and could hardly be expected to do so now. When the
big smoke stack" was put up at Crotty, no one seems to
have even remembered that a lightning conductor it
usually required. It was remembered, though, when a
wandering thunderbolt came to earth eta the chimney,
and strewed the neighbjrhood with bricks. The new
stack is furnished with a conductor, and now stack and
works are to be abandoned. Perhaps the ineptitude of
the bunglers in charge was never so fully illustrated
as when two trucks of ore, valued at £l5OO,
were gaily kicked off on to the wharf at Kelly’s
Basin, and as no one had remembered to see
if the brakes would work, or thought of putting a dead-
end on the wharf in case they didn’t, the trucks shot
over into deep water. Then a heaven-sent genius of a
boss—North Lyell was full of them—got a lighter, and
sought to recover the trucks by dumping a huge anchor
into them in hopes that it would catch on something.
When the truck operated on was reduced to splinters,
divers were sent down, and the ore and truck wreckage
hauled to the top again. It was time Yankee Robert
Sticht got a hand in the game.
Mr. Critchett Walker, for many years
Principal Under-Secretary of N.S.W. and con-
fidential adviser of a long: line of Premiers—
Parkes, Dibbs, Reid, Lyne and See—died last
week of pneumonia. It was at the funeral of the
late J. T. Toohey that he caught the fatal chill;
old men should never attend funerals, young men
should attend theib as seldom as possible. From
the age of 16 to 62 Critchett Walker was a publio
officer, and, having much solid ability, he grew in
that time to be the ideal civil servant —methodical,
secretive, tactful, wedded to routine. There was
no rash experimentalism about Critchett Walker.
At various times he was offered a seat on the
Railway Board of Commissioners and the Public
Service Board, but he knew his groove and stuck
to it.
Melbourne Board of Health lately acquired
the pleasing information that that sink of cor-
ruption, Melb. General Cemetery, is so full that
even the footpaths are being broken up to make
room for more bodies. Meanwhile, the Springvale
Necropolis is yawning for its dead, but they can’t
get there because Bent refuses to open the Ceme-
tery line till he thinks it will pay. And as Bent
is unable to think, this deadlock may last in-
definitely.
N.S.W. Parliament opened on Tuesday
with mostly a moderate and colorless programme.
The question of reduction of members is to be put
to a referendum. Gov. Rawson read, without a
smile, a sentence in the speech which expressed his
advisers’ regret that they couldn’t feed the Free-
trade newspapers on subsidies any longer. No new
taxation is to be proposed. The Labor Party is
promised Bills (which are rather declaratory of
the law than enactive) removing the shadow of
the Taff Vale decision from trades unions, and
amending the Coal Mines’ Regulation Act so as to
make payment by weight compulsory in all cases.
James Ashton and other of the Carruthers
crowd are touring the women’s political leagues
now, telling them that “women’s franchise was
not a popular proposal in the See Cabinet.”
Anyhow, the See Government carried it, and the
Reid-Carruthers crowd only talked about it. The
Reid-Carruthers crowd and their fathers and fore-
fathers haven’t, in fact, been responsible for a
single large measure of electoral reform, though
nearly all the time they have pretended to favor
electoral reform. It was the Dibbs-Barton
Government, which included See and Kidd, two
present Ministers, that gave one-man-one-vote ;it
was the same party, 10 years after, which gave
one-person-one-vote, and these two measures are
the two most powerful instruments of popular
government in existence.

The balance-sheet of the Great Southern Con-
sols G.M. Go. (Vic.) for the last half-year shows that the
underground electric haulage plant, which was supplied
and erected about seven months ago by the Austral Otis
Engineering Coy., South Melbourne, sole representatives
of Ganz and Co. Ltd., Budapest, Hungary, has effected a
saving of 60 per cent, in manual labor required to operate
the mine. This excellent result, and the great satisfac-
tion the plant has given, has influenced the directors of
the Berry Consols Extended Company in placing an order
for a similar plant with the same firm. The plant will
consist of a generating station, three underground loco-
motives, and a complete electric lighting installation.
This order furnishes further evidence of the high esteem
in which Ganz’s apparatus is held in Australia.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, of Melbourne, reports
that there has been a great improvement in the general
outlook of the engineering business since the new year
started, and that his pipe making works in Melbourne
and Perth are working full-handed. Amongst the orders
now being executed at Footscray works is one for 7 miles
10 chains of 18in. diameter light steel spiral-riveted pipes
for the Victorian water supply ; and at Perth works a
contract is in progress for over 6 miles of lOjin. diameter
spiral-riveted pipes, flanged joints, for the Greenbushes
Tin-Mining Syndicate. About two miles of these pipes
are to stand a test pressure of 8001 b. to the square inch.*

The copper market, with none too promising an
outlook previously, is now, according to London Statist,
threatened by a new producer in Central Africa, which,
it is alleged, promises to be as big a thing in the matter
of copper as the Rand is in the matter of gold. J. R.
Farrell, a well-known ’Murkan mining engineer, who has
inspected, says that native workings prove
tons of ore in one mine alone, ana that the average
recoverable value is 15 per cent, metallic copper. The
district is northwest of Rhodesia, and the nearest port is
Benguela, in Portuguese territory.

The Wild Cat Column.
Sydney Telegraph grew desperate as the
opening of N.S.VV. State Parliament approached. A
few days before the event the paper wildly announced
that the See Government, out of pure vindictiveness had
put up the railway carriage rates so that they amounted
in some cases, to a penny on each penny paper. The
morning of the day when Parliament opened it wildly
declared that under certain circumstances it was being
charged five-pence freight on each penny paper. Quite
apart from the gorgeous discrepancies in these
narratives, the whole trouble lies in three pro-
positions : (1) There has been no increase in the
rates fixed by law ; the See Government simply intimated
alike to the papers which supported it and those which
opposed it, that the old legal rate must be paid --for a
number of years the law has been evaded. But the
intention was that the papers should simply pay a lump
sum —equal to one-fourth the regular parcel rate. (2)
The Sydney morning papers, long accustomed to regard
themselves as above the law, refused to pay except
“ under protest.” (3.) The Treasury refused to let any
bumptious newspaper obey the law only “under pro-
test,” and as the papers wouldn’t pay any' other way they
have (until they learn that the law applies to them in the
same way as to other people) to make their arrangements
with the railway authorities and stamp each parcel
separately and go to a lot of extra trouble.
The Telegraph s latest statement, by the wav,
••“ads thus : “ Under the scale of charges adopted by the
Cabinet for the carriage of newspapers by rail, a single
copv of the Telegraph sent to Albury, Cobar, Tenter-
lell, or Corowa would cost sd. The Postal Department
would carry five copies to any part of the Common-
wealth for a penny. But the postal arrangements in
New South Wales are so defective that little or no
relief can be obtained through that department, as
there are practically no morning mails in this State.”
And to most, if not all, the places mentioned, the Tele-
graph does send its papers (a special afternoon edition to
catch the night mails) through the post, and has done so
for manv years What’s the good of saying that a single
paper, if sent by rail as a parcel to a certain place, would
cost 5d., when it is not so sent, and never was. As well
make a grievance of the fact that a single tin tack, if
packed up by itself and sent on a camel to Greece, would
probably cost £5O.

The shareholders’ total funds at the commencement of
the half-year were £182,176 (£152,040 capital, £30,136
reserves), nnd the profit is equal to £5 7s. 9d. per cent,
on this. This result compares with that of recent half-
years in this fashion :
Nov., 1900 ..£6 1 8 p.c. May, 1902 ..£4 18 9p.c.
May, 1901 .. 51211 „ Nov., 1902 .. 418 7,,
Nov., 1901 .. 514 2 „ May, 1903 .. 5 7 9 „
The reserves still consist of a reserve fund of £22,500 and
a large floating balance of £8553. Why portion of it is
not transferred to the permanent reserve fund con-
tinues to be one of the mysteries of the institution.
The coin, bullion, and State securities held amount
to £150,051, as against £174,639 six months ago- that is,
about 25i per cent, of liabilities to the public, against
about 30 per cent. In consequence of this bank’s com-
paratively small capital and its comparatively smaller
reserves these figures fluctuate very much ; at'this time
two years ago they were as low as 19 per cent. Alto-
gether, though the bank is making steady progress, it is
slow progress; the reserves on May 31, 1893, were
£39,118,50 that the losses incurred just after that date
have not yet been made good.

This co. continues to pay the largest dividend among the
trustee co’s. of Australasia, and it also continues to have
no formal reserve fund—its reserves are “ balance
carried forward.” Most trustee co’s. appear to make
very little out of the trustee business ; what profits they
make are mainly from investments. This prosperous
co. could, however, pay about 8 per cent, dividend out
of its commissions alone.
* * * *
The Victorian State Land Bank has raised its
latest loan at a figure which makes the interest £3 18s.
per cent, per annum. (Two years ago the bank could
raise money at £3 7s. lOd. per cent.) The bank lends
out to farmers at £4 10s. per cent., but expenses absorb
about 10s., therefore it really only gets, say, £4 per cent.
There is, therefore, at present rates, a margin of only 2s.
per cent, for profit and to cover contingencies, and as
this is obviously insufficient, and the margin is liable to
get smaller still, there is talk of a necessity for raising
the lending rate to 5 per cent. The misfortune of all
the State Land Banks is that they all depend
for their lending capital on borrowed money. The
true remedy is not to borrow money, but to supply the
necessary funds out of the State revenue, and as State
Treasurer Shiels now says he will have £40,000 surplus
(solely by reason of Turner’s careful financing and
Kingston’s careful collection of the customs revenue from
reluctant and unprincipled importers) he might hand
that over to begin with. This idea would involve the
wildly novel proposition that the State should have
some money saved and earning 4 per ceqt. interest, but
even that shock might be got over. If the State started
a sinking fund, and invested part of it in its land bank,
it would be a very good investment; when the State does
have a sinking fund (which is once every third blue moon)
•t yields much less than 4 per cent, as a rule.

Snow’s syndicate has dropped quite out of
P. 1 }' 3 ' lo notice now, and Adelaide citizens have given up
all hope of obtaining electric trams as a result of the
Act passed two years ago. Already an agitation has
been started in favor of either the State or the munici-
palities doing the work. The city and suburban councils
cannot come to an agreement even to build a drain, so
that the only chance of a better service lies in the State
taking the matter in hand. As for the syndicate’s lying
papers and politicians, who assured the people two years
age that the capital was available and that work would
commence immediately—well, they are lying low on that
subject; lying high on others.

The Queensland National, which took first place among
Australian banks in the matter Of interest earnings 12
months ago, has reached third place ; the Australasia and
the Westralia have moved ahead of it. The Adelaide
shows the most substantial improvement; after
that, the E.S. and A. But that probably needs
qualifying. The E.S. and A. makes up its accounts
annually, to June 30; the balance-sheet is available in
Australia at the end of the year. The figures for this
bank are, therefore, for the period from July, 1901, to June,
1902 : and that was part of the period oi the N.S.W. bor-
rowing boom, and before tire dull, grey drought settled on
the land. The increases shown by the Union and Aus-
tralasia are also discounted in the same way. The
banks which have increased their dividend are the New
Zealand (shareholders have now had the first taste since
the State rescued them nine years ago), the Australasia,
the E.S. and A., and the Victoria (ordinary shareholders).
Some other particulars follow. Banks which
make up their accounts yearly and those which do so
half-yearly are kept separate

* * * *
Some of the troubles of the London Bank of
Australia are coming to light. The Victoria Brewery
(Melb.) owed it £154,000, and it smiled upon the pro-
posal of one of its officers to wipe the debt out. As
part of the scheme, the Melb. Brewing and Distilling Co.
was promoted, and trouble has arisen over that part of
the business. The debenture-holders alleged that the
prospectus deceived them, and threatened proceedings
against the bank as the real promoter ; and the bank,
which has undergone some changes in its high places
since the business was taken up, paid the debenture-
holders £21,000 to let the matter drop—a pretty bad con-
fession for a self-respecting bank to have to make.
* * * *
S.A., which until a few years back depended
almost wholly upon Sydney for its supply of oranges,
found it could grow citrus fruits itself, and plunged into
the business without knowing much about it. The result
.is that, though remarkably fine fruit is being grown a
few miles back from the coast, thousands of trees which
were planted on the Adelaide plains, within reach of the
stiff sea breezes, have had to be rooted out. For all that,
there are now close upon 120,000 trees in bearing as
against 73,000 six years ago. But the outlook for the
industry isn’t very promising ; efforts to open up a profit-
able export business have so far failed.
* * * *
The Westralian Government has a £lO,OOO libel
action on hand. Lately a perambulating Minister was
told that one of the mining wardens and magistrates had
a habit of borrowing money from people who were very
likely, at some time or other, to be interested in matters
before his court: and when the Minister, who by this
time had scented trouble and called, in a colleague, dug
deeper he was told that the warden had also got himself
mixed up in local mining leases. The warden’s defence
was that he wasn’t interested—it was his wife. The
next thing alleged was that the warden had recom-
mended the cancellation of a hospital reserve and its
addition to the ka«e in which his wife was interested,
and that on top of this, he recommended that the ground
needn’t be worked. This was followed by dismissal, as
the officer wouldn’t resign—he held that ail his
actions with regard to the hospital reserve and the
lease were defensible and above board. Over things said
by the Minister for Mines in the course of these ructions
a £lO,OOO writ has been issued.
* * *
Broken Hill Water Supply Co. has permitted
an independent survey of the quantity of water available.
The examiners (the local warden and another) found
less than there was when they previously took soundings,
during the threatened famine in Feb., 1901. At that time,
it was officially calculated there was three months
supply • so that there cannot now be more than six
weeks’.’ The mines and town may hang on a week or two
more by digging holes in the silt and draining it. This
is the nearest to the bottom the place has ever got.
* * * *
British Broken Hill, as the result of recent
developments and tests, estimates that it can make a
protit of £22,500 a year with lead at £l2 and silver at Is.
lOd. standard. A penny rise in silver adds about £2500
to this profit.

finds have been made under the band in ground that was
formerly abandoned and mullocked-up in Block 1., and
this is leading to more deep sinking.
* * * «
The Tasmania G.M. Co. (Tas.), which on a
paid-up capital of £159,559 has distributed nearly
£BOO,OOO in divs., almost all within the last five years, is
now short of funds to carry on necessary development,
and alleges that the only way to get the money is to give
J. Bull more than half the property on condition that he
puts £160,000 into the concern. At the meeting held in
Launceston to consider the position it was proposed, in
opposition to the directors’ ideas, that the necessary
funds should be called up; hue that sensible
proceeding was voted down with extraordinary
enthusiasm—hv 45,692 shares to 3856. One of the
most remarkable arguments used to support the proposed
deal with J. Bull was that they “ would now have good
management.” As a matter of fact, with hardly an
exception, Bull-managed Australian mines have been a
ghastly failure ; in the few Westralian cases in which the
mines have been phenomenally rich and good dividends
have been paid, the management has still been disastrous.
All the same, it certainly couldn’t be much worse than
the Tasmania’s has been hitherto ; a co. which finds
itself stranded after averaging about 100 per cent, per
annum profit for the past five years, can’t very well be
succeeded by anything worse. The proposed English co.
will be in 500,000 shares, of which the present co. will get
210,000 ; £150,000 to he provided as working capital, and
the old eo.’s debts (about £10,000) to be paid off.
* * * *
“Queensland Menzies (W.A,) is looking better
than ever before. In the winze, 110 ft. below the 446 ft.
level, the lode is 12ft. wide and good. The hanging and
footwall lodes, which in the upper levels are separated
by mullock, appear to be making into one solid body.
Co. has already paid three 6d. divs. this year, and should
easily pay three more before the year expires. The mine
is better equipped than in past years, having now a rock-
breaker, self-feeders, and rock drills at work, besides an
additional 5 head of stamps Co.’s div. record has been :
1897, 4s. ; ’9B, 2s. ; ’99, Is. 6d.; ’OO, 6d. ; ’Ol, Is. 6d. ; ’O2,
Is. 6d. ; and ’O3 (five months), Is. 6d. Costs have been
up to nearly £6 a ton, but are now very much less. The
lode is so fiat that 100 ft. vertical gives about 180 ft. of
backs.
* *
In the Lady Shenton (W.A.) the ore bodies
have always been very patchy and erratic, and Manager
Bayfield deserves every credit for the way he Has handled
such a difficult proposition. To date the co. has paid 16
divs., totting up £120,000, or 75 per cent, on its issued
capital. It is now proposed to carry the shaft down to
1000 ft., and this will entail a very large outlay, and a
complete cessation of returns for quite a year. The pre-
sent equipment is also obsolete.
* » * *
Cue G. M. and Exploration Co. (W.A.), a six-
times reconstructed wild cat, holding two leases known
as Kinsella and Last Chance in the neighborhood of Great
Fingall Consols, Day Dawn, has bought the discarded
mill engine and 20 head of old stamps from the Ivanhoe.
This machinery is now en route to the mine, and will be
ready for work in three months. The property’s main
shaft is down about 200 ft., but no ore has been devel-
oped, so that apparently the same old muddling that has
already ruined the co. five times is being gaily pursued.
After nine years of alleged mining no work has been done
even at 200 ft., and the property is unproved one way or
t’other. Being in a deservedly popular district, shares
are certain to be worked for all they are worth. As to
the mine, it is pretty certain to prove a failure, no
matter how good it may really be.
* * * *
Golden Horseshoe (W.A.) had a fairly good
year in 1902, though divs. only totalled 18s., against 235.
per share in 1901. Costs ran into 675., against 31s. of its
neighbor, the Ivanhoe. One reason for this is that the
Horseshoe smelts some 1500 tons a month, on which
smelting and transit charges alone tot up about £4 a ton.
The chief cause of the high costs has been the subsidence
of the whole of the original cyanide plant owing to its
having been erected on the cap of the lode. The creep
is within 20ft. of the old 50-head mill, hut the latter
is apparently on solid ground, and is battering away
gaily. The cause of the creep was that the boundary
lode down to the 200 ft. level was partly in Great Boulder
and partly in Horseshoe ground. The former co. took
out its portion of the lode first, and mullocked up the
ground. When the Horseshoe got to work this mullock
come in, and, in spite of all precautions, a creep occurred,
but the ground appears now solid. Ore reserves are esti
mated at 700,000 tons worth 26dwt. —-far the biggest
reserves of any mine on the fields. Preparations are being
made to duplicate the new mill, and also to send ore to
the Fremantle smelters. Probably the monthly output
will shortly reach 20,0000 z., and, when the new mill gets
going, the co. will easily be the world’s leading producer.
A feature of the mine is that all engines are duplicated,
so that a stoppage through a breakdown is next to an
impossibility. Dr. Krusch has just examined the mine on
behalf of the German holders, and Mr. Frechville for the
French ditto.
* * * *
Bellevue Proprietary, Mt. Sir Samuel (W.A.).
The new 40-head mill is expected to be running before the
end of the year, but very little in the way of ore reserves
is being opened up in anticipation. The co. is more than
paying its way with a 20-head mill, but the mine, possibly
through bad management, has belied all the fulsome
praise that has been lavished on it.
* * * *
Eraser South Extended (W.A.) reports a big reef
worth 22dwt. at 250 ft., and a smaller one worth 9dwt. at
300 ft. In face of this large supply of ore, the co.'s mill runs
eight days a month and treats 500 tons for 1600 z., or
barely 6£dwt. per ton. The gold is very low grade,
' being worth only £3 an ounce. Tailings are alleged to
be worth 6dvvt. If true, the mill is only saving half the
gold. The co. has now been toiling for seven years, and,
on Sept. 18, 1900, paid a miserable div. of 3d. per share—
or £1250. Since then it has twice increased its capital,
each time by the issue of 25,000 shares, and is now in
150,000 shares of £1 each. Mine is well equipped with a
30-head mill, cyanide plant, roek-breaktr, automatic
feeders, rock-drills, &c. That the management has been
bad goes without saying, as, in addition to £45,000 of
Bull’s money, the proceeds of all gold won have also gone,
all above the 300 ft. level. It is fair to state that the pre-
sent manager (Badge) only took charge lately, and haa
now got £25,000 with which to test his abilities In running
a mine.

In the first five years after the inspection of
mines was introduced to Queensland, in 1881, one miner
was killed for every 30,3800 z. of gold that was won. In
the last five years the bill has been one for every’ 55,1900 z.
On an average one Q. miner in every 600 is killed
annually.

SOCIETY
AT THE PRIVATE HOSPITAL.
“The most dangerous stage of a man’s ailment is when
he begins to nurse his nurse.” — Sage reflection.
THE INTRUDING WIFE: “My gracious vie, and
they reported him entirely out of danger only
this morning! ”
The drought continues. Last month’s
crop of infants in Sydney was the poorest for
this five-year period.
From an account of the cyclone at Towns-
ville written by the Bishop of North Queensland :
Those who have been face to face with death can under-
stand how we prayed, and we noted that the first break
in the clouds came within ten minutes of our prayer.
Here the old trouble crops up. A cyclone always
stops some time, and it always stops some ten
minutes after something has happened, whether
prayer is used or not; so does a drought, or a
flood, or a night, or a plague. Therefore, the
efficacy of prayer in these matters can never be
proved, nor disproved. It is all a laborious beg-
ging of the question. If the Church, by prayer,
could stop something that wasn’t bound, in the
ordinary course of things, to stop in any case; or
could start something that might, quite reason-
ably, never have started at all, then it would have
something solid to go upon. A range of mountains
10,000 ft. high, extending from Port Darwin to
Adelaide, would be a better thing to pray for
than rain, for they would supply rain perma-
nently. And their arrival after prayer would be
a proof that could hardly be set aside.
• * * * *
“ C.R.” on humorous remains :
The belief that a corpse has no humor must be revised.
One night recently the driver and fireman of the New-
castle to Sydney train slowed up at Epping Station and
reported that they had passed the body of a man lying
on the down line. Two constables started out hot-foot
with an ambulance stretcher. They arrived at the place
indicated. No corpse. They examined every yard of
the permanent way by moonlight. Still no corpse nor
the slightest trace of a tragedy. Then it was resolved
that the unfortunate remains must have been carried off
by the 5.30 p.m. train from Sydney. The driver of that
train was telegraphed to at Hornsby and he made an agi-
tated examination of his engine, but found no trace of
blood or hair. Then it was reluctantly concluded that
deceased must have been drunk, or else merely humorous,
aud had walked away after making his little sensation.
This recalls the case of the corpse that used to be seen
huddled up on the side of the road between Hornsby and
Parramatta. Just opposite where it was wont to squat
was a stump, not more than a foot high but the cause of
many accidents, two, at least, of which were fatal. The
corpse was always there on these occasions. One night a
number of bold spirits lay in wait for it. It did not
appear, but a man, who passed by in a buggy while the
watchers were there, said he saw it on the other side of
the road. He died soon after. That corpse had a beat of
its own, like a policeman, extending from the black ruin
of the convict station to the place where it was hanged.
It was liable to be seen lying around anywhere between
these points. Old residents used to say that that corpse
could not be killed, but it may be dead by this time.
* * * *
One of the complaints of the Norfolk
Islanders, ventilated during the recent guber-
natorial visit, was the absence of a local fashion
journal and the want of sympathy in that respect
by the Sydney papers, which don’t report the
island “tea and public.” “We have nice enter-
tainments, ”said one patriarch, “but are not able
to keep a chronicle of them.”
* * » *
Maori tragedies are not frequent. When
they do happen, the best brown girl
is generally at the root of the trouble.
The most notable case of late years was
that of Makaore, an unattractive-look-
ing, bull-necked person,
who was hanged at Napier
for shooting a white named
Gollan, whose house he
afterwards set on fire. He
suspected Gollan of having
gained the affections of a
rather greasy brown girl,
who said that she
wo u Idn’t have
Makaore at any
price, and for
whom, apparent-
ly, Gollan didn’t
care much either.
Latest tragedy at
Rata, in Wanganui
district, was the out-
come of insane jealousy
on the part of Kereopa
Ihaka, who possessed a
rather good-looking wife
—so far as the brown
woman ever is good-
looking. He damaged
her badly with a knife,
and then proceeded to
hack himself with the same
weapon. When it was taken
from him he went full tilt at
a wall, in the hope of dashing
his brains out. His skull
being too thick, he grasped a
tomahawk and whanged him-
self on the cranium with much
vigor until stopped. Then
he died in Wanganui hos-
pital, and the jury found
that Kereopa Ihaka had
committed suicide. It didn’t
say he was out of his mind.
Possibly it was of opinion
that he had never owned much
mind to be out of.

Reported to Sydney City Council the
other day that “fine lolly ” —a stuff that is some-
times sold as “ Turkey lolly”—was in one instance
made at a “factory ” which comprised two rooms.
In the factory lived o.ie white woman, three
colored men, six dogs, three cats, and other
animals whose small size and velocity prevented
accurate calculation of their number. Our
“loyal” and insanitary fellow subject is great on
other animals.
Sundry attempts to stir up the “ English-
men” of Maoriland to celebrate St. George’s Day
ended in miserable and well-deserved failure. At
one well-advertised meeting only two persons,
including a reporter, turned up. Pity Irishmen
and Scotchmen could not also be persuaded to
leave off kicking up antics over a mere myth.

“ Hairy Dayid ” : I’ve noticed of late
that among my friends, the street beggars, the
barrel-organ has been replaced by the
squeaking gramaphone. Originally,
when the ’phone was a novelty, it had
the power of collecting large crowds,
but the novelty now has worn off. My
friend, Blind Johnny, an Adelaide
beggar, t’other day, broke his ’phone,
so brought out his organ, which
he had kept in case of emergency.
It is the only organ about town,
and being something of a
novelty, Johnny has been
doing so well that he
hasn’t troubled to have
his ’phone repaired.
Speaking of that’phone,
I heard a remark passed
one night in reference
to it. A crowd was
listening to it struggling
with “ Ave Maria”
when a couple passed on
t’other side of the road.
She said, “What’s
goin’ on over there?’’
He replied, “ Oh, I
s’pose it’s a woman
takin’ a fit; I can
’ear ’er scream-
in’.”
A singing
star who re-
cently visited
Australia did
not follow the
rule of telling
all aspirants
who pestered
him to try
their voices,
that they had
great pros-
pects ahead of
them. One
Society damsel
approached
the visitor to
ascertain her
chances of a
career. He
wished to let
her down lightly,
so descanted on
the cost of living and
paying for a musical
education in London. “ But,
of course,” said the aspirant,
“ I need have no fear as to
that. My people will allow
me £5OO a year.” The star
spoke of the influence re-
nnired to cet a heariTiß-.
“But my pa has heaps of
influence,” persisted the young lady; “he’s So-
and-So, and the question of influence needn’t
stand in the way an instant.” “Then,” said the
bored star, “ then you have everything except the
voice.” And the interview closed with a slam.
* • • *
“Ku-Klux”: I happened into a Mel-
bourne suburban station the other day. Since the
strike the men have been compelled to manage
alone (that is, only one man instead of two on at
a time), according to the deranged ukase of Bent.
It is a fairly busy station. The unfortunate clerk
was selling tickets, train standing at the plat-
form, man waiting to send a telegram, clerk try-
ing to take a telegram by cocking his ear and
repeating the words as they were ticked off. Man
came in to pay for some goods. Arguing the
point with him about rates, clerk gave wrong
change to two people. One (the one who got too
little) came back to swear at him. Another man
came to consign a ton of bonedust. Asked where
he would take it to. Clerk (still trying to sell
tickets, send and receive telegrams," and argue
with the first two men) told him to take it to hell.
Bonedust man wanted to fight. Six more people
wanted tickets. Man with telegram wanted to
fight. Three more people got wrong change.
Finally, clerk seized bell, and, dashing out on
platform, rang it furiously, leaving more people
clamoring for tickets. Then stoker sauntered up
with news that train couldn’t start for half-an-
hour—hot box or something. Then that clerk sat
down and did what the bonedust man and all the
other men had already done—used language.
* * * *
The little brown men dressed in blue who
have been haunting Sydney of late are an inquiring
crowd and their desire for information is incred-
ible. They go into shops and inspect the shop-
walker, and smile on the girls behind the counter,
and feel the texture of things, and lift things, and
look underneath things, and withal they beam so
gently on everybody that no one cares to throw
them out. They go upstairs and downstairs, and
people on top-floors are surprised to find Japs
coming in to explore and paw and beam.
Whether they carry away muon knowledge is
another question, but they jog inscrutably
back to their ships like men who are loaded down
with information, and when anybody speaks to
them they show 32 teeth in their desire to look
happy and intelligent.
• • • *
The Burdekin mansion in Macquarie-
street, Sydney, about to undergo a change of
ownership, occupies the site of Sydney’s first
Quaker chapel. The notorious John " Tawell
made a lot of money in a menial capacity in the
wooden hospital nearly opposite, and turning
chemist became one of the wealthy men of the
time. He built the chapel referred to, and
dedicated it to the Quakers. The present was
not appreciated, however, and the building passed
into the hands of the senior Burdekin. After
a time it was demolished, and the present man-
sion erected on the ground. Tawell, in addition
to being a most pious Quaker, led the anti-liquoT
crowd, and is said to have, in a fit of zeal, emptied
COO gallons of rum into the harbor. He was
a swindler most of his life, and on returning to
England was hanged for murdering his mistress.

handed over by a half-bred
Chinese girl. In some
places in Mongrelia it is
rather hard now to find
a representative citi-
zen who isn’t a bit
of an Asiatic.
AN EFFECT OF THE
COUPON SYSTEM.
1ST. LADY: “ Widow Smith buys only where they
give coupons; she has a mania for collecting
them. ”
2ND. LADY: “ Yes; when Smith died she tried to
find an undertaker who gave 'em .”
The Law decided a queer case at Geelong
(Vic.) lately. The matter was one in which a
harmless, necessary Mary Jane sued her ex-
employer for damages for trespass and assault.
It seems the damsel was about to leave her
‘place,’ and the evening before her departure
stayed out till about 10.30. About 1 a.m. her
alleged employer—one of the Sleepy Hollow’s
representative citizens—burst open the door of
her room and commanded her to dress forthwith
and get out into the night. After some demur,
the young woman says, she dressed herself and
decamped, the citizen meanwhilj standing by
viewing the proceedings and urging her to greater
haste in getting inside her corsets and other
haberdashery. That was the girl’s story, and
without hearing the employer’s story, Judge
Gaunt decided there was no ground of action, the
citizen not having committed any trespass in
breaking open his servant’s room door, and there
being no actual assault. As regards the trespass,
therefore, the Victorian law decides that a man
may legally break into the room occupied by any
woman in his house while she is dressing, or in
bed, or having a bath, and may remain there,
provided he doesn’t actually lay hands upon the
occupant. Does the Victorian law exactly know
what it is talking about when it holds that a
woman has no right to keep a man out of her
bedroom, provided that man owns or rents the
house ?
* * * *
The worship of the Japanese naval visitors
to which a section of the Australian public has
given itself up with a fury of gush during the last
few weeks, means little except that some flighty
people are willing to follow any craze, whether it
is a black cyclist or a yellow sailor, so long as it is
the fashion of the moment. And in a uniform
any sort of man looks very much like other sorts;
put him in a red jacket with some gold braid and
rooster feathers, and a Jap seems as admirable as
a European in the feminine eye—and feminine
eyes are not confined to women. Easily
enough explainable, the Jap craze is none
the less foolish. The women—of both sexes—
who clustered around the Japs would look with
scorn on Chinese; and yet the Chinaman is a
{; nitleman compared with the Jap. He may not
lave the same grimaces of flattery, or the same
cheeky gifts of imitation, but he is infinitely more
honest and honorable. Even in Japan, you will
find the positions of trust mainly held by Chinese;
they don't trust a Jap to hold the money in his
own country. Merchants trading with Asia, too,
will tell that, whilst a Chinaman keeps, good
faith, the Jap merchant is usually a liar and a
rascal. If Australian society folk need to gush
over any Asiatic, they should at least have the
judgment to choose the Chow; but since, by
special legislation, the law keeps all Asiatics out
of the country, all gush should be done by tele-
scope.
THE GREAT PHILANTHROPIST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I gathered my gold with the stolen brains
Of men who were slaves. Their bodies and souls
Toiled for me ; I but held the reins ;
And now that the thunder of censure rolls,
I sit with my millions in sumptuous ease,
And bequeath to the nations my libraries.
I rewarded my minions with scanty fare,
And hoarded the gold that their hands had.made.
I doled out bread and pence as their share
Of the money ground from their souls dismayed.
And the sovereigns grew to a mighty pile ;
While mine was a mission to watch and smile.
When millions were mine I paused, and thought
Of the world to come that is mostly gold ;
And I guessed that a front seat might be bought
With a million or two, if all weren’t sold !
And it might turn out an excellent deal,
For there’s plenty of gold up there to steal.
So I cast around for a starting-place
To unload my soul of the weight it bore.
To die with wealth is to die in disgrace—
’Tis strange that I thought not of that before !
You can tread on the toilers and steal and lie,
If you scatter some millions before you die.
So I tip my God, and I bargain with man ;
I say my wealth is a useless thing;
1 boast that I use it as best I can,
That joy to humanity it may bring.
I heed not the starving, the blind and lame--
I spend my gold for applause and fame!
And the wrecks I have made pass daily by,
While men call me blessed and worship my gold;
And I hear not the hungry and hopeless cry
Of those who to me their bodies sold,
But I sit with my millions in sumptuous ease,
And bequeath to the starving my libraries!
M.L.
JOHN PATRICK.

Tlio unspeakable Victorian magistracy had
an offender after its own heart before it the other
day—Robert Hill, laborer. To quote the Age
report:
Accused struck her and the child, knocked them down,
and dragged her by the hair into the yard, where he
punched her in the face and made her mouth bleed. lie
dragged the baby from her, and kicked her as she lay on
the ground. The child’s leg was in splints, as it was
suffering from hip disease, and they were torn off.
Attractedbycr.es for help, Mrs. Ralph rushed into the
yard, apd snatched up the infant. Accused then turned
on her like a savage beast, and struck her and the child
several blows. She and Miss Cottril escaped from the
house. He then burst in the door, and again assaulted her.
He subsequently kicked Miss Cottril several times A
fewminutes afterwards defendant’s sister-in-law, Maria
Weber, widow, residing in Lygon-street, rushed to the
house, and found Hill brutally kicking his wife as she
lay on the floor. He took off his coat, rolled up his
sleeves, and, leaping towards Mrs. Weber caught her
by the wrist as she was making her escape through the
front door, saying, “ I’ll do the same to you.” After a
fierce struggle she got away with bruised hands and a
badly strained side. The police then arrived on the
scene and took Mrs. Hill, who was still unconscious, to
the Melbourne Hospital, where she remained for several
days. On the charge of assaulting his wife he was fined
£a, or two months’ imprisonment; and on each of the
other charges he was fined £2, or one month’s imprison-
ment.
This was at the Carlton Court. A Bench which
could punish a vile ruffian like that with a fine
must have a strong practical sympathy with
brutality. The Bulletin would not care to meet
the Carlton bench in a lonely street on a dark
night.
* * * *
A Maoriland man got an inb.nt on his
hands in quite an original way lately. There
were two perambulators on Linwood, a way-side
railway-station, and two women talking. “To
Blankimoa, ” said one woman, nodding at a pram.
The porter whisked a pram into a van, and just
then the train started. Then there was a shriek.
The wrong pram had been taken—the pram that
was left was empty ; the other one had an infant
buried under a large quantity of wool. A gallant
Maorilander, sizing up the situation, jumped into
the moving van ; and when he had patted the
infant off to sleep he started to think. And he
went on thinking when he lifted the pram out at
the next station and was told that the next train
back wouldn’t be till next day. Then a sad
Maorilander started to wheel a pram and a baby
back to Linwood by road, the infant howling as
he went. He was wearily shoving his burden up
miles of hills and down miles of dales when he
met an excited mother who had been sprinting
after the train.

A new principle of law was introduced at
the Adelaide Criminal Sittings, a recent English
case being followed. The “dying depositions ’
of a girl in a hospital were rejected, because they
were not taken down verbatim, question with
answer, so as to show how much of the declara-
tion was voluntary and how much suggested by
the questioner. Also, it was not clear that the
girl was in a “hopeless settled expectation of
death ” (although she died a few days later), so
the depositions were bad every way, and the
prisoner, a “ lady herbalist,” was at once declared
“ Not Guilty.” Her counsel was Rupert Ingleby,
son of the Q.C. of that name who was drowned
with Judge Wearing in the Gothenburg wieck
many years ago, and both Judge Boucaut and the
Crown Solicitor admitted that they had not been
aware of the new point of law successfully raised.
Sydney's Sunday Slimes, which recently
humbly opined that Australia was honored by
the presence of a small brown Sultan, thus opens
a report of the visit of the Japanese :
Less than a couple of years ago we were honored with
a visit from Royalty, and the bonds of Federation were
forged. Now the historian must steep his pen once more
to record the visit of the Japanese fleet, the importance
of which is perhaps not fully realised at this juncture.
The Mary Ann office “historian” then proceeds
to “ steep his pen ” :
By the time the Vice-Regal and other representatives
graced the picturesque park with their presence, the scene
was reminiscent of the occasion of the swearing-in cere-
mony by his Royal Highness the Prince of Wales—then
the Duke of Cornwall and York—of the first Governor-
General and members of the first Commonwealth
Ministry. (/ /!)
It is only two years since the Yorklet was here,
yet the intensely loyal Sunday Grime has already
forgotten (or was apparently too dead to notice
it at the time) that G.-G. Hopetoun and the
Commonwealth Ministry had been sworn in
before the Duke of Cornyork arrived, and that
said person simply opened the Commonwealth
Parliament in Melbourne, whilst his sole perform-
ance in Centennial Park amounted to the fact
that he held a third-rate review there on a rainy
day.
* * * *
Governor-General Tennyson stated, at a
recent Lord Mayoral luncheon, that the present
salary attached to his lofty job is quite sufficient
to maintain two Government Houses and keep
up the dignity of the position—which is rough on
the frail Hopetoun, who left Australia a crushed
wreck, all owing to insufficient pay. For once
Australia cordially agrees with Tennyson. There
are many points of disagreement, but the suffici-
ency of the salary is a matter on which there is
absolute unanimity.

Claimed by Mr.
E. B. Havell, in the
Nineteenth Cen-
tury, that the vic-
torious Englishman,
not cont e nt with
bleeding India white,
has set himself also to
vulgarise the Hindu,
“ who before stood on
a higher artistic plane than
himself.” The typical Indian village arts, such as
pottery, metal-working, and weaving, have been, he
states, ruined by shoddy, aud Indian architecture
has been smothered in the interests of the vulgarly
pretentious and banal English architect. Australia
has been so accustomed to take the Downing-st.
view of the “magnificent work of civilisation”
being done by Britain in India that it is dis-
quieting to find an eminent authority (Mr.
Havell is director of the Calcutta School of Art)
holding that “the Empiie” there is associated
with what Spencer calls re-barbarisation.

executions will make the first departure for many

mends to mercy. In view, too, of the various
incidents surrounding the trial—the dis-
charging of one jury to allow of the informer
for the Crown having his memory refreshed ; the
cheap and absurdly inadequate provision for the
accused’s legal defence; and the hint of the Full
Court that an appeal on points not taken by the
cheap defence might possibly have succeeded—tl e
See Government had better, in the interests c f
the community, refrain from sending the two men
to the gallows. Perpetual imprisonment will be
just as adequate a safeguard to the public.

“'Vul ” contributes a slanderous item :
The review given in honor of the visiting Jap warships
turned out about the most humorous circus Sydney has
been treated to for some time. Original intention was.
for State Commander Finn to act as master of circuses,
but, at the last moment, the supreme Hutton took
charge. First bungle happened with the landing of the
brown visitor. He had been instructed to land at Rush-
cutters’ Bay, and guides and mounted police were await-
ing him there to conduct him to the ground. But the
Jap landed unexpectedly at Farm Cove, and by the
time a small boy and a dog had rushed off to notify the
authorities of the fact, and the authorities had dispatched
the police and the guides to the spot in a violent hurry,
the said authorities had become quite hot and confused.
When the procesh got under way at last, it was
suddenly discovered, with mirth, that the visitor
wasn’t putting implicit faith in his hosts. Notwith-
standing that a British officer led the way and
mounted police were in attendance, Sydney was
treated to the spectacle of two small Jap officers
walking in advance of the contingent, studying now a
large map of the city, then the route they were following,
in an undisguised determination not to be led into
ambush. The Jap Admiral had evidently heard about
the Push. When the force reached Centennial Park, the
authorities were scandalised to find that the visitors had
come fully armed and evidently prepared to die hard.
After inspection by the Governor-General and other
personages, the combined forces on show began to per-
form. Half-a-dozen bosses were giving different direc-
tions. When the general advance was about to begin,
the British Tars and their Jap brothers got the order in
advance of the rest, and at once began to move. After
they had gone a bit, however, they discovered that they
were alone, so they made a violent effort to reach the
line again, but had hardly right-about-faced when the
rest of the troops began to advance, and the two sections
straightway fell into each other’s arms like long-lost
brothers. The height of the farce was reached at the
conclusion, when the O.C. called for cheers for his Jap
Majesty, and, when these had been given with doffed
helmets, completely forgot to call for a similar yell for
our own lawful King. What the consequences of this
disloyal oversight will be, Heaven only knows.

“ Kati-Kati ”: Re birth-rate. Live in a
street where there are 17 adults (not counting ser-
vants) and only 10 children. Parents are all
married, most of them for between 5 and 10 years,
and there seems little likelihood of additions to
population. Incomes from £2 to £4 a week.
Affairs must surely be very prosperous in the
country Australian youngsters come from, that we
cannot induce them to emigrate. P.S.—There
are 20 cats in our street.

Brisbane just; now is a soft snap for
Burglar Bill and Co. The principal reasons are
the ineptitude of those in command and the short
manning of the police force. In one densely
populated suburb containing many bad characters
one constable was left to protect an area of over a
square mile and thirty miles of streets on a recent
Saturday night. Years back an expensive Royal
Commission proposed many drastic alterations,
but the report was ignored, and the old policy x>f
let it slide still continues. In fact, it slides now
more cheerfully than ever.

A WOMAN’S EYES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ILL.
Mine eyes have feasted on the sight
Of morning’s dawn above the trees,
And watched the purple haze of night
Beget the soft, sweet, sun-dip breeze ;
My vision rapt hath known the best
Of beauty that the earth doth prize,
But give to me, above the rest,
The love-light in a woman’s eyes.
Ah—the mask may hide but not disguise
The love-light in a woman’s eyes.
MURU.
RELIGION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We chant it out in accents mellow:
“ All we like sheep have gone astray.”
The church-lights gleam rich red and yellow ;
We chant it out in accents mellow,
And mean it—of the other fellow !..
On each successive Sabbath Day
We chant it out in accents mellow :
“ All we like sheep have gone astray.”
S. ELLIOTT NAPPER.
TRIUMPH OF EDUCATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Q.
Young Johnny’s left at last the school—
For joy he cuts a caper ;
He struts, a brainless, lettered fool
Who’s learnt to read a paper.
His creed is yet a trifle crude,
His manners show the Vandal;
But see—his mind is seeking food
Amid the printed scandal.
He browses now on sweet divorce,
And wonders at the Lady ;
He follows now the Ring, the Course,
And all the gossip shady.
He takes a page of murder fell,
And just a pinch of arson ;
Pursues the tale of Daisy Bell
Who drugged and robbed a parson.
And so he reads till all his soul
Is dim with murky vapor ;
For Art and School have won the goal—
He’s learnt to read a paper !
P.L.
