RAINCOATS.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, Juno 16,1903.
Mt Dear Arini,—
Just as we were learning to translate such
exercises as: “Have you the mat which the
Shogun gave to the proprietor of the tea-house,
for Plum-Blossom, the Geisha?” the Japs bade
us “Sayonara!” and the three Jap warships
shook the spray of our harbor off their rams and
swam northward. Sydney society is very sorry,
for the Japs were amusing, and were the cause
of a great outburst of entertainments : but their
departure relieves the two Vice regal staffs of a
regular old man of the sea. The showiest vice-
regal Chrysanthemuming of the past week was the
Tennysons’ “At Home,” at Federal Government
House. It was not numerous, only a few hundred
persons inclusive of the guests of honor, but it was
very fine as to quality—of the uniforms. Most of
them might have been bought straight out at the
Mint without preliminary assay.
As the function was so very official there was not
much youth mixed with the beauty. Exceptions
were the Cranbrook girl and a debutante, Mattea,
eldest daughter of Surgeon-Major Roth, the 7ft.
high, or so, swordsman. Debutante Roth, a tall
damsel, in fine white robe and white violets,
looked so very decorative in a chaste Corinthian
fashion that one didn’t object to the mirrors quad-
ruplicating her. Another exception was Senator
Neild’s perky, bird-like, little daughter. She
has been the idol of the junior Jap officers,
and was always surrounded by a crowd of the
inscrutable, alligator-eyed ones, who didn’t
understand most of her twitterings.
Lady Tennyson—l wish she would lessen the
intellectual aspect of her forehead with a few pin-
curls—had got herself into an epic gown of cream,
with scrolls of cobwebby black lace occurring at
frequent intervals all over it. Mrs. B. R. Wise,
who can make epigrams, but cannot do her hair
over a roll-top wire fence, had what looked like a
sleeveless redingote of point lace over a black
gown, and an extensive Watteau blue bow with
ends that trailed on the Vice-regal carpet. Mrs.
Fitzwilliam Curtis wore a white and pinky some-
thing that suggested apple and peach blossoms,
with a sash of wide velvet ribbon of an exquisite
shade of blue, tied donkeys’-bridge fashion at the
back.
The arrival of Monsignor Count Vay de Vaya,
at Federal Government House, as the Tennyson’s
guest, rather put the Japs in the background.
The count-priest, who is Apostolic Prothonotary,
whatever that may be, is a Hungarian nobleman,
and represented Francis Joseph at the late Vic-
toria's Diamond Jubilee. He is in staggeringly
high society in the old world, and has been a
guest at the Imperial humpies in Russia and
China. At the recent Vice-regal-Mayoral func-
tions Monsignor, in his violet robes, faced with
scarlet, had the effect of something new under
our sun. He is slender, of medium height, and
40 years of age, but, even in the noonday light,
looks 25. A thin, fresh-colored, grey-eyed, long-
nosed face, a modest, smiling manner, and a
much-decorated front are his. Amongst his
decorations is a gold medal, stamped with Franz
Josef’s side-whiskered profile ; a large cross with
large brilliants (might be topaz or chrysoprase)
hangs from his neck, and, as he talks (capital
English), lie constantly twirls a huge jewelled
ring on the finger that with a woman is the
engagement one. I didn’t notice how he parts
his hair, but his shoes are buckled patent leathers;
and he is probably the only high ecclesiastic who
ever visited us without a collection plate concealed
about his person.
Federal Premier’s wife and some military dames
escorted the Count into the moonlit night to
view from the lawn the illuminated warships, Jap
and British, set in the dark dream-like sea. If
the picture didn’t impress him our climate must.
Women strolling about at an hour before mid-
night in winter, moonrays and starshine on bare
shoulders and deltoid muscles. There are com-
pensations for absence from the Vatican, and
there are Australian nights as good as the Floren-
tine ones immortalised by Heine.
The Japs’good-bye took the farm of an “At
Home” on board their flagship, which was deco-
rated by sailor-made chrysanthemums, cherry-
blossoms and wisteria in rice-paper. The patient,
hard-working Rawsons were again on deck, look-
ing at what the Japs said was the biggest gun in
Australian waters, and watching the juggler pro-
duce eggs—dozens of them—from the hem of a
handkerchief (a griat gift, this, when eggs are 3s.
per dozen) and a laughing jackass out of a hat.
A little widow is a dangerous thing. No man
should be allowed to marry a widow, let alone a
relict older than himself, without first satisfying
a medical expert that he is free from all G.P.I.
symptoms. I am moved to these reflections by
the news of the murder of the Servian King and
Queen. Poor Natalie, the dead King’s mother,
might be properly described as a queen “pre-
eminent in grief.” Milan, her husband,
was a faithless consort, an arrant rascal, and
a bad ruler. He was the original of the
despicable potentate m Daudet’s “Kings in
Exile,” in which book ex-Queen Natalie and her
son (just murdered) also figure. When Milan
judiciously retired from his kingship, lest worse
things should happen to him, and stipulated for
what he evidently regarded as the safer post of
commander of the army of his son and successor,
boy Alexander took the throne, and the people
were prepared to love him extremely and join
hands all round. But a woman intervened. The
widow Maschin, who was one of Natalie’s ladies
of honor, made large, dark eyes at him. She was
close upon Natalie’s own age. Everyone at
Court saw the “eyes,” so the Queen-mother dis-
missed her. The widow as much as told the
Queen-mother to “ shift herself,” and married the
youthful King, in the face of a storm of opposi-
tion from his councillors, mother, subjects, and
even his fallen frisky father. The union sowed
dragons’ teeth, and the harvest was reaped in
blood last week.
In this sordid age, nobody can retain an illusion
long. It now appears that the diamond-dotted
teeth of the Sultan of Johore are a set worn only
on State occasions. His ordinary week-day teeth
are made of the usual materials. Thousands who
have watched for the opening of the royal mouth,
and been disappointed, will now understand the
wherefore of the mystery. The Sultan and Sul-
tana are staying at the Australia; where the
guests fail to take full meals—being too busy
watching for the Sultan to smile.
The death of Chief Under-Secretary Critchetb
Walker, on Saturday last, of pneumonia, was a
severe shock to old-time Sydney. He had been
suffering some years from diabetes, and whilst
attending the requiem service of the late Mr. J.
Toohey, at St. Mary’s Cathedral, he got a chill
and his death occurred after a week’s illness.
Mr. Walker was unmarried. A sister living at
Randwick (Sydney), and a brother in Queensland,
survive him. He had built a lovely house at Port
Hacking, near the cottages of his great friends,
the Jack Wants and the B. R. Wises, and con-
templated living there, after his retirement, and
having as much of his beloved fishing as he listed.
An ex-Melbourne woman journalist, now living
near London, recently read a paper before the
just-founded Austral Club in that city on (lean
up against something) “Ostrich Farming in the
Colonies As an Occupation for Women.” The
lady evidently thinks that ostrich-rearing is on
all fours with ordinary poultry keeping. A group
of large, violent birds, seven or eight feet high,
chronically on the watch to knock the stuffing
out of their keepers with a kick, do not provide a
suitable occupation for women here—or any-
where else. I hope the reader told the Aus-
tralite (who yearned *o go ostrich breeding) to
carry a forked stick, . a good long one at that,
to jamb against the bird’s neck while plucking his
feathers. If she had no stick and the violent bird -
started to pursue her, her only escape from being
a mangled remains would be to lie down fiat in a
hollow. The bird might dance on her but that
doesn’t quite kill, even though his weight is not like
that of a turkey, or any of the winged creatures
that women usually farm ; and he seldom leaps on
his victim for more than half-an-hour at a stretch,
though he may come baok for a final jump before
he definitely retires.
There will be a heavy mail, letters and parcels,
from Japan to Sydney, after the black warships
get home. That is if the Jap officers keep faith
which the girls to whom they promised to
send tea-sets, rugs, screens, booklets of verse, and
picture post-cards. Two of the brown officers
made their way up to the private house of one of
the Ministry to get his daughter’s autograph in
Bobby Burns birthday books. Most of the dam-
sels who met the little warriors are now exhibit-
ing perpendicular-line writing in tea-caddy char-
acters, opposite certain dates, and poetical
quotations, such as “’Tis better to have loved and
lost than never to have loved at all.”
Cards are out for an American dance to be
given by a group of U.S.A. citizens on July 3rd,
at Paddington Town Hall, to start at (to quote
the cards) “ half after eight.” The Governor-
General will be present, if he and Lady Tenny-
son are in Sydney on the date fixed.
Mrs. Lance Rawson, widow of a Queensland
squatter, was re-married in London recently to
Mr. F. R. Ravenhill.. Mrs. Rawson was known
in Australian journalistic circles and once ran a
Labor paper at Rockhampton, Queensland.
Sir Harry Rawson’s aide, Major Holman (now
don’t sound the 1), had a narrow escape from
death last week when riding in a paper chase got
up for the benefit of the little Tennyson boys.
The party was returning and when on the high
road near Cranbrook the Major’s horse bolted and,
slipping on the asphalt, threw its rider on to the
tram line. Though the brain concussion was not
serious, Major Holman has two severe cuts on the
back of the head.
Some items of the late J. T. Toohey’s will have
gone abroad as follows: —To his widow, £lOOO a
year for her private use and .£3OOO a year for the
up-keep of “Innisfail,” the Toohey house at
Wahroonga on the North Shore line. If the lady
marries she loses the £3OOO a year, but gets an
addition of £4OO a year to her pin money.
Maurice Toohey, the only son, has £6OOO a year
and cannot marry without the consent of
his stepmother and Mr. Sargood (son of the
late senator, one of the executors). If lie
marries against their joint consent, he loses 80 per
cent, of his inheritance. The three Misses
Toohey get <£31,000 each. A very small amount
is bequeathed to charities. And some relatives
by marriage get small legacies.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Justice Francis Winter, of Papua, and his
Melbourne bride have been starring at private
functions. They were at the Wattle Club’s
christening the other day, and had to stand as the
target of many eyes. The new Mrs. Winter
wears her dark clothes in a dashing manner. The
grey tinges in the Justice’s beard count for a
selateness and a solemnity not in his manner.
The gentleman is a very bright party, and much
less unbending than the local bencher.
“Mrs. Fred Stephen has set off for India’s
coral strand, where her chum and matrimonial
relative, Mrs. Bingham, reigns (so ’tis said) in a
swagger camp set. She was one of Melbourne
society’s most assured and energetic members,
and gave wonderful help to any charity she
sympathised with. Of late, for some reason or
other—mostly, I guess, the other—she has taken
a place in the Social Reserve corps, and iust
quietly piloted Mrs. Stephen (mother of Chief
Justicess Madden) along.
“The Cliveden widow’s friends, discussing her
daughter’s recent marriage, hark back to the
beginnings of the match. Bridegroom Lindsay,
then a bachelor member of Lord Beauchamp’s
Sydney staff, chanced to come over to Melbourne
‘ Clip ’ with his chief and party. The Beauchamp,
himself, so runs the tale, had in his own domain
been matrimonially hunted, journalistically har-
ried, and popularly discussed till he had lost his
nerve and his trepidation had infected his suite
(like himself, unmarried and unprotected). While
in Melbourne, the shy, skeerybmd was invited to
an extra chic dinner at Cliveden, whose hostess,
as is her wont, made no attempt to monopolise
her eligible guests, but bade to meet them the
Y beauty (more sylphlike than now), the
Z heiress (now married) —in short, all the most
sought-after charmers of the moment. Neverthe-
less, the honors of the evening fell easily to the
pale, pensive daughter of the house.
4 Welcome as a white violet to eyes weary of waratah ’
said the verse-quoting Beauchamp, meaning,
maybe, that shy girls are scarce in Australia.
The head of the band, ’tis understood, did not get
past ‘ lisping his appreciation in numbers,’ unlike
henchman Lindsay, who promptly set about
securing the ‘little southern flower,’now trans-
planted to his own garden.
“ Out back in a sister-State, they were recently
discussing details of a ball to be given in honor of
their State-Governor, touring thereabouts. A
supper-committee of ladies came in to expound
their views to the mere men on the financial
council. ‘Trifle would be nice’ mused a com-
mittee member, ‘but perhaps it’s too expensive.’
‘Tripe! Why tripe’s cheap enough!’ responded
a councillor, formerly engaged in meaty pursuits.
He was a hearty soul, and laughed louder than
anyone when they explained that ‘trifle’ was a
synonym for his old friend ‘ tipsy cake,’ but at the
end of his guffaw he remarked that ‘ considering
that His Excellency’s inside, like his own (the
speaker’s), was not so young as it used to be, he
shouldn’t wonder if midnight tripe wouldn’t sit
under the vice-regal waistcoat a jolly sight easier
than t’other stuff.’
“Carrie Haase, Mdme. Kellermann, and
Napoleon Boffard, plus Herr Kahn, gave a
pleasant little entertainment in Glen’s Hall,
t’other night. Miss Haase, who is the daughter
of a Melbourne printer, is a young elocutionist,
gifted with a fine voice, and deserving credit for
the fact that she recites stuff worth hearing on its
own merits. She has a bright, intelligent, attrac-
tive face, and looked well t’other evening in flow-
ing white and wreath of white roses in her hair.
On the other hand, the Haase stage “ actions,” so
far, are not free or graceful. Her first recital was
given up to the ‘Light of Asia.’ Next is a
‘ Tennyson Evening,’ while the last will deal with
Shakspeare, Stephen Phillips, and Schiller’s
‘Joan of Arc.’ Mdme. Kellermann showed fine
pace as a pianist, chasing airs through lightning
variations and catching them every time. Kahn’s
violin treatment of‘Gipsy Dances’ was spirited,
and Boffard contributed six songs in parcels of
three. Worst of song-groups is that the senti-
ment of one song can’t sink in, if hearers are
whisked without break to the vocal expression of
some other, perhaps diverse, emotion.
“That new sect which broke out near Bendigo
under the guidance of a long-haired American
gentleman named Jeffries, and had a revelation
to the effect that it was to leave off work, is
already in trouble The wife of the principal
local believer—presumably the one who left off
work hardest —has already appeared at the court
to find out the current quotation for a summons
for desertion. Whether the male convert has
departed on a white donkey for Jerusalem, or
how he has left, hasn’t transpired, but new sects
which leave off work seem to have no luck some-
how.
“ One of those who were victimised by a
notorious picture-dealer in Europe was Mrs. Ellis
Rowan, who had several hundreds of pounds’
worth of pictures brushed out of her income.
New York has been setting up her petite frame
by a series of new hygienics, which are supposed
to have scraped 20 years off her age.
“Amateur performers seem to think the
Excellencies and Waxellencies were sent into this
world to be offered up to them as a frozen sacrifice
in draughty halls. Incidentally, the Exes, and
Waxes, may be permitted to dabble in political
ceremonials and so forth, but their highest privi-
lege is to draw the gilt-edged frivollers (or some
of them) to amateur theatrical shows. But when
the State Clarkes and the stately Maddens turned
up unanimously to watch ‘ The "Prude’s Progress ’
at Prahran Town Hall, only a few people followed
the vice-regal lead, and the public came not at
all. Jerome’s comedy might have been better
amateured; but perhaps the small audience was
responsible.
“The playfulness of a set of ‘superior’ she-
mummers in a Melbourne hostelry is a never-
ending source of wonder and delight to other
sojourners. They skip and chase the leisure
hours with the inconsequent airs of children.
How artless!
“A woman doctor has voiced her alarm to
several patients, of the untold injury being
inflicted on the commissariat department of the
feminine frame by the steel girders and the
whaleboned clips employed in the corsets of the
hour. The compression of the waist in former
years is said to have been a mere mole hill to the
mountain of danger in the present lashing-in of
the pantry parts.
“The Western 3quattocracy is gradually getting
off to spend the results of recent wool clips
abroad. Half the Colac homesteads are left to
the moth and the rust, and the other things that
consume, while their owners gad on the Con-
tinent. The latest to weigh anchor are Mr. and
Mrs. Tom Fairbairn. The Christian Scientists
lose a strong supporter in Mrs. Tom, who sent all
Toorak wondering when she shook off her old
beliefs and her frivolities to embrace the new
faith. Mrs. Tom taught her chums to dispense
with the formula, ‘ How are you ? ’ Being a
staunch and ever-mindful scientist, she declared
she could never be anything but well, and was
insulted by the casual inquiry.
“ A visit the Hambourgs paid to the Loretto
Convent, Ballarat, has been much spoken of. It
appears that the musical brothers were fellow-
passengers with the Rev. Mother-General, head of
the Irish Order, and a distinguished Loretto sister
who is a great harpist, coming out on a visit to
Ballarat. Aboard ship, Boris was so distressed for
an accompanist that at last theR.M.-G. gave per-
mission for the sister to supply the piano
parts, and then the two Hambourgs’ manes shook
out their raptures at the sister’s skill. Hence the
call at the Convent.
“It is a wise Melbourne woman who knows
how to dress herself for Flemington. The other
Monday, the majority had ventured in silken
apparel under the clear sunshine and an assuring
sky. For an hour, the Terrace tiles glinted in the
goldness of things, and skirts were set a-trail.
Then came clouds, and finally heavy sheets of
rain. The Terrace was cleared as if by magic.
“ A few rows of smart flutterers at the holiday
Monday Concert in the Exhibition sat as proof
that Napoleon Boffard has the ear of some
of the Society set to which he has tootled so
long and so tenderly. Toorak, as a rule, does not
turn out to the ‘popular’ concert, so M. Boffard
may pat his chest y pillow a little harder than
usual, and say “Nap, my boy, ze ladies lofe
you.’’ His face, that night, glowed like a large
prize Jonathan apple, and his millinery effects
dazzled the audience to the faint dim back
rows of its odd thousands. Presently the moire
coat-flaps face a benefit house, and the stout
bosom of the Tenor will unburden its sweet song
for the last time, so they say, under a Melbourne
roof.
“ From the Friday inauguration by Premier
Barton it would seem the Wattle Club is going
to flourish like the green bay tree, but things are
not always what they seemed at first. Over two
hundred social souls are enrolled and eager to eat,
talk, read, write, 101 l or sleep there. The counter-
pane in the best bedroom is a temptation to most
women of a cream complexion to rest thereabouts.
The men visitors on Friday had a glimpse of the
boudoirs and other rooms and marvelled at their
luxuriousness. Up in the club’s tea-room certain
of the newly-blessed members talked with the
Fed. Ministers, while the mass of the gathering
crushed for its comforts in the larger hall below
stairs. Mrs. Drake, Mrs. Irvine, and Mrs. Isaacs
had their particular parties. It’s a big and ex-
pensive venture.
“The chemise comes into blushful prominence
in Madame Sans-Gene. But the blue silk and
lace amalgamation worn by Sans-Gene in the
Court apartment, and referred to as “chemise
and petticoat” is a pardonably pretty dishabille.
Only a few frail Mb 6 ribbon threads hold the spare
article to the shoulders, but in spite of extra-
ordinary wrigglings and dancing capers it remains
secure, and nothing happens.
“ A drab audience, mostly frozen about its open
strip of chest and funnybone, left all the bril-
liance of the ‘ Sans Gene ’ house to come from the
far side of the footlights. There the duchesse of
the title role glows, mostly with a sober back-
ground of court suits or brown swinging-doors, in
a summery silk, over which posies of pink play a
kind of ring-a-ring-a-rosy up to a margin of moss-
green velvet and an edge of ermine. The per-
fection of this garb, and the artistic get-up
generally, strike a note quite out of harmony with
the barbarities of the ‘ Sans Gene ’ tongue.
“There was a carriage load or two of smart
women at the Oaklands gathering on Saturday.
One style obtained—the scutty skirt, the martial
coat, and the cross-bred toque of Summer hue and
cold season stuff. Good arrangements now help
to make Moonee Valley a draw. There is, among
the marquees, a toilet tent, and there blue noses
may be powdered with impunity, and the hat that
went askew on the long way out may be jerked to
its legitimate angle. Anyone who suspects Mel-
bourne flutterers of assuming the tired feeling
should see them do a sprint to the tea tent at any
of these free afternoon race feeds, and once there
fight for their fourth and fifth dishes of tea.

Senator J. T. Walker pulled off first place
in the A.M.P. director-election, the other day
It was thought his futile career as Senator had
damaged his repute generally, but A.M.P. voters
apparently still believe in him.
A life-long abstainer was lately picked up
in a helpless state of drunkenness in Christchurch
(M.L.). It transpired in court that he had taken
a good deal of ginger wine, under the fond delu-
sion that it was a strictly temperance drink.
Died, as the result of a fall from a
camel, Drew Williams, about the best known of
S.A. prospectors. He spent practically all
his life in the Far North, and knew its mineral
country as well as any man, barring only Govern-
ment Geologist Brown, who is an easy first in that
connection.
“ Erin : John Mitchel, the Irish rebel,
in which title lie gloried, and Premier Irvine’s
uncle, in which he wouldn’t glory,
was a solicitor in early life. He
defended Gavin Duffy when prose-
cuted over an article in the Nation,
which Mitchel wrote. Then he gave
up the law for journalism. He
split off from Duffy and co. be-
cause they wouldn’t go far enough,
just as Duffy had sheered away
from O’Connell. Mitchel found-
ed the United Irishman.
Transported to V.D.L., he
escaped to San Francisco.
Strange to say, he took
up the Confederate side
in the U.S. civil war,
while Meagher fought
for the North. Two of
Mitchel’s sons fought for
the South. He helped Jeff.
Davis as a journalist. Tn
1874-s.he was twice returned
to the House of Commons for
Tipperary, but Premier Disraeli
managed to exclude him.
Mitchel died in 1875, aged 60.
Vic. Lands Minister Taver-
ner, who was a coach-driver
in his pre-Parliamentary
days, carries traces of
his old employment
in an extraordinary
shrug of the shoulders
whenever he is con-
versing or delivering
one of his jerky speeches.
This habit, generally
thought to be due to some
nerve trouble, springs from
the coach-driver’s method of
“shooing” off aggressive bush
flies when they settle on his neck
and face and his hands are engaged
with the reins. Thus Taverner is
always “shooing” away an imaginary fly,
and the fly’s name, presumably, is
Prendergast.
A political veteran, seldom nowadays heard
of, is Mr. James Garland, residing near Tatn worth,
member for the Lachlan and Lower Darling in
the first N.S.W. Responsible Parliament. He is
grand-uncle of John Garland, the present member
for Tamworth.
Lady Weld, the wife of Governor Weld
of Tasmania and Westralia, died on 9th*April, at
St. Soholastica’s Priory, Fort Augustus, Scotland.
Her name as a nun was Mother Mary Gertrude
Dolores, and she took the veil in ’99, her own
daughter being her “Superior.”
Another historic house is doomed. The
Vaucluse council requires tenders for pulling down
Clovelly, the erstwhile home of Watson Parker
(third Premier of N.S.W.), Ned Flood (a rich old
politician, who was Works Minister for 25 days),
and John Robertson (five times Premier).
Reported that King Ned
Intends to take advantage of his extreme popularity
throughout the country to ask Parliament to increase his
allowance by £30,000 a year,
That’s the worst of being affable to a king or a
clergyman ; you never know how much it is going
to cost you.
Steve Morrell, youngest of Melbourne
City Councillors, is developing into a substantial
property owner. Pubs, are his fancy, in which lie
emulates Sam Gillott, the gawd Mayor, who
owns some twenty. Morrell is a Spaniard,
brought young to Australia, and belongs to the
clarf Parer, all of whom are in the feeding busi-
ness.

When Bully Bent comes out of Cabinet
meetings nowadays and doesn’t want to say any-
thing to the waiting pressmen, he sails airily
along the corridor singing at the top of his fatty
voice. If a question is put, Thomas just wags his
nead and sings a little louder. And the fact that
i.nomas can t sing is never so painfully apparent
as when Thomas sings.

Mary Scholastica (Jane Frances Therry),
who lately died in the Benedictine convent,
Subiaco, aged 69 years, was a native of Sydney,
daughter of Roger Therry, a long ago Judge of
u P£f me Court, who retired from the Bench in
1859. The Judge published a volume of reminis-
cences, which smote some of the first families so
hard that he was asked to recall the work, and
did so as far as he was able. There are still, how-
ever, copies to be purchased at a price. Therry
came to N.S.W. with letters of introduction from
Daniel O’Connell. In 1841 he became Attorney
General, was Resident Judge at Port Phillip in
1845, and in 1846 returned to Sydney as a Judge
of the Supreme Court and primary Judge in
Equity. He died in England in 1874.

A FREE COUNTRY.

In a N.S.W. up-country township
the medical top-dog is afflicted with a periodic
drought. On the first symptom of its break-up
he invariably wires to his confreres in the next
towns thus—“Am on. (Signed) Blank.” On
receipt of this Delphic utterance both bottcm-
dogs adjourn to the pub. and join him, and the
district has to postpone its births and deaths for
at least 10 days.
* * • •
It is reported of a very rich and well-
known pastoralist that, when he first took up
land, he was stone-broke, and his whole stock was
one cow. But it was a fertile cow, and the season
was good, and the increase the first year consisted
of 13 calves and a foal.

“ Ed.” on more bush dentistry:—
I was present at a tooth extraction by a qualified
chemist. It was in a Victorian has-been gold-mining
town that died in the sixties, and the chemist and his
stock in trade had been there eversince. The patient, a
young woman, sat on the floor, her back to a wall. A
neighbor held her head. The ancient brought his relic
of the Inquisition, in the form of a corkscrew, working
through a thimhle-like cap. On the end of the corkscrew
were a pair of jaws on a swivel to clutch the tooth ; the
cap fitted on the gums. The operator got to work ; then
there was a sharp crack (the jaw-bone) and the tooth
came out, hut the patient could not close her jaws for
four weeks. Saw an old identity extract his own tooth
once. He tied the offending molar short up to an open
door, fastened a yellow handkerchief to the handle to rouse
h's fury, let out a Fenian yell, and kicked the door. He
nev_>r f It it.
“ Scotty W.” : Be affection of one animal
for another. Take the case of the celebrated man-
killing stallion, Cruiser, which was so strongly
attached to a cat. Again, Bob Smith, rabbiter,
and S. Melb. ratter, had a slut that used to camp
with the sow that had suckled her at Brookong
(1891).

“L.L.” : Near here (Mittagong, N.S.W.) is
a white gum from which, about 20 feet up, project
two branches, which, after growing divergently
for a few feet, rejoin , and continue as one branch.
I think the case is unique.
* * * *
Will Jarratt gives a gloomy (and to some
extent true) picture of the “ typical cocky ” '•
No idea of personal comfort, no thought for the con-
venience of others. When he goes on the land he dumps
his family on the nearest sand ridge, and begins to clear.
Acres, horses, seed-wheat, implements; but no work for
something commodious to eat, sleep, .and spend Sun-
day in. A dozen, counting cocky, cockyess, and offspring
often live in one stuffy, unsightly apartment. The food
isn’t the thing it could be if the woman were not required
out-of-doors. For there are thousands of wrinkles for
making a poor dish at least tasty if the house-wife is
given the time to cook. Dogs and cats and tattered hens
of no particular breed hang about with a furtive eye to
business. While it isn’t raining the domestic supply of
water is in a remote reservoir—always remote. What’s
wanted isn’t another horse, or another acre ; but 79, or
even 97 sheets of bark, and enough sun-dried bricks for
3chimnevs. Then time, alacrity, expertness, and nogrum-
bling. The same slovenly, slip-shod methods that account
for the up-ended condition of his domestic felicity cause an
appalling loss of time in the cocky’s paddock. Wrenches,
grease-pots, and tug-chains are incessantly in demand —
when in hiding. No decent place for the cocky to doss ;
no peg for Poll’s winkers. Plenty of sharp, aggressive
points to stake a horse, or dislocate an eye ; but it takes
four hours and a pitchfork to rescue a trace from the
dung-heap. If Dad hurriedly wants a shovel, one —
casually—is in the 40-acres, the other up a box-tree,
where Ponto got the ’possum. Binders, winnowers and
the like are left to split, curl, and accumulate rust where
last used. Everything put down promiscuously. His
fences also indicate what a careless, inadequate person
the cocky is. Waifs, weeds, and truants intrude without
even stepping over a rail, and debauch undisturbed till
the best part of a promising barley-patch is pillaged and
underfoot. Cocky dreads to effect repairs—can’t afford
the time--and trespassing flocks and herds drift in and
relieve him of jealously-numbered stooks. The futile
Smith’s belongings cross into the imbecile Brown’s oats,
and the latter’s poddy calves return the visit next day,
munching pensively as they go. He is too fond of multi-
plying his wheat area, adding to his string of weeds, and
neglecting to provide for the incidental downpour. Stock
at all hazards ; wheat whether or no ; water left to Pro-
vidence and Parliament 1

have met {hem mostly of another color. An
instance : Helped
with his chaff-cut-
ting one whose thin-
faced State-orphan
kiddy was absolutely
the most ill-used in-
fant I’ve encoun-
tered. That day,
when driving the
horses, the tail of the
cocky’s left-off coat
which the kiddy was
wearing got caught
in the clutch, and in
a moment he was
thrown down. Ran
out to find his arm

Boss wanted to finish
cutting, but, as I
wouldn’t hear of it,
the poor child was
grudgingly driven
four miles, in a dray,
to the doctor’s, arriv-
ing there drenched to
the skin. As a result,
influenza set in, necessitating both doctor and
apothecary, which riled the boss considerably.
Last time I saw the patient, he was minding
sheep on a sloppy road, his arm in splints, and
doing his own barking—he had no dog.

“ Bobrid ” : Curious that, while no matter
how hard you ram the earth into the entrance of
a rabbit burrow bunny can whip it out in a jiffy—
you have only to plug the hole with a bran-bag or
even a newspaper, and the tenant is “ broke up ”
in one act and looks out for another lodging.
* * * *
“Bob R.”: Despiteall the fuss about foxes,
where rabbits are plentiful they will never touch
a lamb, much less a grown sheep. The writer is
on a station where foxes are numerous and the
lambs in thousands, yet he has not known of an
authenticated case of lamb-killing by a fox. When
a lamb is found dead with its tongue eaten out the
fox is always blamed, but I believe that in most
of these cases the common crow is the criminal.
* • * *
N.S.W. Aborigines Advocate telling of
how Christian civilisation has lifted up the
blacks: —
The aboriginal race was once strictly moral. The
penalty in some tribes for adultery was death, sometimes
by burning, but now they are demoralised. We have
taken from them, or laughed them out of, their old laws
and ceremonies and given them nothing but vices in
return, consequently their last state is worse than the
first.

“J.A.F.”: Be goat-meat. I was, some
years ago, at a cookie’s place where a parson was
doing a wholesale christening. The old cocky
proudly ushered the “ meenister ” and myself into
the sitting-room for dinner, the dish being goat’s
head, au naturel —horns, teeth, eyes, and all. The
skin only had been taken off, and most likely a
good tuft of beard, too ! We passed.

“Gumleaf” on a phase of “local in-
dustry ”:
Saw two queer “ bottle-o ” sights in a Melbourne suburb
t’other day. A very fat man sat slumbering in a ram-
shackle cart, while his scraggy horse leaned his head
against the kerbstone to keep himself steady. Two little
children, ragged and barefooted, aged about 6 and 7
years, dodged in and out of the backyards, piping
“ Bottle-o ! ” and calling bo each other to know whether
“ yer wukked Mrs. ’Arris or Mrs. Jones.” The kids were
evidently devilling for their sleek, sleeping father in the
cart. Round the pext corner a wild-eyed, hairy man
jogged along, harnessed between the shafts of a heavy
hand-cart. The man was happy and contented. He had
a child’s toy whip, which he kept on cracking, and
shouting out sternly, ,f Get hup ’osses.” The cart was
piled up with bottles, and the man was tossed about
between the shafts as the load rattled over the rough
stones. Inquiries showed that the man with the hand-
cart heavily loaded was a simple lunatic, who fancied he
was driving horses the moment a whip was put into his
hands. A shrewd individual had got hold of him, and
found the illusion was even stronger if the man was
attached to a hand-cart. Hence he used him as a moke
to cart around his bottles. The illusion was so strong
with the lunatic that he would not rest a moment, and
when the journey was not straight ahead he would trot
the imaginary horses up and down the street, like the
coachman of “ me lord’s carriage,” so that the animals
might not get stiff.

“B. Belle ” : Molasses again. At a sugar
mill, the other day, some Kanakas (who were
afflicted with a skin disease) were sent into some
molasses tanks to clean same out. Three days
later skin disease disappeared. Don’t know any-
thing further about the molasses.

was recently much mystified by repeated disap-
pearances of chickens, but was enlightened on
discovering a horse, kept in the yard with the
fowls, engaged in “scoffing” the last of a clutch
of eight—feathers and all. Fact.

“ Grotsey ” : Surely a record “shout.”
When Clermont was booming, the publicans raked
in the shekels hand-over-fist. One favorite Bung
never took less than £3O a night for drinks—
sometimes more. After 12 months or so of this
he visited Sydney. While he was there he looked
over a German warship then in harbour and spent
£7OO in shouting champagne for all hands on
board. The man is now composing music for a
bare living. Fact.

“Jaf.”: The N.S.W. cocky is always
grumbling about rabbits and asking Govt, to do
“something” to keep the pest down; yet, as
soon as a trapper asks leave to trap on his farm,
the cocky demands “royalty”—so much a week

* * * *
How Monday “ fell in ” ;
“ Monday " was our fighting champion. He was a naif-
caste who had been on the station for years, and had
been taught to box. One afternoon a hawker arrived at
the homestead ; and, after tea, began business, but lie
had hardly opened his boxes before he had a dispute
with Monday, and the fight was soon arranged. The
moonlight was but faint, and some trees made it still
gloomier, so that the men could only just about see one
another. Monday crept forward, bending and crouchii.g.
The hawker gave one or two light, springy hops, and the
next thing we heard was a tremendous thud, and
Monday was lying on his back. The steam was knocked
completely out of him. “My word,” he spluttered,
“him fight diff’rent way altogether. No more fight
Monday.” Next morning I helped the hawker in with
his horses. The fight was still puzzling me. “ I’m
blowed if I could see how you did it, mate,” I remarked.
“ All I could see was a whirl of arms an’ legs.” “Yes,”
he replied, with a wink, “ a couple of years of acrobating
in a circus is a good training for that game. Teaches you
to turn somersaults, you know, and to get home with
your feet as well as your hands.” Then the whole business
was as clear as daylight.

“Ursus” : In some British New Guinea
back villages I have seen native lads smeared all
over with soot and wearing broad green leaves
behind, only below the waist—nude everywhere
else. Asked the late Rev. Chambers about this
custom, but got no satisfactory explanation. Any
Bulletin reader know anything about this ?

“THE ONE THING NEEDFUL.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By the flukes of Jonah’s whale, by the spikes on Satan’s
tail,
By the hinges on the Seven Gates of Hell,
I am sick and tired of wearing out my soul in useless
swearing,
For I’ve realised that cursing is a sell.
Yes, the dull old plain profanity is weariness and vanity
I want One Word, the sound of which shall be
A trumpet and a sword, not a useless tinsel gaud,
But an adequate, complete epitome.
A word that will combine all the swears for which I
pine ;
One crashing curse that thunders like the sea;
All the wrongs a man may feel metamorphosed into
steel.
And welded into one great blasphemy.
In itself it should unite all the venom and the spite
Of every curse th it e’er was uttered j et;
And should concentrate in one all the oaths beneath the
sun,
Since face to face with Fate strong men have met.
Then a man could rend his fetters with the magic of the
letters
That composed that splendid star of observation—
All the sulphurous universe of anathema and curse
Would circle round that sunlike objurgation.
I am deadly tired of “Blast!” and the charms of
“ Damn ! ! ” are past;
Such futile words no longer move my soul;
And the joys of life have faded, and my weary brain is
jaded,
And I yearn for that One Word which is The Whole.
PONEMAH.

Alba : I was told that he lived about
midway between Glen Innes and Inverell, and
t n Cal^ y> 1 left the coa “ l ‘ th 4 half-
way pub., followed a track along the ridge until
I sighted smoke rising above the pines, and pre-
sently came upon a tent with a kind of gunvah
parlor at the back. He was sitting cross legged
W 3 ™ a ? e3Smg P aper , a to subscribers.
He eyed me suspiciously, but when I pulled out
tobacco he invited me inside. The tent was
simply packed with old Bulletins. He slept on
Bulletins, and his table and bed were supported
on Bulletins. After some talk, he told me in a
cautious whisper that he was Turramutta Bill
editor and sole proprietor of The Bulletin’
(How he has hitherto escaped the “ Aboriginali-
ties columni, by the way, is a mystery.) His
printing machine is a marvel of cranky ingenuity
At first, writer mistook it for a butcher’s
gallows, but investigation showed that the
cross-beam was a mass of wooden type-about
a column of • set-up” aboriginal words A
groove in the side of the gallows allowed
the beam-like press to come down upon a flat
slab of cedar supported by a dozen kerosene-tins
f, o l . qi i I H, d v Casua l ly after tl,e health of the staff.
Staff he said “ Why, Hop’s just gone down
th ( gully to git a bear. 'He gits lots o’ bears, an’
he s all right with a boonferang; but, between you
an in©, be can t bile a billy. Look at his drorin 99
—and he pointed out various charcoal smudges on
the face of the cedar slab. While waiting for Hop
to come along with the bear, I discovered that the
crank s tent was the meeting-place of the literary
bullocky and the poetic swagman ; indeed I soon
found that an untorn Bulletin may be readily
swapped for tobacco, bread, damper, tea, or
sugar, and that editor Turramutta Bill had sense
enough to know the value of his back-numbers.
On the way back I met “ Hop,” who proved to be
an aboriginal with a fine literary manner, but with
a somewhat too pressing desire for rum or beer.
He carried a sugar-bag, but no bear. That night,
while dining at the pub., I was startled by a
terrific din in the direction of Bill’s camp, as
though half-a-dozen elephants were caracoling in
a wilderness of loose milk-cans. The publican
however, reassured me with the information that
it was only Turramutta Bill “going to press.”

“ Taparura ” to The Bulletin :
New Guinea is the cradle of the Melanesian races— the
fathers of lying, thieving, cadging, treachery and dis-
honesty live here in +
Papua, and their
children throughout
Melanesia can’t hold
a candle to them.
The missionaries
teach their pupils to
distrust the ordinary
white man because

“ Percol ” ! Some years ago a “ religious
Chautauqua” was instituted in Victoria, and held
its first two annual meetings in the Grampians.
The thihg, however, soon fell through, and now
the only trace of the movement is at a very pretty
water-spot where a little creek flutters down a
rugged precipice. On the most prominent rock
is painted, in huge, staring capitals : “ Whosoever
drinketh of these waters shall thirst again, but he
that drinketh of the Waters of Life shall never
thirst.” The whole picturesque effect of this
beautiful spot is spoilt; but then, to disfigure the
handiwork of God appears to be part of the reli-
gion of to-day.
“Trier”: On a way-back road recently
I met a swaggie pushing a wheelbarrow contain-
ing his belongings, and having on one side a kind
of outrigger, to which was harnessed a fine collie.
The canine was tugging away in great style, and
thus materially assisting the “ firm’s ” progress.

Any Bulletin reader ever find a cob of
corn containing an odd number of rows of grains ?
Cockies in the know will bet on evens all the year
round. —W.
A Vic. agricultural expert was lately
lecturing in the country, and was met at an. out-
efAcc-way place at early morn by a young fellow
who had to drive him some distance. They first
went to the local pub for refreshment. Asked
what he would have, Bushy bashfully remarked
that he would take the same as the expert, who
called for sherry and bitters. He oarefully
watohed his host sprinkle the bitters in his glass,
empty the stuff out, and fill up with sherry ; but
unfortunately reversed the process himself. “My
blanky oath,” he exclaimed after he had gulped
down a glassful of angostura, “ yous foreign
blokes do drink some funny stuff; but I s’pose
you get pretty used to it.”
Tho street preacher was enlarging on the
iniquity of excluding the harmless necessary
oigger. “Will the black man be shut out of
Heaven because of his color ? ” he shouted.
No ! black or white, yellow or dusky, they all
go straight in.” The man who didn’t seem to be
listening said, “They’ll have to die first,” and
said nothing more.
A picture of female life in Freetrade
England (from the Daily Mail)
Not only do women forge chains in Cradley Heath, but
they also make bricks. Any man who has ever done
laboring work will know that brick-making is in some
respects harder work than navvying. There is the inces-
sant, monotonous stooping and lifting.
It was pitiful to watch these women. In fact, to tel]
the truth, the poor things did not look like women at all.
The heavy labor had taken the woman-shape from their
bodies. The softness of the sex had gone from them.
They worked in silence. They did not sing as the
women sang when they were forging the chains. And
their faces looked different from the faces of the women
workers in iron. The chain women had not the look of
masculinity that these women had. The work at* the
forge was more interesting and, of the two, less hurtful
to them as women.
As one saw them laying the bricks in silence in the
monotonous rows, one thought of the heavy pall of smoke
that lay over Cradley Heath—the pall seen from the
distance. One thought of the feeling that came to the
mind of there being something sinister behind the thick,
heavy, distant cloud. And surely there was something
sinister. It was here before the eyes—here in this town
of the leaden light.

About an Adelaide identity :
One long streak of humanity, Percy \V. Berry, book-
seller, and son of Rev. Joe Berry, of Adelaide, has
inherited all the eccentric nature of his dad. He it was
who ostentatiously refused to put on sale Paris Nesbit’s
now defunct Morning, on the grounds of its blasphemy.
Also, when the S.A. Early Closing Act came into forcej
at the end of 1900, he was the man who sent round copies
of this memorial card to his customers: “On the Ist
January, 1901, suddenly, at “Pennsylvania,” Allen’s
Corner, Kent Town, from acute early-closing disease
induced by an unreasonable Act of Parliament, after d
o’clock, the valued trade of the proprietor, aged 4 years
Deeply to be regretted.” In his shop window he pasted :
“ This establishment will be closed every evening at t!
o’clock, and on Wednesdays at 1 o’clock, until Parliament
learns to mind its own business and permits me to mind
mine,” or something very similar. Percy’s business didn’t
die, but his utter failure as a prophet hasn’t discouraged
him in the least.

communications insufliciently stamped. Aame and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-

State) accompany.

“N.”: A new book, named “ Lumsden’s
Horse,” concerns Don Quixote and 250 other
Quixotes. When the news of the Magersfontdlfc
smash came through, Lumsden, a wealthy Indial[ '
planter, was staying at the Melbourne Club. He
slapped his tist on the mahogany, and declared it
should not be. .He immediately wired to his
friend Playfair, in Calcutta, an offer of 50,000
rupees towards fitting out a regiment of British
residents in India, as volunteers for S’Africa.
The corps of 250 was formed, at a cost of £19,500
sterling. The fellows were all swells, and yet
went for ordinary British cavalry pay, beginning
at Is. 2d. a day for privates. After nine months’
service, they were dead tired of patriotism and
disbanded.

After a life of 22 years, the Orient mail
liner .Austral is to be broken up. On Nov. 11,
1882, she sank at her mooring’s in Neutral Bay',
the result of carelessly leaving certain valves
open, one life being sacrificed.

perience of millions of people in all quarters of

oontury has confirmed the value of Hunyadi Janos
as the best and safest natural aperient. Invalu-

My Melbourne correspondent continues :
“ Now that Justice Williams has retired, Holroyd
.T. is senior puisne judge. His grandfather, Sir
George Holroyd was an English judge. Of him
an old law magazine relates that when His Honor
was a young barrister, circuit chums dubbed him
‘ Selig Holroyd ’ because a Welsh woman, worried
by his cross-examination, demanded ‘Young
man, why do ye ask the likes o’ they selig (f.e.
silly) questions?’
“ Dr. Morrison, lately-deceased, who wore M.A.
and LL.D. after his name, won his spurs as a
schoolmaster, less, perhaps, by scholastic brilliance
than by tireless energy, organising skill, dauntless
courage in taking arms (if need be) against a sea
of boys, and also by his handsome appearance and
unfailing supply of social tact and charm in deal-
ing with parents. lie his ‘pluck,’ old boys still
remember how the ‘ bully boarder,’ a Herculean
lout of 20, defied * Old Morrison ’ and went to the
theatre against orders. The Doctor was having a
chat with a orony or two in his study when
he heard the mutineer come in. Instead of
hurrying upstairs to confront him, he placidly
finished off his little party and then read for a bit.
By this time, the big boy upstairs had branded
him as ‘frightened to tackle him,’ and was dozing
comfortably in bed. Suddenly he woke with a
start. Someone (the other boys, he supposed) was
flailing him through the bed-clothes like an insane
thrashing-machine. He roared—‘ Stop that! you
adjective fools; What are yer doin’?’ ‘l’m
flaggin’ye. Thats what I’m doin’!’ explained a
masterful Scotch voice, whose owner then went on
to flail harder than ever. At this a squeak of gig-
gles came from the surrounding beds, and for the
rest of the fray (for there was a fray) the rebel was
hampered by the fact that his assailant had turned
the laugh against him, not to speak of other disad-
vantages, such as inconveniently light costume
and the other man’s supple cane.
“ The she-fencers who are fascinated with the
foils had better guard against developing the
excessive and over-done suppleness of Norman
Partridge, whose swinging gait and loose-legged-
ness as Orlando in ‘As You Like It’ are attri-
buted to redundant sword-exercise. Fencing
is, like many other pleasant things, good in small
quantities. An over-dose of sword-drill spoils.
“The small snap-shotting fraternity, who charge
little more than a thanks a dozen, have Melbourne
literally by the no3e. The three most popular
attics are crowded from dawn to dark. You
penetrate dust, grit, stage-tinsel, broken-down
woodland scenes and Athenian backgrounds of a
dilapidated character, to get to the office (?).
There you get your number, and invoke the
suffering smile that is the special concomitant of
the camera, in a dusky hole that has a cracked
scrap of reflecting glass in a patch of the wall.
If you take your head-gear off, you place it upside
down on the one and only crazy chair—for obvious
reasons. When your number is called, you hustle
through the waiting smilers to the studio. That
studio defies a nib sketch. Out of the debris —
the shawls, boxes, boots, masks, candle-ends, tags
and banana-peels—you pick the camera’s eye
and cling to it. An unshaven machinist pulls
the trigger four times and you are hustled off.
When you give your mite and seize your dozen
effigies later on, you are agape with incredulity.
There glows your face—minus its freckles, black-
heads and wrinkles —encased in a Florentine
mount, or fronting a scene as gorgeous as a
Giorgione canvas, or peeping from a Spanish
garden hinting at rare delights. And that is
why all the smart folks this side crowd to the
cheap attics in Bourke and Collins streets.
“Melbourne women have followed the men in
the fashion of champagne-bottle sleeves. You
come across fat and short, long and lean, all with
a long neck slope Similarly, the toilettes take
en masse to the long-haired cloths. It is a Winter
of monotonous dressing and feminine discontent.
A few of the unhappy try to get away in smooth-
face materials, but to be in the fashion your dress
must be cat-haired. The plaids and tartans are
now a slump in the market. The melancholy
grey remains in absolute power, and colors are
a violent break away, only authorised by uncom-
mon good looks. The ordinary smart woman
must peg along in grey.
“ A seedy individual, in draughty pants, comes
now knocking at the suburban door with the
request, ‘Want yer ’and or yer ’ead read, ma’am?’
Since the palm romancers and the seers have been
switched off the city parlors, random cranks have
taken to the calling, and claim to lift the curtain
from Shadowland at an absurdly low figure.
' ‘ Marshall Hall describes Mark Hambourg as
‘an artist of altogether extraordinary powers,
who probably has hardly his equal in the world,’
and goes on to say that Mark’s nervous, original,
passionate temperament colors all he touches.
As a musician, the Hambourg of nowadays must
sometimes smile at his old self, the gifted but
exaggerated, unequal and sensational performer.
All the same, when people go out to enjoy them-
selves, they prefer a bill, not given up exclusively
to bric-a-brac. Mark’s own personality supplies
all the rococo an audience can assimilate, so it’s a
pity the rest of the concert fixtures did not
include less bare, crude ugliness. What with the
arctic cold of the Town Hall, the forlorn untidi-
ness of the platform, the hideous attire of sweet-
voiced little soprano Bachelor, and the blue
noses of suburban female hordes, it’s not sur-
prising that daily-paper puffsters had to gush
their sweetest to save the Hambourg season from
being a frost.
“Touching the ugliness ot Melbourne Eight
Hours’ Memorial The most absurdly hideous
specimen of memorial stone work in Melbourne is,
perhaps, the fountain erected at a breezy point of
the broad roadway in Elizabeth-street North.
The inscription on this public outrage state-s that
it is intended to immortalise the Duke of York’s
visit—and it looks the character. Nothing less
absurd could be expected of the person, or persons,
who seized the occasion of the opening of the
tirst Australian Parliament as an excuse for
reminding posterity of the little incidental duke
from England.
“One Federal dame watches the moods of her
spasmodic spouse narrowly, and always weighs
his utterances in the scales of her attested wis-
dom. Sometimes lie slips, verbally or politically,
and she is sorely put out. Anon he givesstrength
and volume to his pipings, and then she turns
proudly to the nearest Federal brother with
‘lsn’t John in great form to-night?’ It’s naive,
and the forlorn member, mayhap, envies the said
John ; but the women snort and wonder how an
enlightened member of the tempting sex can
pander so to her p trtner. Another clover dame,
whose Federal spouse is somewhat of the dull
Dogberry nature, never plays prompter or reviser
in public. She does all the worry, and tables all
her suggestions in their own little back Blooms-
bury, and sails into the galleries with an ostenta-
tious air of good-humor and want of interest.
The energies of Mrs. John Forrest are sought
by friends this side, but, so far, the Western dame
does not rush in with amusement plans. Those
croquet parties, and the subsequent troubles as to
t.ie exact ohalk-lining of the Ministerial and
Opposition mesdames in social corroborees, are
still a sore remembrance.
“An aristocratic affectation of the hour is a
limp of some kind—mostly with a cane accom-
paniment. In a leading hostelry lounge, the
other day, limping parties were the vogue. Two
mummers arrived for the tea corroboree in the
dot-and-carry-one-fashion. A smart dame fol-
lowed, leaning on a sturdy stick, and then a Lydia
oocurred, taking slow, languid steps with the aid
of a cane. After this procession of the lame and
halt, the ordinary sound skirt-whisker lost interest
m one s eyes, and the limping contingent with the
canes by their sides, seemed to own a novel
attraction. That’s how the craze starts, I hear.
The playful stick takes the fancy, and the handy
sprain ensues. The long hotel corridors provide
a favorable, promenade for the limpers, and when
a pensive air is worked up, the result is alleged to
be disturbing to the susceptible onlookers.
“ What hundreds London is calling ! Happen-
ing down at Port Melbourne the other day, was
struck at the sight of one of the tremendous
White Star liners, with decks crowded to the
gangway, just about to steam off. Several
hundreds were on board. The crowd was a mixed
one. Here and there artisan groups, clean,
strong, happy. Yonder, a batch of mud-com-
plexioned women whose pinchings for this trip
showed as traces of impoverishment in their faces;
then brusque business men, faded actresses, and
rovers, and a variety of seedy couples.
“ The newest scent device is secreted in the
fancy, hat-skewer. It rather serves a suitable pur-
pose in, say, a violet essence where millinery runs
in the direction of that flower. But one sees
danger ahead. Attar of roses will give out its
fragrant breath from a hat-plot of cornflowers or
decaying foliage. The thing will be to match your
hat-skewer scent to. your head appointments, and
not associate the like of myrrh with unsuitable
millinery.
“Allan and Co. have published, in large type,
a Manual of Scales and Arpeggios, by Mr. Frank-
lin Peterson, the convalescent professor of Melb.
University Conservatorium. A well-arranged
educational work. The scales in contrary motion,
with fingering, should be very useful, " and the
beginning on third note of scale is a good idea, as
this is a very frequent progression in classical
works. Another book for pianoforte students,
published as Allan’s Edition, contains 40 practical
exercises, going on Germer’s principles for double
notes, stretching, &c. H. W. Cambridge is
responsible.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.
“ Polstan ” on the relative value of
things :
Many are the uses of the hairpin of woman, and next
to it in value comes the long, sharp hatpin. This is
mainly used in the aggressive. One of the Melbourne
Scottish Regiment recently had about three inches of
blue hatpin rammed into his calf as he stood at attention
on a crowded rbadway. He immediately formed himself
into a hollow square. On another occasion a trooper who
had been assisting to keep a crowd in order found a 10-
inch pin up to the hilt in his horse’s haunch when he was
rubbing the poor beast down after the parade. It didn’t
matter about the Scotchman, but to injure the horse was
a deed of beastly, unnecessary cruelty.
The black-coated brigade is always more
or less up in arms against the “ nakked wumman
that men hug a’ th’ nicht in a ballroom.” An
Australian thumper recently declared that half
the social evil in his district is due to “figure
dancing.” “That style of dancing,” he explained,
“is the one in which a man, any man, a pairfeet
stranger even, clasps the waist of a married or un-
married wumman whose upper body is covered
by two shoulder-straps only, and verra often the
man lays his cheek on the wumman’s bare shoulder,
as they go footing it round the room like a pair o’
trussed fowls. What else but sin—dawmnable
sin—can come from such a shameless proceeding ’ ”
W. N. Willis, M.L.A., as a land agent
needs no black-tracker to guide him through the
devious paths of the Land Acts. He fights all
his oases, and the harder the task the better he
like it, and wins all the time. 21 Bligh-street.*
Another instance of lovely woman’s daunt-
less spirit where matrimony is at stake. A
Ballarat bride-elect was dancing at a ball given in
honor of her impending alliance. During a square
dance the heroine became giddy—says the local
reporter—and “fell with great violence to the
floor, receiving severe cuts to her face in the
region of the eye, besides painful abrasions to her
head, also shock to the system.” The marriage
being due next day, the ball broke up under the
impression that a postponement would be neces-
sary to permit the lady to recover from her
one shock, fifteen outs and abrasions, and her
black eye. But her other eye took in the situation,
and in a partially-repaired and fully-decorated
state she came up smiling to the altar. She didn’t
turn giddy and fall when the parson said “ Wilt
thou take this man ? ” She just took him ‘‘ in the
presence of a large number of friends.”
According to Dr. Armstrong, Sydney
City Health Officer, it is rather the excep-
tion than the rule for the 40 odd munici-
palities of Sydney to enforce their own by-laws in
respect to cleanliness, and when the council’s
inspector does prosecute for a more than ordi-
narily glaring case of dirtiness, as often as not he
is compelled to withdraw the prosecution at the
instance of interested mayors or aldermen. In 38
out of 48 municipalities in and around Sydney the
sanitary inspector is also council clerk, or gutter-
sweeper, or office boy, or something ; and so he is
really no sanitary inspector at all for all practical
purposes ; and in but three out of those 48 dirty
boroughs are disinfecting operations attempted at
all. The result of this beautiful state of affairs is,
of course, typhoid. The huge municipality of
Balmain, with a population of over 30,000, lias
but one sanitary inspector to see that it keeps
itself clean ; and it uses its park as a tip (as a rule
the park is used as a tip, just as an alderman, if
presented with a priceless work of art, would
naturally keep it in the pigsty). Balmain, with
but six per cent, of the metropolitan population,
has twenty-two per cent, of the total typhoid cases
of the metropolis ! When is that Greater Sydney
scheme coming along, to consign the dirty-minded
local alderman to his well-earned oblivion in tb*
public ash-barrel ?
The East-Australian fashion of using the
revolver as a means of removing an enemy or a best
girl, is matched in Groperland by the growing
taste for dynamite. It is not very long since a
parcel, received through the post by a Perth man,
blew the recipient’s arm off on delivery. And
now there comes the tale of a lady who was sent
upwards by the same means. The detectives
affirm that a short piece of fuse was attached to
a dynamite percussion-cap and placed in a piece
of firewood, with the result that, when the lady
was entertaining a friend at tea beside her own
grate, the fire blew up violently, seriously injuring
the hostess, and removing the whole of the chim-
ney and the best part of the room. The police
have arrested the mother of a girl with whom the
injured woman’s husband had once “kept com-
pany,” before marrying his present wife. It is to
be hoped she, and her sex generally, will be
cleared of the charge. Woman was comparatively
safe while she only used a gun; she seldom hit
anything with that, except a spectator occa-
sionally. With dynamite or a hat-pin she is
about as dangerous as a man.
Sydney Freeman’s Journal publishes a
fantastic sketch of the British Empire in the year
2000. The King “abnegates” his throne, and
“ dismisses ” the few remaining barons who refuse
to resign their privileges and act up to the spirit
of the times. “The people throng the streets,
small factions walking gaily along singing the
well-known Hymn of Liberty, which had been
composed some years before in honor of the Peace
Movement.” Even when all is well, and every-
body is happy, there are still factions. It is an
Irish need. Erin’s land laws are “copied in
every portion of the habitable world.” The
Dean of Cashel, addressing a monster meet-
ing in Phoenix Park, says:—“The people in
their ways of paying homage to God, are as
tolerant and unwise {sic) as their constitution.”
The population of Ireland has increased to
40,000,000. The leader of the Irish Parliament is
named Dwyer O’Dwyer, and he announces that
the Treasury has a surplus of £10,000,000.
Immediately under this gorgeous forecast appears
the statement— “In Salthouse, Norfolk, a duck
has been hatched with four legs and two tails.”
Alleged by Dr. Brockler, of Vienna, who
has devoted much time to investigating the sub-
ject, that European vegetarians never have great-
great-grandchildren, and tend to lose their own
sight, courage and hair early in life.
From the Wesley Church Signal
(l/6/’O3) eu
“ Matilda” is leaving Sydney, returning to Fiji. She
is a sweet, good-tempered girl. The Sunday School
children love “ Matilda.” Mrs. Lindsay and family will
miss her very much. Her training reflects great credit
on Mrs. Lindsay. Several presents were made to
“ Matilda.” The Young Men’s Institute could not refrain
from showing their love to her by giving a present.
If she were a white girl the Wesley paper would
call her Miss Matilda Smith, or, at the very least,
Matilda. Being a brown girl, she is “ Matilda,”
in inverted commas, as if she were a bear at the
Zoo. How these holy people love to patronise
their “ pore colored sister ”!
Quaint item from a North of Scotland
paper about how the descendants of the old
Vikings live in Shetland :
Sheriff Shennan has just given his decision in a breach
of promise case which was raised in the Lerwick Sheriff
Court some time ago at the instance of a young woman
named Robina Georgeson, residing at Melby, Sandness,
against a sailor named Walterson, residing at the same
place. The damages were laid at £lOO. The Sheriff finds
that there is a custom prevailing to some extent in
Shetland, and known as Shetland courtship, in pursuance
of which young men during winter visit their sweethearts
at night, and frequently sleep with them without un-
dressing. He also finds that, from October, 1890, to
April, 1891, the defender, in accordance with this
custom, visited the pursuer, and slept with her ; that in
April, 1891, the defender went south as a sailor, and
thenceforward corresponded with the pursuer till his
return to Shetland at the end of December, 1893 ; that
between October, 1890, and December, 1893, the defender
courted the pursuer, but the pursuer has failed to prove
that he promised to marry her. . . . Mr. Robertson,
solicitor, in addressing the Sheriff on behalf of the de-
fender, is reported by the Shetland Times to have said
in the course of his remarks that the custom of Shetland
courtship was to go away and sleep together two or three
nights a-week. Defender said he never dreamed of such
a thing or thought of such a thing as marriage until he
got the letter. This was followed by another letter, which
contained a most startling statement. Referring further
to the evidence, he said pursuer denied that she had s'ept
with John Cheyne, and she did not remember if he had
promised to marry her, but Kate Campbell, in the wit-
ness box, stated that John Cheyne courted her, and
slept with her while she was in Melby. After that, when
pursuer’s evidence was taken in chambers, she admitted
having slept with John Cheyne, but she said she did so
because he was her cousin. He submitted that so far as
the parish of Sandness was concerned, this practice of
young people sleeping together was just a winter amuse-
ment. Young men came home from sea and looked for a
girl to spend the winter with, and to get the news of the
parish they did what was called sleeping together.

“We must use the Federal telescope as
well as the State microscope.”— Attorney-General
Deakin.
The Lewis Ministry gave Tasmania one-
man-one-vote, and Tasmania wiped it out at the
first opportunity. Which was exactly Dibbs’s
experience in N.S.W.
It is said that 200 Victorian ex-strikers
(engine-drivers and firemen) have got billets in
other countries, and are departing immediately.
Alas for Australia, that it should be drained of such
good blood !
“ X. 0.” writes :
If, as alleged, the N.S.W. Labor movement sinks the
fiscal issue, how comes it that Sydney Worker, its official
organ, is, to all intents and purposes, an importers’ jour-
nal ? Whenever that paper touches the fiscal question,
which it does frequently, the importing interest is
boosted as much as possible.
Premier Seddon justifies his title of “the
frozen-meat patriot ” by announcing that if
Britain can’t arrange preferential trade with M.L.,
then M.L. will have to make trade treaties with
foreign countries. His allegiance, so to speak,
goes with his mutton.

Melbourne Tocsin has been publishing the
pedigrees and past histories of Bent’s black beg
pardon, loyalists—and has thereby roused the ire
of certain individuals who urged the Minister to
hold an inquiry as to how the intelligence leaked
out. The Tocsin will probably be “ suppressed ”
when the Irvine gang meets again.
S.A. Parliament usually meets early in
June (this custom has been almost a fixture for 22
years), but Opposition-Leader and virtual Premier
Darling is away in England, and he seems to have
issued orders to his minion, Jenkins Iscariot, to
wait till he comes back. Consequently Jenkins
will not meet his faithful followers in a strong
American accent till July.
Those politicians who are babbling about
the “cost” of the Federal High Court, are hereby
invited to consider that the abolition of the useless
State Governors would cover the cost twice over.
The High Court has certain duties to perform
which no other institution can do. The State
Governor chiefly opens bridges which would open
naturally without his assistance.
Senator Higgs has been the first this ses-
sion to break away from the Labor party’s attitude
of ladylike complaisance, with a notice of motion
condemning Vic. Gov. Clarke’s meddling with
Australian politics by advocating the naval sub-
sidy proposals. This action is, of course, terribly
offensive to the “best circles,” but it is un-
doubtedly necessary, and courageous withal.
The cables that appear in the Sydney
morning papers would, as a rule, create the
impression that Britain has risen as one man
against Chamberlain’s Protectionist proposals.
Yet things leak out occasionally even in their
columns. The other day they recklessly admitted
that, in the Midland Counties of England alone,
1000 manufacturers had joined anew Protectionist
league.
The latest N.S.W. Public Service sensa-
tion is in connection with a messenger. „The
messengers are entitled, of course, to ‘ ‘ run ” the
public offices, but this particular one seems to
have gone beyond even the high privileges and
perquisites of a messenger. Probable that the
matter will be hushed up. A messenger is a
messenger, and it would be almost unconstitu-
tional to punish him.
Human flesh is not the only thing that is
grass. Announced last week that the British
Government had x'efused to purchase the Spanish
cable, as no one could foretell to what extent
ethergrams would presently supersede cables. If
cables snuff out, land communication by wire will
not long survive them. Then where will be
another £4,000,000 worth of our “ permanent and
reproductive ” loan works ?
The Irvine-Bent gang is still dealing out
their “justice” to the Vic. railway drivers and
firemen. Several who desire to leave the service
for foreign parts cannot get their “clearances”
from the Government. One man in particular,
having on offer a lucrative position in S’Africa,
has been on his knees (d la Peacock) for weeks
past, in a hitherto vain attempt to obtain his
clearance and insurance policy.
A good motion before the House of Reps,
stands in the name of Mr. Wilkinson —suggesting
a bonus of a half-penny per lb. on “ginned”
cotton grown and ginned in the Commonwealth,
and £2500 to the person first producing cotton
goods of the value of £5OOO from cotton grown in
Australia. There is room in the Commonwealth
for a great cotton industry ; and a bonus, with a
high tariff, is the way to create it.
Queensland Premier Philp, during his
recent tour up as far as the Gulf country, only
once addressed a public meeting of the electors
and that was at his stronghold, Townsville ; and
even there, despite favors past and to come, his
reception was painfully mixed. He returned say-
ing the country was obviously with him. In
many places the country showed rather an incli-
nation to rise up and fall on top of him.
Members of the Australian Parliament do
not seem to take the trouble to study the Aus-
tralian Constitution. It has been repeatedly
urged during the debate on the Judicature Bill
that “ the State Courts with the Privy Council
would serve all necessary purposes for awhile.”
But the Australian Constitution prohibits appeals
to the Privy Council —except by leave of the
Federal High Court—on Constitutional matters.
The Northern J.P. always makes things
as easy as possible for offenders against the blacks.
In the case of one Ah Duck, charged with supply-
ing liquor to a female native, the Bench
discharged the accused, on the ground that
they believed the woman did actually receive
liquor from Ah Duck, but then they believed
she got grog somewhere else also. And these
be the J’s.P. by the grace of the Philpocracy.
There seems to be ground for the sus-
picion that Raven Shiels is at the bottom of
the Icicle Government’s swindling of its em-
ployees. Just before the strike Bent repeatedly
promised the immediate payment of the long
overdue increments to those getting less than
£125 a year. Irvine also said he was surprised
to hear they had not been paid—and promised
prompt redress. T’other day the Age asked
the Commissioners for a little light on this
very shady matter, and Fitzpatrick frankly
replied ‘‘ he was ready to pay; he was only
awaiting the Minister’s authority.” As the
financial year closes this month it looks as
if Shiels was doing another mean trick to
make his deficit smaller by the few pounds
thus saved.
Mr. Yes-No Reid is now in favor of pre-
ferential treatment of British goods* ‘if the present
tariff is maintained.” In 1897, as reported by
London Daily News, Mr. Reid said :
As to the various proposals for an Imperial Zollverein
and preferential duties in favor of British goods, he con-
demned them outright. Canada might do as she pleased,
but in his opinion the British Empire was better main-
tained by its present policy of Freetrade, even with
enemies, than it could be in any other way.
The Russianising of Finland is said to be
proceeding apace. So is the Russianising of Vic-
toria. A policeman at Ballarat is being hauled
over the coals by his superior officers for being
present in plain clothes, in his own time, and as a
private individual, at a meeting addressed by
Tom Mann. In Victoria very soon nobody will
have any liberty at all except Bent. And he won’t
have any liberty—only license to be brutally
objectionable.
John Haynes, M.L.A., proposes to settle
people on the land in N.S.W. by “drastic reduc-
tions in railway fares.” Bujt the people witli free
railway passes don’t settle on the land. Some of
them don’t even settle their bills. And if people
have to be paid to go on the land by being carried
to and fro largely at the cost of the city worker,
in addition to having their produce carried (as at
present) at one-fourth the rate charged to the
manufacturer, doesn’t the backbone of the country
begin to look very like a miserable pauperised
soup-ticket ? Also, any good bit of land made
available is already, applied for ten times over,
and the reduction of railway fares won’t make
land.

Bent it was in some other chap s department.

I remarked quietly that I had been waiting outside Bent

affluent sponger on the public purse was a man
managed a whole branch department for £o a •
Also, I informed him that, up in the Lands Department,

bably be a clerk on £176 a year, minus percentage reduc-
