After all the alleged economy which was
to result from Bent’s cutting down of the Vic-
torian railway-passenger service, the Age laments
that there is, after all, “ practically no saving.
There are still 63 trains a day to St. Kilda. On the
Sandringham line (which loses £9OO a week) there
are 23 trains a day instead of the former 24 ; and
so on. The only real difference is that the
are more erratic in their time-keeping, and the
engine breaks down six times instead of once.
Already the Freetrade Tories are smelling
round to see whether it is any.use nominating a
candidate against Trades Minister Kingston tor
Adelaide district at the Federal elections in
December. But it is pretty certain that before
nomination day comes they will discover
that the man to beat “ Charley ” has yet to be
born. Kingston is a native of the constituency
for which he intends to stand, and for 20 years he
represented a part of it in the State Parliament.
Senator Staniforth Smith contributes to
Sydney Telegkaph a valuable article on Papuan
conditions. He condemns the absurdity of
allowing big grants to land-grab co’s., plumps for
a leasehold system of land-tenures, and shows
that to be the only way to a steady and healthy
development. To encourage settlement in Papua
he reckons the Commonwealth should give its
Possession tariff preference, on articles which do
not come into competition with Australian
products.
The Iceberg Government, having silenced
the railway employees, is turning its attention to
certain Vic. school-teachers who lifted up their
voices against the Reform Bill and separate repre-
sentation, and one who made an extra loud noise
is being “dealt with.” It’s not known whether
drawing and quartering or boiling oil will be the
result, or whether he’ll get off with the loss of
pension and compensation rights. What splendid
inquisition bosses Irvme and his Ancient Bent
would have made !
The fact that the Victorian mercantile
community has entered no protest against alleged
Senator Reid representing it in the Common-
wealth Parliament goes to show two things:
(1) That its sympathies are with customs frauds;
(2) that it feels that the representative of a firm
which has been convicted of defrauding the cus-
toms is a fit and proper individual to speak for his
fellow importers. The political existence of
Robert Reid is in itself sufficient justification of
Kingston’s watchfulness.
Australian Treasurer Turner, who knows
as mf!ch about finance as any man in Australia,
reckons that Kingston keeps the expenses of his
department down to £lO,OOO below what might
fairly be expected, simply by the vigor with which
he abjures every semblance of “frill.” If Aus-
tralia paid Kingston £5OOO a year he would be
cheap at the price. So would Turner. At present
Australia has the amazing luck to get such
financiers as Kingston and Turner at about one-
tenth their value, but the luck can hardly last.
S.A. Premier *:o a labor deputation asking
for compulsory arbitration :
He believed that the majority of the best manufac-
turers would be in favor of some legislation that would
prevent the possibility of strikes, provided that the
legislation was of such a character as would not place
them at a disadvantage with the manufacturers in the
other States.
A fair enough proviso, and pointing again to the
conclusion that industrial legislation must be
made a Federal matter. '
Writes George Pile—or it may be his dog
—on the prosperity of a Protectionist country :
The American papers, which I have been carefully
studying, tell us no tales about a permanent unemployed
section of the people—thej- are in abundant evidence
here, the product of the Labor party microbe. Before
the advent of that party these were non-existent.
Will George Pile —or his the pro-
ceedings of the old Legislative Council before
Responsible Government, and note the votes that
were put on the Estimates to relieve the chronic
unemployed of Sydney? Also, will he hunt up
the records of a long ago Commission of which the
then young and rising Parkes was a member,
which sat to consider the unemployed question of
that day ?
OVER-LEGISLATION.
And yet the bird wonders he can't fly!
The Omrah shipped at Port Melbourne
last week 250 tons of harvesting machinery for
Buenos Ayres, via London. This was built by
Nicholson and Morrow, of Carlton (Melb.), who
have already this year shipped 250 tons to ’Frisco
and have still another lot to go to Argentina.
Will Reid or M'Millan kindly explain how it is
that Protection, which, they say, raises the price to
the consumer, enables these harvesters to pay a
vastly heavier freight and yet beat British goods
in a market that used to be mainly British ?
Importer M‘Millan, in one of his recent
Parliamentary wails, said that Customs Minister
Kingston started on the assumption that nine-
tenths of importers were swindlers. Considering
that an influential deputation of importers
(Robert Reid and Co. being among the firms
represented) assured Kingston that at the single
port of Brisbane the softgoods importers alone
swindled the customs out of -£250,000 in three
years (£83,000 a year), which was something like a
quarter of the revenue, it would be no wonder if
Kingston did think that.
When N.S.W. Justice Pring decided that
a soldier, however vilely he might be swindled in
the matter of his pay, had no legal redress, there
was a huge and silly orifice or discrepancy per-
vading the whole argument. The talk, gabble
and judicial fuss was mainly based on the theory
that if the soldier was granted the right to sue his
employer, the State, while still serving in the
army, he might burst up the campaign by apply-
ing for injunctions and other large blue docu-
ments. And the principal problem before the
court was whether a soldier, after he had left the
army and become a private and, perhaps, some-
what drunken citizen again, had a right to sue for
his back wages, when his action couldn’t affect
the campaign in any way.
Robert Reid and Co., the softgoods firm
of alleged Senator Reid, has compounded the rest
of its customs offences, which seem to be
numerous. Queensland Supreme Court gave
Minister Kingston authority to accept a fine
without further legal expenses. Two clauses in.
the report are significant:
This order for leave to compound shall have the same
effect for all purposes as if judgment had been given for
plaintiff in respect of offences alleged in the statement of
claim. . . . Defendants thought the course now adopted,
namely, that of a settlement, the better one, and consider
they have been met in a very fair way by the representa-
tives of the Commonwealth Customs Department.
Which seems to convey: (1) That the softgoods
firm gives up the pretence that it isn t guilty; (2)
that it ceases to profess that it was prosecuted out
of private animosity, and admits that it is being
treated quite as well as it deserves.
HIS ONLY HOPE.
“The one material source of Australia’s greatness, if
it ever is to become great, must be —Australia.” — Aus-
tralian paper.
The S.A. Parliamentary Commission on
Railways is finding out things which would make
a less dead-tired country jump. For instance
(Engineer-in-Chief under examination): —
What was your estimate for the Outer Harbor Rail-
way ?—£BB3o, and the contractor’s price was £9740.
What was the consideration which gave the work to-
the private contractor?—lt was because the pendulum,
had swung completely the other way. Formerly every-
thing had to be done departmentally, and now everything;
has to be done by contract.
“Everything has to be done by contract,” even,
though the public has to bleed for it! Well, the-
S.A. public knew, or might have known, what it
was doing when last election it plumped for
Jenkins Iscariot.
This is the record of Dry Dog Reid, the
shuffler, time-server, and somersaulter, re the
Bill for the establishment of a Federal High
Court:
(1) Early last session he said there-was no hurry-
about it.
(2) When the Bill turned up, he opposed it bitterly,
and (he says) he was the cause of it not passing.
(3) Early in the recess, he boasted publicly that he had
prevented it passing.
(4) Later in the recess, he denounced the Barton
Government for not passing it, and urged that it should
be the very first measure of the new session.
(5) When it was introduced as the first measure of the
new session Reid didn’t support it and didn’t vote for it.
He stayed away. But his principal iollowers, presumably
by his direction, for the leader of the Opposition is.
supposed to direct the Opposition policy, fought bitterly
against the Bill which their leader had declared to be-
urgent and essential, and almost unanimously declared*
that a High Court wasn’t wanted.
A man may get to some kind of success by theses
tactics, but it is a very dirty kind of success..

One of the loudest laughs the House of
Reps, has heard for a long time arose at the
frantic flight of Spruce Myth when Kingston rose
to reply to his attack during the Address-in-Reply
debate. Myth was beautifully dressed and
brilliantined, and he danced to the table on tip-
toe and poured forth the vials of his ladylike
wrath on Kingston, and the big Minister lay back
and smiled. Then, when Kingston rose to reply,
there was a vision of a beautiful suit of clothes
bolting through the door, and a smell of hair-oil
flying through the corridor, and Spruce Myth was
gone.
Premier Philp moaning in North Queens-
land :
In responding to a toast at Townsville, he said he
thought that Australia would give North Queensland
justice some day, after they had dispensed with the pre-
sent insane legislation which the Federal Government
had passed to please a section of the people.
Of course, that legislation was passed “to please,
a section of the people ’’ —the larger section.
Under the Australian Constitution the larger
section rules. And Philp wants this legislation
repealed to please “a section of the people ” —the
smaller section. Meanwhile, the smaller section
pretends to regard it as an outrage that it doesn’t
rule.
INDICATION OF CROUP.
Hoarseness in a child that is subject to croup
is a sure indication of the approach of the disease.
If Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy is given as soon
as the child becomes hoarse, or even after the
croupy cough has appeared, it will prevent the
attack. It always cures, and cures quickly.

Illuminating item from Sydney D.T.’s
Canadian correspondent, printed by that paper
v ithout it being noted that it was loaded:—
Vancouver, B.C., May 2.—The vessels of the line from
Vancouver to Sydney always leave here with a full eariro,
but they invariably come from the Antipodes light. So
great is the demand at this port for cargo space in the
steamers of the Canadian-Australian line that shippers
must now contract three months in advance for it.
Under present conditions in Australia there seems to be
an unlimited market for American and Canadian pro-
ducts.
Bo highly-protected Canadian manufacturers have
to book space three months ahead for shipping
their goods to Australia, whilst low tariff Aus-
tralia, doing nothing but consuming the Canadian
goods and paying for them out of borrowed
money, has to return the ship light because it has
nothing to send in exchange 1

sulting Engineers, University Chambers, Phillip-
street, Sydney. Patents, Trade Marks, Copyright
throughout the world..
About the Stows :—
Only one is left now of the elder branch of the Stow
family which, one way and another, had a very great
deal to do with the making of S.A. The father, the Rev.
T. q., was the province’s first regular parson, apart from
the official chaplain. A Congregationalist, he is to be
remembered chiefly for the great and successful fight he
made against the proposed introduction of State aid to
religion. Unlike N.S.W., S.A. has no rev. pensioners.
Stow Church, where the late ex-Professor Roby Fletcher
had a pulpit for years, is liis memorial. This Rev.
T. Q. left three sons who became notable. Randolph
Ishani, the eldest, was probably the most successful
lawyer the province has reared, four times Attorney-
General, and Supreme Court Judge for a year or
two until his untimely death. A younger son, Augus-
tine—he who had an apoplectic fit as he was walking
through an Adelaide park a fortnight ago, and never
recovered—had a precarious political career from 1863 to
1871. He reached office once, under Strangways, as Chief
Secretary. It was one of S.A.’s numerous very sudden
Ministries ; it came in and went out inside a fortnight.
Augustine, who wasn’t built for Parliamentary hurly-
burly, dropped in to the Public Service, and slum-
bered comfortably thereafter as Chief Clerk of the
Supreme Courts. The second brother, Jefferson Pick-
man, still lives—he put in a few strenuous years as editor
of S.A. Advertiser in its struggling democratic days, un-
til S’langdon Bonython developed journalistic aspirations
of his own. Then J.P. too dropped into a State billet,
being appointed Police Magistrate.

A Russian Jew told a Sydney evening
paper lately something about the Jews of
Odessa and Kishnieff that makes one wonder at
the recent massacres. According to him, the
Jews are not extortionate money-lenders, as the
Russian law prevents more than a “certain per-
centage” being charged. What the percentage
is he did not explain, but contented himself with
saying that it was a very small one. But some
men would consider 250 per cent, per annum dirt
cheap. In Kishnieff the Chosen form, he says,
about half the population. Also, they have a
“quarter” almost entirely tothemselves, in which
the houses are built of stone, round quadrangular
spaces, with huge iron entrance gates. Such
places ought to be veritable fortresses, and, given
a supply of iron bars and plenty of boiling
water, ought to have held out against the fury of a
mere unarmed rabble. A correspondent who saw
the Armenian massacre in Constantinople some
years ago told of how, from a tower, he saw 15
Armenians, armed to the teeth, on a house-top.
They had at least three revolvers each and every
man of them a rifle and a sword. A Turkish
officer riding past caught sight of them and dis-
mounted, tied up his horse, climbed on to the
roof with only a scimitar, while the correspondent
waited to see his mangled remains flung down on
the crowd below. But the trembling Armenians
merely threw themselves on their knees, and the
Turkish officer slew the lot, and, being somewhat
tired thereby, sat on one dead body and quietly
6moked a cigarette afterwards. Supposing that
Jewish quarter of Kishnieff had been held by
men like the Spaniards who held Saragossa !

EMERSON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The centenary of Emerson—born May 25, 1803
—has just passed. Dr. Garnett tells us, in his
biography of the Sage, that “at an early age he
contracted an evil habit of sucking his thumb.”
The ways of the wise at an early age, and the ways of the
foolish wight,
Give hints of the days to come.
Did Turner draw with a charcoal stick? Did Mozart
music write?
Well, Emerson sucked his thumb.
What though his mother cased his hands in mittens of
knitted wool ?
Ay ! what though she spanked him some ?
Yet the hand of Fate stretched out to him as he sat on
his little stool,
And Emerson sucked his thumb.
n later life he was grave and calm, and he kept his mind
serene
’Mid the world’s vast, busy hum ;
While some through greed or creed went mad, and some
through love or spleen,
Old Emerson sucked his thumb.
On Egypt’s plain as he saw the Sphinx and gazed on its
battered face,
The Sphinx thought, “ This is rum,’’
And murmured low, “You’re another, then.’’ Of sur-
prise he showed no trace—
No 1 Emerson sucked his thumb.
Philosophers, some with tongue in cheek, or with fingers
stretched from the nose,
Or banging the blatant drum,
Have waxed dogmatic o’er systems vague. Their mean-
ing ? Goodness knows !
But Emerson sucked his thumb.
1 see him there in his Concord home, with his lofty tran-
quil brow,
In his meditations dumb.
But flashing thoughts like electric sparks betimes ; and
I’m glad, somehow,
That Emerson sucked his thumb.
D.BOUVERIE

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The big dividends on the wet Birthday Cup day
in Adelaide were responsible for some curious financing.
Two brokers arranged with a bookmaker that they should
be considered to have a pound on every starter all through
the afternoon, at “ tote odds.” They divided £24 profit
between them. A lady, who tried the same thing on a
smaller scale, neglected to arrange for the removal of the
usual 15 to 1 limit that the local Man with the Bag
imposes ; consequently, with 17 starters in the Oup, she
lost on the race, although the dividend was £59 Is. ! In
another transaction, however, it was the Philistine who
scored. Late in the afternoon, when big dividends were
the usual thing, a sporting lawyer sold, for £27 10s., a
ticket on an outsider that had just won. The purchaser
was a bookmaker, and 10 minutes later the dividend was
declared as £l4 18s. (£4O had been confidently expected,
but a small run on the number just before the start
brought the odds down). It was also stated that a local
theatre-manager spent £l7 in “ backing the lot ” in the
Oup, and, on sorting out his tickets afterwards to collect
the £59, found that he had inadvertently backed one
horse twice, and missed one—the winner 1
• * • •
Walter Hickenbotham, trainer of old Carbine
and a dozen other of the best, after about 40 years’ expe-
rience : “I think Carbine was the best horse Australia
ever saw. He would always do his best. Up to a mile
and a-half Newhaven was faster, I honestly believe, but
was hardly so reliable. The Barb stood out at his time
just as Carbine did in his. Newhaven did the most bril-
liant trials I ever saw. Then Mostyn—up to a mile, I
doubt whether even Marvel was his superior. The best
mare we have seen—Wakeful. Quiver and La Carabine
were good. And the fastest two-year-old was Wigelmar.
She could fly. If she had trained on, there is no know-
ing what she might have done.”

* * * *
Tote operations at Port Adelaide Club’s meet,
Saturday, ran anything but smoothly. Immediately
prior to fifth race working-battery petered out, and
tickets had to be given by hand, which caused consider-
able delay. Machine went all right for next and last
event, but announcement printed on programme that
dividends would be paid on second horse when 12 or
more started created more bother. Ten lined up, and
lots of people with second-horse tickets destroyed them
only to hear later that 12 was a misprint—it should have
been 10. Backers of the winner kicked vigorously against
any of the money going to second nag, and claimed that
they should get it all. Many refused the dividend, vow-
ing they would go for the full amount, while others
accepted it under protest.
Ernest Brodribb, the Australian turf-plunger
of a dozen years ago, died in London the other week,
aged 43. Brodribb seemed to take Ernest Benzon, the
“Jubilee Juggins” for his model, but whereas Benzon
dissipated his fortune before it fell due to him, Brodribb
played with readv money. He gambled every way, and
was “rooked” all the time. It was in 1890 that young
Brodribb, an amiable, bright-looking fellow, paid 4600
guineas for the two-year-old gelding Titan. The animal
proved a disastrous purchase for the plunger, but showed
some good form after it was sold for a moderate sum to
Donald Wallace. Poor Brodribb’s horses were trained
with Wallace’s choice little lot ; nevertheless, he suffered
through his connection with the stable, for when Carbine
won the Melbourne Cup in 1890 Brodribb had backed his
own horse, Prince Consort, likewise Tantallon (another
stable companion of the famous top-weight), and other
starters and non-starters—but the name of Carbine was
not written in his book. A singularly wrong-headed
plunger, with his heart in the right place.
The wealth of Edmond Blanc, whose horses ran
first second, and third for the Grand Prix of Paris, last
week, is derived from the Monte Carlo casino. Ranking
as “one of France’s foremost sportsmen” and finest
fashionables, Blanc is just the keeper of a gambling hell,
writ large. The character of gambling hellmonger is
brilliant or shady according to the vastness or littleness
of the hell.

The tote’s alluring odds once again. A field of
five lined up for a £2O stake at Ashburton (M.L.), and
an old chestnut named Beira, reckoned to have a dog’s
chance, got his head in front, and paid £44. There was
only 30s. on him in the machine.
Jockey J. Cameron, who recently rode winner
(Awahuri) in Wanganui (M.L.) Handicap, has had heaps
more than a fair share of close calls. Found out by some
one that 14 nags have been killed under Cameron, his
collarbone has been broken six times and dislocated once,
while “shakings” innumerable have happened to him.
Surely this is without a parallel the world over.

An inconsequential-looking prad, possessing as
much brilliancy as an antiquated steam tram, galloped
home a winner in M.L. recently, for the first time in three
years and gave his owner such a shock that he fainted.
Transpired afterwards that the owners of the other ned-
dies all quietly backed him and waited to see if he really
could win when he had nothing to beat. '

N.S.W. and Victoria have been doing a good

between the States in this direction, as a prominent
sport recognised five Maoriland noddies while on a visit
to the Commonwealth—all racing under aliases.

in Clyde Stakes (6 fur.) after being last into straight, and

easy winner. Yet the success was lucky, for both Ariana
and Bombshell (third) should have beaten Euston, but

to leave barrier. t , ,

The victory of Elorin in the Birthday Handicap,
at recent V.R.O. meeting, was another illustration of the
fact that horses are apt to be all the -better for having
taken things easy on the track after being properly wound
up. Florin’s recent performances in S.A. were not very
satisfactory, although he took the Onkaparinga Oup.
Subsequently the son of St. Swithin met with a slight mis-
hap, and, returning to Melbourne, he was not given much
work until he went out, unfancied, for the Birthday
Handicap, and won by sheer gamenese. In losing
“ condition ” the 6-year-old animal had gained vigor.

Entries for Epsom Handicap and Metrop., the
two big events of A.J.O. Spring meeting, show a small
shortage as compared with last year. Epsom has 107—7
less, and Metrop. 97—4 less. Victorians have nominated
liberally, and Queensland and Maoriland are well repre-
sented. Each race has 29 who’ll be syrs. old at the time
of running ; 4-year-olds total 52 ; 3-year-olds, 41; 6-year-
olds and aged nags number 26, but, as a whole, their
prospects do not impress much.
Newcastle scales tampering case is, like similar
discoveries at Moorefield (Sydney) and Brisbane, still, as
far as the law is aware, the act of “ some person or per-
sons unknown.” Attorney-General refused to file a bill
against owner (Kingsley) and jockey (Moran) of Gentle-
man Jim (in connection with whose weighing the trouble
occurred), but Newcastle Jockey Club has disqualified
both, and a couple of others, for life. Evidence against
the two named was not strong; they are appealing to
the A.J.O.
• * * *
Kalgoorlie (W.A.) racecourse is shortly to be
blessed with a brand-new brick totalisator, 200 ft.
long by 24 wide, with 100 operators; and, as a conse-
quence, there’s much wailing amongst the “ bookies ”
on the goldfields. There’ll be a serious attempt to have
the new building up for the big August meetings ; and
the healthy turn horse-racing has taken in the West
amply justifies the buying-up of some of the best
sprinters from N.S.W.—'notably The Persian and Skein
Dhu.

Atßiohmond (Vic.), 11th, Belmont M., from

* * * *
An old Rand wick (N.S.W.) identity passed
away last week in Mrs. George Donnelly. For a number
of years she was known to sportsmen as the landlady of
the Halfway House, Randwick, now the A.J.O. Hotel.
Her first husband was Johnny Cutts, who rode the winner
in the first and second Melbourne Cups, 1861-62. When
Johnny died, the widow married Johnny’s successor in De
Mestre’s employment—the crack rider, George Donnelly.
She survived both husbands.

Old-time Jock Donoghue died of Bright’s
disease at Cootamundra (N.S.W.) recently. Many events
on the flat and over the leps were appropriated by him.
Donoghue had, unlike most of his fellow jocks, kept
some of his winnings, and for years thrived as a farmer.

Re jockey Carslake’s three successes at V.R.C.
Birthday meet. His rise to prominence has been very
rapid, and he is still only a youngster. Apart from his
skill, Carslake is cool as a cucumber in the most exciting
finishes, which is a rare thing with one of his years.
Carslake p'ere was a good rider in his day.

» * » *
Under the new system of time-keeping intro-
duced in European motor-car trials, one-fifth of a second
becomes a clumsy division of time. The “ Mors ”
method of electric chronometrage (is that the right
word ?) gets down to the hundredth part of a second. It
was given a thorough trial at the Nice races in April, and
is said to have stood all possible tests.
* * * *
The Stock Exchange “ walk ” is the latest craze
in Australia—a silly kind of pedestrianism, following
closely in the wake of the London 1 took Exchange
50-mile tramp. For all that, if it doesn’t cause a few
fatalities, it should tend to put a more aesthetic appear-
ance on the pot-bellied ’changers.

Ex-world’s champion billiardist, H. W. Steven-
son, opens in Melbourne coming Monday. Four days
before leaving “ down under ” he lost supremacy to previ-
ous holder, Chas. Dawson, from whom it was wrested
and held 21 months. Stevenson won by a margin of
2594 in 9000, and lost by 300.
• * * *
The programme for the annual meeting of the
National Rifle Association (Sydney), in October, is full of
alterations as compared with last year. Foremost is the
decision to exclusively adopt the Bisley-sized target—
which, in reality, means a huge reduction in the size of
the bull’s eye at all ranges. From a first-class shootist’s
standpoint this is a much-desired improvement; but the
average second-rater, who is mostly after the stray prize-
money, will find .the conditions exacting. As an .instance
of the bull’s-eye .diminution, it maybe noted that the
36in. “bull” at 700 yards of previous years will be reduced
to 20in.; and the competitor who succeeds in putting up
a possible at this distance will want something more than
“ luck."
* * * *
N.S.W. Rowing Association is in a quandary
as to whether “ manual laborers ” shall be admitted to
the Association as full-blown “amateurs.” For a con-
siderable time the “tone.y ” class has held a monopoly in
amateurism, and any motion to allow the ordinary
person into the ranks is met with great hostility. The
“amateur” portion of athletics in Australasia is just
about as nauseous as ’tis possible to imagine, and a few
more years of “ class ” amateurism will about wipe out
that branch of sport.
* * * *
Talked-of Towns-Tressider match still hangs
fire —nothing definite can be got from the champion.
“ The least Towns can do is to fix some date, supposing
it is not until October or November, to meet and sign
articles. That, at least, would give general satisfaction,”
says Tressider ; but, beyond stating that he will row and
it is no use fixing a definite time till his business is
properly under way, Towns’ intentions are still known
only to himself. So the other stands idly by, with good
supporters behind him, every one of whom expresses
unqualified confidence that after he and Towns do meet
there’ll be a new champion of the world.
Public complaint having been made that the
language of some Broken Hill footballers in the midst
of an exciting game wasn’t polite, the authorities took
action and a policeman was told off to prevent any
repetition of the offending expressions. The first match
under the new Social Purity rules had interesting points.
The policeman stood in the centre of the playing ground.
The players didn’t swear, but a good deal of play centred
around the constable. Player after player bumped him,
apologising most sweetly. They also called to one
another like this: “ Here I am, William dear,” “ Pusher,
dear comrade, pass the ball to me,” “ Run, Carrots, run,
there’s a good chap.” The audience was too flabbergasted
to barrack with any vim, and when the match was over
the usually leather-lunged onlookers were content to
drive home singing hymns. It was altogether a nice,
clean match, though it wasn’t football as the game is
understood at the Hill.
* * » *
Features of Victorian football up-to-date are
the “ surprising reversals,” usually charged to the
machinations of Ikey Mo ; and the unexpected revival of
Carlton. Latter has been at, or near, the bottom of the
premiership list since ’9O or thereabouts. Last year it
looked up, and this year—thanks to the accession of new
blood from Geelong and elsewhere, but chiefly to the
coaching of ex-skipper Johnny Worrall —it was undefeated
up to June Bth, when it succumbed to the prevailing
“ unaccountable reversal ” epidemic and went under to
Melbourne, which thus scored its first win for the season.
At present Fitzroy is at the top of the list, but it may be
at the bottom before the season closes. Carlton has
beaten ’Roy and has gone down to Melbourne, now
bottom. “Saints” have defeated “ Sowth,” and latter
has walloped once-mighty Essendon ; yet, if play counts
for anything, latter should still hoist this year’s pennant.
* * * *
The gentle football barracker tough ! Con-
clusion of Richmond-Brunswick match, at Brunswick
(V.) Saturday, saw a pack of these wild beasts bombard
the visitors with blue metal till they escaped in cabs.
Captain (Alex. Edwards) was badly cut about the head
and face by a stone that crashed through the vehicle he
sat in. Week before, Newtown-Balmain (Sydney) match
on latter’s ground was disgraced b3 T something similar,
but set was against one man (a Newtown player) disquali-
fied for rough methods; he was hustled and belabored
till his side left the field and came to the rescue, when a
free fight ensued. For some minutes subsequent to the
appearance of the police a serious riot threatened. Ulti-
mately the victim was safely escorted to the tram—a
hooting, jeering throng of 2000 (mostly Balmain bar-
rackers) following.
W. R. George, the “father ” of amateur cycling
in N.S.W., holds a record through having occupied the
position of president of Sydney Bicycle Club for 23 years.
He and Arthur Crane started the Sydney Club in 1880,
and subsequently, with late James Martin, originated
the N.S.W. Cyclists’ Union. As a consequence, Sydney
bikists recently gave him a “ royal ” send-off, ere a
departure to view the sights o’ Lunnon Town.
* * * *
N.S.W. Bike League situation gets more in-
teresting and noisier every day. Monday night 59
attended a special general meeting, and waited some
for secretary Branagan, who had been arrested during
the afternoon for alleged “forcible entering into the
League room and removing therefrom furniture and.
other property belonging to the members.” Branagan’s
appearance was cheered. The protest of MTntosh
(elected in Branagan’s place by ignored council) that •
“this meeting is illegal ” was over-ruled. Then the 59-
got down to business. Original council was incontinently
bundled out through their seats being declared vacant,
and a decision arrived at that an executive committee of
14 he appointed to carry on the business of the league
pro tern. Branagan’s action in taking possession of the
League’s property was endorsed, and authority given for
the raising of subscriptions to defend him if necessary.
It transpired that original council had withdrawn all the
League’s money from the bank, and left the new execu-
tive with an absolutely depleted treasury, but an assur-
ance came from the holders of the coin that not a penny
had been spent since the trouble over Branagan began,
and the amount was still intact.
* * * *
World's amateur 3-miles running recovd was
broken recently by English carpenter Shrubb, who
got through the task in 14.17 3-5. Same man
finished within 35secs. of W. G. George’s world’s 10-mile
best, in English championships early last month ; and
last Autumn the 4-miles record went down to him. That
Shrubb had a good chance of creating fresh figures for
3 miles was abundantly evidenced in his attempt on
those for 10 miles —he covered the half distance, 5m., in
25 13 4-5, the speediest performance ever accomplished
for the first half of a 10-mile race. Fastest amateur
3-miles prior to Shrubb’s was S. Thomas’ 14.24, put up in
1892. Professional top-notch time is P. Cannon’s 14.19 L
at-Glasgow, in 1888, but this hasn’t the impress of
genuineness usually associated with records.
* * * *
Marvellous club manipulating crank Tom Bur-
rows had an endurance swinging match with another
Australian at Capetown (May 12), and waved the imple-
ments continuously for 40hrs. lOmins., thus eclipsing
world’s record of 40 hours created by Maorilander Bax
and Australian Griffiths at Newcastle six years ago. Aus-
tralians are unique at this game. Athletic history has
no mention of any other country producing an endurance
club-swinger capable of getting any way near the Bur-
rows-Bax-Griffiths achievements ; Englishman Lawton’*
25 hours is the nearest.
* • * *
Hargreave, Warner’s lefthanded bowler, made
a sensational return to England. His county, Warwick-
shire, had apparently to do without him against Surrey,
hut rain prevented anyplay on the first day of the match.
By the second morning, Hargreave, hurrying across from
Marseilles, was in London, and going straight to the Oval
was included in the team. He took 6 wickets for 41 and
9 for 35 ! Warwick won easily, in about a day and a half
of actual play.
* * * *
A strong effort is being made to revive boxing
in Adelaide, where it has been practically dead for years.
Jim Hiscutt, who had Cocker Tweedie beaten in Sydney
when the police interfered, four months ago, met him
again, on Birthday Cup night, in a “ converted ” Salvarmy
barracks, packed to the roof with a 10s., 4s. and 2s. crowd.
Hiscutt, probably remembering the early rounds at their
former meeting, went for a knock-out from the jump,
and Tweedie’s superior science was of no use. The whole
affair only lasted a round and a half, and in that time
Cocker was on the floor for 9,8, 4 and 3 secs, before
giving in to the mighty blows of the Barrier individual.
Fred Evans, a local man, has challenged the winner, who
fought at 9st. 41b.
*., * * *
Inter-State lightweights Palmer (Melbourne)
and Blackmore (Sydney) put up a two minutes scrap at
Sydney National S.C. last week. Blackmore got a weighty
left hook home early, and Palmer, badly scared, kept
falling down every time danger threatened till a right on
the ear provided an excuse for remaining there. Referee
M’Donald called it “ no fight” and declared all bets off,
which is the best way of dealing with anything having a
suspicious flavor, and the only remedy for “schlenter”
matches. Palmer certainly got it hard the first time,
but afterwards never made the slightest attempt to fight.
Not likely that Palmer will ever have another chance in
Sydney, which is a pity, for he is unquestionably a clever
boxer and a hard hitter too.

Concerning Australian heavyweight pugs -and
their bruisings. Champion McCall accepted telegraphed
offer from Queenstown (Tas.) of £2OO purse and £2OO side
bet to fight James and considered match arranged. A
subsequent letter from supposed purse-giver told that
James wouldn’t fight for less than £250 or the gate, and
suggested McCall should go for the latter. The New
Englander, however, wishing to know what was to
come to him, refused ; then Sydney National S.C. came
albng with £225 aud £2O exs. for James. Later, Queens-
town promoter wired “ £2OO offer still good ” but as he
went back on his first undertaking McCall considered
that agreement off and now says he’ll fight in Sydney or
nowhere. James’ alleged refusal to fight for £2OO in face
of the fact that the wire originally received by McCall
said James would meet him for £lOO purse is peculiar.
Strong likelihood of Sydney pugs, George Johns
and Jack Blackmore, being matched. Blackmore, one of
the cleverest in Australia to-day, promises to clinch after
meeting Victorian champion light-weight, Williams, three
weeks hence. Johns will have difficulty in getting to
weight (lOst.) insisted upon by Blackmore, but he is full
of sand. The exact quantity of sand in Blackmore is
unknown, but a fight with Johns will, no doubt, make it
visible. Tivoli Rickards talks of taking Blackmore to
England ; same man talked in the old days of taking
Griffo, Jack Fuller and co. there, but they never went.

* • * •
Second match ’tween Billy M‘Carthy (M.L.)
and Harry Phillips (Q.), at Sydney Gaiety A.C., last
week, resembled that at Adelaide. M'Carthy evaded
always, while Phillips was furiously, but as a rule fruit-
lessly, aggressive till the second round, when he sent
Mac. to the floor for nine seconds with a weighty right
on the jaw. M'Carthy stood up, rattled, and was soon
spread-eagled again, but spunk of a rare order, with the
help of the ring ropes, enabled him to struggle to his
feet in a stooping position. Phillips ladled it out hot
till “corners.” M'Carthy was still dazed in the third
clash, and clever footwork alone kept him on deck.
Each held t’other with his left and drove the right in
frequently and forcibly. Getting away from “holts,”
M'Carthy jolted a short arm right and, quicker than a
flash, Phillips was sent limp against the referee, and thud
to the boards, “ outed ” beyond question. It was a rare
stroke of luck for M'Carthy, who couldn’t have stayed
much longer and was in very poor “fix” as compared
with his stronger opponent.
* * * *
Bruisers Lyn Truscott and Alf. Yercoe
(brother of ex-middleweight champion “Soldier” Thomp-
son) roughed through a scuffling, clinching scrap at
Sydney National S.C., Monday night. Yercoe began
dazed and weary-looking, and never brightened once.
Though in excellent condition Truscott did nothing but
shove Vercoe’s head back with left pushes—they were
not hits. Had half of them been hits the scramble would
have quickly ended. Vereoe couldn’t punch hard enough
to make the slightest impression, and (when the towel
went up from his corner in the 15th roiind) while both
Vercoe’s cheek bones were grazed and he bled from the
teeth, there wasn’t a mark on Truscott, nor did he seem
to have turned a hair. As a bdxing contest the thing
was poor indeed though genuine enough ; yet it appar-
ently excited some.

Cabled, Sunday, from Wellington (M.L.), that
one-time top-notch ’rastler, Harry Dunn, was found dead
in a hut at the Globe mine," Reefton —“he had been
drinking heavily.” Dunn worked in Reefton mines for
past few years—mining was his calling. Inside 20 years
back he was first heard of as a ’rastler, and competed
successfully during the big Australian boom of that
period, when world’s champion, Tom Cannon, Donald
Dinnie, Prof. Miller, Jack Connors, Sali, and others were
up to their eyes in the game. Later, Dunn went to
America, and met with varying fortune, but was never
defeated at his own style—Cumberland. In Sydney,
since his return, he faced Lucifer at the Lyceum in a
Greeeo-Roman match, which Dunn won. While compet-
ing (Cornish style) at Hibernian Society’s St. Patrick's
Day Sports subsequently, Dunn, quite unprepared for a
surprise, went under to strong man Pagel, then un-
known (who entered as “Williams”). Dunn collapsed
mainly through utter lack of condition, for Pagel knew
little or nothing about the game, and relied solely on his
great strength.
• * • *
“ Bob R.” : Don’t know much about city racing,
but I guess the country owner could give even “ the
ponies” a point. Went to a race meeting in a Southern
town t’other day. First race —six started. Two jocks
had dummy spurs on ; one horse carried shoes heavy
enough to make a buggy tyre ; another horse “ bolted ” ;
whilst a jock at the run-home was lucky enough to have
a strong bridle ! The favorite won because he couldn’t
walk slow enough to lose without falling down. Another
race —three starters. One was left at the post; the out-
sider won by three lengths ; protest entered by the owner
of second horse on grounds of interference (presumably
the first horse had put its tail in the second one’s eye).
Stewards met (they had backed the second moke) and
upheld the protest ; also, in their indignation at nearly
losing their money, disqualified the rider of the first horse
for six months. Last race was run in a canter ; the whole
lot were “ dead ” ; and the corpse that won was the most
disgusted-looking deader imaginable
The incurable habit of leaning on the
Government ! Broken Hill, almost the richest
mining town in Australia, is bargaining with a
person who thinks he can make rain, and who
asks for £2500 if a deluge is forthcoming. And it
wants the Government to put up most of the
money. Every helpless leaner against a post in
Australia wants the Government to put up the
money.
Dr. McCarthy, the rain-inaker, says that
if he produces rain at Broken Hill and then the
slag city fails to pay up, he will not take the matter
to the law courts, but he will give the Broken Hill
people “suoh a showing up that they will he
ashamed of themselves.” But why show them
up? Why not drown them out?
Passed out at Bendigo, recently, Charles
Walters, who claimed to be the first mining
speculator in Australia. The old man, who had
reached his 83rd milestone, served an apprentice-
ship to the coachbuilding trade in Hobart, and
moved to Melbourne in 1849. When there, the
report of a gold discovery by a shepherd in the
Pyrenees attracted his attention, and he sent a
man out to explore the promised land at £1 a
week. This, he claimed, was the first money
spent in the search for gold in Australia, and thus
he was the first mining speculator. What became
of the poor devil at £1 a week isn’t known. The
world is full of poor devils at £1 a week.

cold from the draught of an open window or door. BUT ALL THIS IS NOW CHANGED.

luxurious Pullman Railway Carriages) admits into any room equipped with it a constant supply of outside fresh air
free from dust, smoke, or wet, and ABSOLUTELY WITHOUT DRAUGHT, irrespective of weather conditions. It
gently diffuses the air and also acts as an exhaust. IT SOLVES ABSOLUTELY THE PROBLEM OF HEALTHY

Alexander Morrison, the just deceased
Principal of the Scotch College, Melbourne, was
not cursed with a blind greed of gold. He
“ farmed ” the college, and, of course, could
employ just as large or small a staff as he pleased,
and pay as he thought fit. But “Doc” wanted
the S.C. to be the boss Victorian college, and so
the staff has always been Al. Maurice Carton,
University lecturer in French ; Ingram, classical
lecturer at Trinity College, Melb. University; and
Aiken, one of the most brilliant science Students
that Melb. University has so far produced, were
all on the S.C. staff.
The excursion by s.s. Victoria to the South Sea
Islands has been organised for the purpose of visiting a
large number of places of interest—principally mission
stations-which are not in the trading routes' Passen-
gers will fully appreciate the unique opportunity now
available for cruising 7000 miles of the Pacific on a 3000-
ton ocean yacht. The Victoria leaves Sydney on 25th
July next. The prospectus of the tour sets forth a pro-
gramme far in advance of that of any previous excursion
to the Dreamlands of Coral and Palm. Excursionists
will spend eighteen days ashore. Cardinal Moran and a
clerical party go with this excursion on a tour of inspec-
tion'of the mission stations.*
S.M. Herald describes it as “a serious
allegation ” that N.S.W. Government ispostponing
payment of accounts till the new financial year.
Of course it is serious, but every N.S.W. Ministry
has done it since Dibbs and honesty went out to-
gether. That is what Reid’s “Cash Basis” is
for. Before Reid’s time, all accounts belonging
to the year had to be charged to the year. Reid
created a system by which all accounts not paid
on 30th June are charged to next financial year,
and started by not charging till next year six
months interest on a large portion of the public
debt. That * ‘ serious allegation ” has been
allegated regularly for seven,years now.
The Tasmanians are already weeping a id
tearing their garments that they put out the mem-
bers of the last Government —Lewis, Bird and
Mulcahy—and left the youngsters now on the
Treasury Bench without an Opposition. The new
Assembly sworn in the other day is a ragged crowd.
More than one-half of the members have, per-
haps, never seen the inside of a Parliament House
before. During the one-day sitting a week or
two ago, R. C. Patterson acted “by request” as
leader of the Opposition, but what he led wasn’t
clear. All the most experienced of the last
Parliament are gone, and there’s nobody left to
defend the Lewis policy. A lot of people wanted
to see a change of Government, and to get rid of
the income-tax, but few really wished to see the
old war-horses shot as useless.

Re an interesting ancient :
Melbourne’s bard, Fitzgibbon, who, at 70, was lately
elected to his fourth term as Chairman of the Board of
Works, is, in spite of his “ White Knight ” craze and his
poems, a sturdy democrat in many ways. To an Argus
interviewer he gave interesting details of his own life*
hut kindly kept a veil over the idiosyncrasies of many of
Melbourne’s municipal identities whom he has known.
Born at Cork, the young Irishman was at Forest Creek
diggings in 52. Then became reader to Victoria’s
first Leg. Council, where he met Andrew Clarke,
Childers, Stawell, Molesworth, Faw'kner, and O’Shanassy.
Next he became Melbourne’s town clerk, Jacky Tommy
Smith was Mayor. From ’56 till the Board of Works began
sewering Melbourne the other day, he watched a long pro-
cession of municipal nobodies—whose very names are now
forgotten—pass along, and most of the time he had to
struggle against their stodgy Toryism and Parliamentary
aggression. Though he doesn’t say so, the White Knight
can have but a poor opinion of those gas-baggy old
pioneers. Fitz. wanted to municipalise gas, water
supply, hydraulic power, and other works for the public
advantage, but couldn’t heat municipal incapacity,
allied to Parliamentary jobbery. He wanted the cor-
poration to acquire as much real property as possible, so
as by-and-bye to do entirely without rates, but “No,
no,” said O’Shanassy, “You would make the corpora-
tion too independent of Parliament, and we object
to that.” Even when the council was ge ting
power to build shops, etc., on its market frontages
and had got its Bill through the Assembly, old man
Fawkner—the Fats of those days being just as dense as
to-day—blocked the Bill persistently until “ Big ” Clarke
showed him that his own property would be improved by
the council’s improvements. Fitz. also had many tough
fights for the public’s parks, ever open to the attacks of
greedy' speculators and needy Governments, and even his
vigilance and tireless protests couldn’t save them all; J.
M. Grant, when Lands Minister in the ’6o’s, gra’ bed and
sold the frontages of Roy at and Fawkner Parks, along
Sydney and St. Kilda Roads. With a less energetic,
public-spirited servant Melbourne would probably possess
few parks to-day, as the “redoubtable old pioneers”
early acquired the habit of annexing all they could lay
their hands on, and though they couldn’t get the deeds to
the land they actually carted away the best soil for their
private gardens. They' were rapacious robbers, those old
pioneers ! Also, though he couldn’t block the swindle
altogether, it was mainly owing to Fitzgibbon’s efforts
that Melbourne will own its own tramways in 1917 —tie
original Bill gave the co. the right to build the lines and
own them for all time. Altogether, Melbourne owes
Fitzgibbon more than it thinks, for he stood up for the
people’s rights w hen there was no Labor party to keep
watch on the jobs of scheming politicians and needy
speculators.
Stuck well into the middle of an Adelaide
obituary notice the other day was the announce-
ment that the dead man (a well known prospector)
“wired only a few days before his death that he
quite endorsed the high opinions expressed
about the mine.” People who have to sell
wild cats can’t afford to be particular, perhaps ;
but it looks like playing it rather low down to
use an obituary par to puff the Something-or-other
Extended.
Charles Hadin, whose prospecting mate.
George Withers, was probably the first white man
to find gold at Coolgardie, died lately on the
African Gold Coast. Withers was speared by the
Westralian blacks, and the pair had to clear.
They told Bayley and Ford that there was gold in
the Coolgardie district, and these two risked it,
getting there about 12 months after the others.
Hadin had only been a few months in Africa,
where he had been working with W. Frost,
another old Westralian battler.
Went under in Adelaide t’other week
Leeson (mostly known as ‘ Lazarus ’) Staples,
one of S.A’s. very old time licensed victuallers.
He humped the bluey over many, many miles
in the wheat State in the early days, and one
time was worth many thousands, but latterly
dropped much of this through drought. He kept
the old Kapunda Bazaar in the beginning of the
fifties, and also used to run a stall in the East
End market, Adelaide. His age was generally
understood to be something immeasurable.

“H.” on Smiths :
There is a new book on “The Smith Family.” The
author comes up exultant, with his catalogue of achieve-
ment, but you leave off with the query : Where is
Napoleon Smith ? Darwin Smith ? Scott Smith ? Dickens
Smith? Gladstone Smith ? You tap with an inquisitive
hammer all the sounding bells of Shakspeare, Homer,
Dante, Goethe, Voltaire, Balzac, Tamerlane, Confucius,
and what not, but no Smith responds. To be sure there
are Adam Smith, two Sydney Smiths, and the Rt. Hon. W.
H. Smith, M.P., Minister of War, and Head of the
Admiralty, the original Sir Joseph Porter ; but the appal-
ling opportunities of the Smith battalion otherwise do
not struggle much above the commonplace, unless—and
here the author is strangely at fault—unless the New
World of Australia is called in to redress the balance !
There, indeed, we have Spruce Myth, Jacky Tommy
Smith who went to England with a chaplain and came
back with two- donkeys, and a Melbourne journalist
Smith or two—also Pumpkin.
In Sydney, the Sunday trams are supposed
to leave off running for 90 minutes morning and
evening, in order not to disturb the services in the
handful of churches which abut directly on the
lines. But, of late, the Department has begun to
abbreviate the period of stoppage, and it looks as
if there might presently be no stoppage at all.
Wherefore the S.S. Evangelical Council has depu-
tationised the Department very seriously, and
wagged its beard at it very sternly, and asked it
where it expects to go to when it dies. The
Bulletin has a certain sneaking sympathy with
these dour old preachers. It suggests that the
stoppage be extended to 2J or even three hours each
time, and that the prohibition of traffic be ex-
tended to include ’busses, cabs, private carriages,
bikes and ferry-boats. Then the pious can hoof it
to church and hoof it home again, and the Sab-
bath will be in better repair than for a long time
past.
Monday, 25th May, was the 62nd anni-
versary of the lighting of Sydney with gas. The
same co. has held the monopoly all through.
Be the passing of Parkes, “ M ” writes :
The very last time Henry Parkes was in the city of
Sydney, I was standing about 6 p.m. at the corner of
Elizabeth and Hunter streets, when I caught sight of the
veteran limping feebly along from the direction of Parlia-
ment House. As he approached, I saw a look of unut-
terable sadness in his ghostly face, and a strange
sardonic light in his eyes. As he stood waiting for an
Annandale tram, with his eyes fixed on the ground,
and the crowd bustling around him, no one gave more
than a passing glance, and even several M’s.P. brushed
by him unheedingly. He was so lonely, so depressed
and manifestly feeble that, although not intimately
acquainted with him I felt impelled to inquire how he
was. “I am very tired,” he said —“ very weary ! Nothing
interests me now ; and I feel that Ido not care.” “ You
are not well,” I ventured. “ Physically,” he replied, “as
well as ever ! My frame is full of life, and would hold
out for years if I wished it. But I don’t ; for my mind is
tired, very tired, and I should like to let it have its long
rest.” “ You will be brighter to-morrow,” I said, “ when
you come into town and meet some old friend. “ I have
few friends,” he answered—“ fewer than almost anybody.
Those few I will meet at home, if they care to see the
last of me, but I will never come into town again. This
is my tram. Good-bye.” He held out his shrunken
hand, and I sorrowfully assisted him into the car.

Sydney, July 11th, 1902.
Dear Sirs: My little son was trou-
bled with a very terrible cough for
about six months, and was so bad, that he
could neither eat nor sleep, and wasted away
to a mere skeleton. On trying Scott’s Emul-
sion, I found he improved from the first dose,
and liked it; it first eased his cough, then gave
him an appetite for food, restored sleep to
him, and finally built him up into feal robust
health.
About two months ago my husband had a
severe attack of pneumonia. I induced him
to try Scott’s Emulsion, and the result in his
case can best be told in the words of the doctor
who attended him. He said : “He had never
in his experience as a medical man, seen a pa-
tient who was so bad get on so well and so
quickly.”
Faithfully yours,

is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass and let it stand until
the next morning. If it is then cloudy, shows a sediment like brick-dust, if particles float about in
it, or it is of an unnatural color, then your kidneys are affected, and no time must be lost in taking
measures to restore them to health and activity, or you will soon begin to feel the effects by suffering
from Bright's Disease, Diabetes, or some less serious but more painful complaint.
Rheumatism, Gout,
Lumbago, Sciatica, Ansemia, Debility, Neuralgia, Digestive Troubles, Stone,
Gravel, Bladder Disorders, "all arise from defective action of the kidneys or retention of
URIC ACID
in the system, owing to this defective action.
The Kidneys. —lt is the province of the kidneys to separate from the blood, surplus water and
urinary impurities. The kidneys of the average person filter and extract from the blood about three
pints of water in twenty-four hours. In this quantity of water should be dissolved about an ounce
of urea, ten to twelve grains, in weight, of uric acid, together with other animal and mineral matter
varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce. If the kidneys, through weakness or disease,
are incapable of extracting this solid matter, a certain proportion of it remains in the system, acts as
a specific poison, and produces one or more of the diseases named.

Our Burst.
feature of Australian communities is their
necessity for periodical bursts. It doesn’t matter
what direction the bursts take so long as they are
ursts. Australia feels that way now. In
Victoria the burst has actually started, and they
fire pleased to call it “reform ” over there. The
spectacle of an alleged “reform” Ministry being
a lowed to cut into old age pensions, hospital and
charity votes, to tax incomes of £125 per annum
and let the wealthy landowners of Melbourne and
other cities go scot free with hundreds of thou-
sands of pounds worth of property, is only
explainable by the fact that the State is on
the burst. And what a burst it must be
when the same gang is allowed to take
active steps to suspend Habeas Corpus Act, and
arive the capitalistic motor car through Magna
h'Auu , and ar “ & an ? s of University pushites
with batons and special permits to bludgeon in-
otiensive citizens who happened to linger near a
railway-station ! N.S.W. has not started its burst
yet, but it s getting that way. It’s like the recur-
rent boozer in a Woolloomooloo boarding-house
who keeps on making spasmodic trips to the
corner pub., returning to stand round the hall and
he stairs with a glittering eye discussing public
finance, the foundations of belief, or original sin.
±he recurrent boozer will always get on the tear
sooner or later, but if there are a few other dis-
reputable boarders egging him on in the hope of
getting a few free drinks he gets there all
the sooner; and the four Sydney dailies are
mu ,*?, P° sltlon of those disreputable boarders.
1 heyll ha,ve N.S.W. on a howling tear very
shortly without doubt, and shouting reform
as it rushes down the track that leads to sweating
civil servants, murdering the sick poor in hos-
pitals, and robbing the aged and causing untold
misery for little or no gain. The Labor and
.Progressive parties should start an energetic
propaganda, so that when N.S.W. does howl
down some track or other it may be the one that
leads to graduated land tax, absentee tax,
compulsory resumption of big estates for closer
settlement along the rich, well-watered coast-
line ; and the abolition of borrowing. Every
man has the feeling some time or other that
he must either rush out and kill somebody or
else found a great charitable institution. It is
the question of directing the feeling the right or
the wrong way which marks the difference
between a Ballance and a Bent.
The Enfranchised Woman.
Constance Clyde on the woman voter
In respect to politics the man voter has 99 pitfalls to
fall into ; the woman, 999 ; and the men who are educat-
ing her to use her right are responsible for most of them.
She needs, first of all, to avoid the individual who harries
her to vote for “ clean men,” and she isn’t, unfortunately,
avoiding him. At a recent small female meeting one
woman (out of 30 or so) expressed doubts as to the
alleged cleanness of some Parliamentary person whom
they wanted to do some political “chores” for them,
whereupon the majority withdrew their votes, as
they considered that whoever was their knight
should not have a breath upon his name, and
also that his escutcheon should be stainless and
his crested plume something not to be sneezed at. The
Franchise lady who wears her hat over her corrugated
countenance feels herself a political St. Elizabeth in these
days, which fact is going to play into the hands of the
politicians with the chaste white foreheads. After the ro-
mantic voteress comes the “earnestwoman.” Lotsof “earn-
est women ” (Protectionists) with the University brand on
them have informed me that if it comes to choosing
between the Freetrader, who will raise the consenting
age, and the Protectionist, who isn’t interested in the dole-
ful subject, they will choose the George REiD-ite ; which
show's a deplorable deficiency in their sense of propor-
tion. Because, if Protection gives the consenting ladv
(or her father) good employment, she may not be so
ready to consent as formerly; and even if ‘she is, it is
a hard, irreligious truth that the girl who consents and
gets employment afterwards is better off than the non-
consenting girl who has no work and no adequate means
of support. When a country girl gets good work and
wages she soon settles down to being as moral as she,is
able to be.
Many women are Protectionists or Freetraders accord-
ing to the size or sex of their families. The mother of
young children looks at her drought-smitten breakfast
table, and when the butter won’t go round talks bitterly
of Barton. Mrs. Smith, with two sons in factories and
another coming on for factory life, is a Protectionist;
but Mrs. Jones, whose daughters earn no wages, is a
Freetrader, because “if the girls’ clothes aren’t dearer
already they soon will be.” The only way for the Govern-
ment to convert her to common sense, is to supply her
with three wage-earning sons-in-law in double-quick
time. The maiden lady of uncertain age is sometimes a
Freetrader, because she considers boys should “ settle on
the land and rough it; it makes men of them.” It is
always other people’s sons that we W'ant to make men of
by turning them as near to the brute creation as possible.
The only way to convert this severe person is to get
her a husband—and Government will find that a tough
business.
»
One of the Maoriland M.H.R.’s elected last
November is T. E. Taylor, who is worth watch-
ing, and who stirred up things pretty considerably
when he was in the House before. He is Seddon’s
pet horror, and keeps that worthy old person in a
constant flutter. At a banquet in Christchurch
the other day, after nearly everyone had got off
a load of flattery of Chamberlain, Taylor got up
and paralysed things in this style :
The questions dealt with by Mr. Chamberlain would
have to be discussed pretty thoroughly within the next
year or two, but he rather shrank from endorsing all
those things proposed in the name of Imperialism.
There was no instance recorded where an empire had
accomplished a great deed ; there were heaps of instances
where a small people had accomplished mighty things.
If, while he was in the House, the matter came up for
actual settlement, he would not discount the employ-
ment, the education, and the comfort enjoyed by the
working men of the country one per cent, for the
sake of the Empire. (Hear, hear.) The Maoriland
Parliament could render a more signal service to
the race they belonged to by conserving the rights
of the people, and setting an example to the Old
Land. He wanted to know what this Imperialism meant.
Did it mean that an English institution like the House
of Lords was to be allowed to put a finger on the affairs
of the colony ? If an empire had to give a House of
Lords, let us go out of the partnership altogether.
(Applause.) He loved his country, but he would not tie
round the necks of the people the problems that the
apathy of the English people had created in the Old
Land. So far as he was personally concerned, he would
be very sceptical of arguments that were based on pure
sentiment. The people should watch with the greatest
care any proposition such as had been made by Mr.
Chamberlain. He failed himself to see why we should
do anything in the way of Imperial trade that was going
to injure our people for the benefit of the British manu-
facturer.

A Melbourne pressman writes :
The Ballarat legislator has to be a wily bird. For years
that Sabbatarian centre has been represented in Vic.
Parliament by pious and teetotal politicians. I once met
a Ballarat member at Parliament House, and we had
drinks, the politician’s being gin and lithia, reiterated
several times. After that we caught the train for the
golden city. When we got out to refresh at Bacchus
Marsh, the member drank soda straight. At Ballarat, I
again suggested drinks. A horrified expression crept
over the legislator’s phiz, and he loudly remarked “My
dear fellow, I never touch it,” —then in a whisper,
“Leastways, not in Ballarat.” Verily, the Ballarat
member must not let his left hand know that which his
right hand lifteth.

At Poverty Point.
Nellie Stewart 19 offered a tempting American engage-
ment for her Nell Gwynne. It goes without saying that
“Sweet Nell,” the work of an American author, has been
played everywhere in the States, and the Australian
arti9t i 9 everywhere spoken of as the star performer of
the name part. This country has produced a famous
actress.
Melbourne’s Daily Syme begins to show a fine Tory
regard for class distinctions. Even the theatrical critic
reveals it in his notice of “ Breaking the Drought,” when
he says : “ Mr, Styan gave a capital character study—not
of a typical squatter, but of what would be described in
the back-blocks as a ‘ bush cocky.’ The average squatter
is generally a man of some education, and some experi-
ence of city life, but as represented in the play he is an
unsophisticated bushman to whom champagne is a perfect
novelty.” Squattahs must not be libelled in this way.
David Syme himself is among the wealthiest of the elect.
Notable how “clean” American novels and American
plays are, compared with the average European produc-
tions. This is particularly noticeable in farces ; most
English, French and German farces hinge on some hus-
band or wife having, or wishing to have, a night off the
matrimonial chain ; the American farce makes the com-
plication spring from a man’s desire to go to a prize-fight,
or some other comparatively harmless amusement.
Silvery tenor C. Rose is about the unluckiest vocalist
in Australia. He had just succeeded in arranging a
series of dramatic performances in Prahran Town Hall,
when he fell dangerously ill.
Herr Plock, who died recently at the age of 79, never
discarded the typical stage German accent, which he
interlarded with Yiddish. Twenty years ago he was worth
£lOO,OOO, but lost every penny of it in Matthew Davies’
banks.
Roland Cunningham, brother of Arcus Cunningham,
“ created ” the tenor part in a new comic opera at one of
the London suburban theatres. Cunningham scored
heavily, but the opera, a perversion of Audran’s “La
Toledal,” was killed by the dull libretto.
Robert Courtneidge, who came out to stage the elabor-
ate productions of “Midsummer Night’s Dream” and
“As You Like It” for Musgrove, is now journeying back
to via America, with his wife and smart little
daughter, Cicely. Courtneidge may return to Australia
one of these years, but for the present he has some
speculations brewing on the other side, and, having
made a big mark as manager of the syndicated theatres
at Manchester, he is likely to get fixed at the Big Smoke.
Scholarly artist and indefatigable man of business are
combined in Courtneidge's nervous, unassuming per-
sonality. Whatever he does is done for all it is worth.
Amusing to note the wrath of Melbourne’s musical
cranks and critics on discovering that Mark Hambourg
couldn’t give his bank-holiday concert at the Town Hall
because Nap. Boffard had hired it for the occasion with
no intention of using it. Ze great Napoleon’s policy was
quite legitimate. He was running a popular concert at the
Exhibition Biiilding on the same night, and didn’t want
any opposition at the Town Hall. And how wise and
bold a speculator the gaudy stout gentleman would have
looked in the event of the Exhibition Building getting
burned down before his concert fell due !
Maurice Grau, just retired through physical breakdown
from running grand opera enterprise in New York, says
he has been so successful because he treated opera as a
"commercial proposition.” Grau says he is no musical
critic, has never “ discovered a voice,” and wouldn’t
know one if he heard it.
“ F.” : No doubt (according to 8., 6/6/’O3) Snazelle
is a bit short; but I reckon that a three months’ course of
running lantern views would spoil the best of tempers.
I saw Snazzy several times, and never saw an evening got
through altogether smoothly. Worst experience was in
a big Vic. provincial town, when the man that managed
the views mixed things up from the jump. Snazzy stood
it well, even when the picture of a blacksmith appeared
as an illustration of “The mill wheel,” which Snazelle
was singing. At last the fiend with the lantern put in a
waterfall upside dawn, and Snazelle exploded. He began
by being laboriously sarcastic, saying that the waterfall
might have been upset lately. Then he stood off the
slide manipulator and cursed him vigorously. The very
next picture had a fly, magnified till it looked like a
winged alligator, walking over it. The fly had sneaked
in at the lantern man’s end. Snazelle let go again.
After that the lantern man got flurried and everything
was upside down or sideways, and Snazelle let himself go
wholesale. It wag a great night.
A London weekly recently offered three prizes for the
three ladies who were voted to be the prettiest per
formers then showing at the London and provincial-
theatres. Miss Kitty Rayburn, of the Britannia Theatre,
Hoxton, topped the poll with 6972 votes ; Miss Dorothy
Frostick, of Liverpool, being a good second, and Miss
Daisy Jerome, Manchester, a fair third. Marie Lloyd was
19th on the list (Great Jupiter!), Ada Reeve 22nd, and
Grace Palotta B6th, with a paltry 316 votes ! The three
prize-winners were playing panto, boys’ parts, and by
leaving much of their beauty unadorned they scooped
the sympathy ef the public. But a public which could
give one vote to Marie Lloyd for “ prettiness ” is such an
ass as not to be worth considering.
Charles Mathews, when making a speech from the
stage, was considered peculiarly happy in his impromptu
replies to interjections. The press people were a shade
less enthusiastic, because the comedian always handed
them in advance his written address, containing inter-
jections as well as replies. Mathews kept his own stipen-
diary interjectors on the premises.
Old-time “Mikado” executioner, Jimmy South, now
finds sanctuary in Queensland’s old-age retreat, Dunwich.
He took part in a stirring drama performed for the
amusement of the inmates, on Easter Monday, and was
much applauded by his co-fogies.
Re ft defunct publican :
Every shilling bequeathed to Melbourne charities by
the late Charley Wright was fairly owing to them, and
a long time overdue. “ Charley ’’ touched the age of 81,
and the period of his night-house glories was way back in
the dim past when Bourke-street, Melbourne, ranked as
a h 1 of a thoroughfare, and Wright’s "Coliseum”
and “Canterbury” were the hottest of licensed liquor
and loose-leg shops. Wright dedicated £lOOO to the
Foundling Hospital that has yet to come to pass—a most
appropriatf bequest; £3OOO in trust for Melbourne Hos-
pital ; £2OOO for the Alfred Hospital, and £lOOO to be
invested for the benefit of the poor-boxes at four Mel-
bourne courts of petty sessions. All of his £30,000 estate,
bar some £2OOO, goes to charities. The will directs an
expenditure of £5O for a tombstone, and £6O for a
memorial window in a church. A memorial window to
Charley Wright! Shades of the departed who drank,
danced, ai£d wasted at the Coliseum—are you listening?
The Conscienceless sweating practised by
the Bent Railways is at last becoming too much
; £or even the master-sweater —the Victorian farmer.
'jjL 1«4 of these agriculturists protested lately
ty&aihst the suspension and prospective punish-
metat of the Guildford station-master, who
overslept himself one Sunday morning and so
delayed a train. The official; who, single-handed,
runs the electric staff, goods, parcels, passenger,
stock and telegraph business for the township,
had started work on Monday at 5 a.m., and kept
straight on till Tuesday, 8 p.m. Started again
Wednesday, 5 a.m., and went straight on till
Saturday 2 a.m. Then he tried to cram a week’s
proper rest into 3 hours and slept in. He should
have been awake, and on duty'again at 5 a.m.,
and he wasn’t. He was only in bed twice, and
had but 14 hours off duty in the whole 6 days.
The local agriculturists only ask that his offence
in not being on duty again and fully awake at
5 a.m. should be overlooked. They don’t ask the
punishment of the heartless savage—whether it
is Bent or any other savage—who caused this
hideous state of things. In fact, they are very
moderate agriculturists indeed.
N.S.W. Opposition leader Joseph had a
dinner party on the eve of the Session opening,
and in an atmosphere of thick grease his sup-
porters larded him, and he larded himself and his
supporters. And there was a cannibal glare in
every eye. Solemn Lee looked at Joseph hun-
grily while he larded him, and Ashton seemed
to be guessing at Joseph’s weight as he wielded
the rich grease-mop. The guests who were
not Ms.P. looked ravenously at the guests
who were, and you couldn’t help imagining them
scrunching one another’s bones. Jt was the
hungriest-looking festivity possible, ‘ aiid even
after much meat had been eaten and much
whisky consumed, there was still the impression
of an aching void everywhere, and, going home, as
much envious scandal was talked as after a
mothers’ meeting.
Sydney D.T. again on the iniquity of
making an importer explain a prima facie cronk
invoice to a magistrate in court:
Where intention, or gross carelessness, can be shown,
it is right that there should be a prosecution, but, in
other cases, we make a suggestion, etc.
Now who , if not the magistrate, is to determine,
and where, if not in court, should it be decided, what
is intention and what is gross carelessness? Is it
the Minister in his private office ? Is it one of his
officers in a private office ? No doubt there would
be no objection to either of these, so long as the
importer was let off ; but if fined heavily lie would
yell “ star chamber,” and if sent for trial he would
mourn about having his case prejudged. When
the customs were in State hands there was a good
deal of this quiet settling-up of questionable
invoices before a private officer in a private office,
and the suspicion of bribery, influence, and palm-
grease was over it all.
Thus his Honor, quoting a ease in N.S.W.
Divorce Court: “ The husband agreed to return
to her if in the meantime she acquired the accom-
plishments of piano, reading, writing and deport-
ment ; but it appeared she did not acquire these,
and gave birth to a child, to which he contributed
2s. 6d. a week.”
Melbourne Bard of Works Fitzgibbon ex-
plained, the other day, that he was moved by
Heaven to apply for the chairmanship of the
Board of Works, at £2OOO a year, when he was
getting £l5OO a year from the City Council.
When Town Clerk Fitz was first implored
To seek a fatter billet,
Thought he, “ I’ll go and ask the Lord
If I am meant to fill it.”
He went to church, with mind at ease
And there, in accents fruity,
He prayed upon his aged knees,
“ O, Ruler of the land and seas,
Instruct the parson, if You please,
To guide me to my duty.”
The preacher’s text was but a word,
The one word “ Watch ! ” (how thrilling !)
Fitzgibbon bowed his head, and heard,
And answered, “ Lord, I’m willing;
Although Thy servant’s old and gray
(Full 58 my age is),
I guess I know the time o’ day
By this Thy ‘ Watch,’ which is to say
I’ll never throw a chance away
Of making better wages.”
Then forth from church he slowly went,
And, feeling quite a martyr,
Communicated his intent
To jaunty G. D. Carter.
“ Restrain," quoth Fitz, “ your sinful smirks
I am the Lord’s anointed ;
A chosen servant rarely shirks
Two thousand pounds a year, and perks,
God calls me to the Board of Works !
He sha’n’t be disappointed ! ”

“Jaybell” on some Queensland poli-
ticians :
Up to present, the last-elected Laborite in Bana-
land hasn’t done anything extraordinary in the fire-eating
line. Dawson used to emit blue-flame when the fancy
struck him in past days, likewise Higgs, but Higgs’ used
to dart farther across the House and sizzle more Minis-
terial whisker than Dawson’s. Now that both have gone,
Joe Lesina has a heavy job to keep up former traditions.
His awful habit of coming in loaded with about 4£cwt. of
documentary impedimenta, and persistently referring to
the'mound of nondescript literature as his “notes” is
rather blighting than otherwise, and makes even his
kindest friends wish that he would set a match to the
heap. If he could be prevailed upon to make a speech
in handcuffs, thus compelling him to depend solely upon
his own memory and unaided efforts, it would be among
the finest ever recorded in Hansard. The 4£cwt. of
literature blights Joe’s efforts.
Vic. Justice Hartley Williams (now re-
tired) held his first criminal brief, associated with
the late J. H. Dunne, in the prosecution of bar-
rister and journalist G. H. Supple for the murder
of Walshe, a publican of Spring and Lonsdale
streets, and an ex-detective. Supple, with a
loaded revolver, was in hot pursuit of G. P.
Smith, barrister, journalist and politician, when
Walshe, standing in his bar door, rushed between
the pair; Supple, almost blind, when grappled
with, fired or else the revolver went off. Higin-
botham, in defence, argued that the weapon ex-
ploded consequent on Walshe’s collision with
Supple, and a point was reserved built on that.
The Full Court sustained the conviction, and the
Executive said that Supple must hang, but finally
commuted the sentence.

with it, yellow fever.

A Land of Premise.
A tale is told in England of a young man whose
ability was so small that nobody would pay him a
penny for anything he could do. There being
nothing against his character, his friends supported
him ; but with that lack of cheerfulness which
usually characterises such aid. After a few years
they wearied of the arrangement, and sent their
burden here. The night before the young man left
London, he asked some questions of a traveller
well acquainted with our country. “My friend,”
said the traveller, who was a bit of a wag, “ Aus-
tralia is the finest spot in the world. Nuggets of
pure gold may be had in abundance by merely
raking the surface of the ground, and food of every
kind is so plentiful that the people will pay you
liberally to eat it.” The young man was charmed
with the picture, and strangely enough no sooner
stepped off the steamer which brought him here
than he trod upon a purse. On finding that it
contained only £lO, he contemptuously threw it
into the water. The story ends here abruptly.
They who find, more often than not, do so only
after long seeking. Not until after she had been
treated by three doctors, and tried many medi-
cines, did Mrs. Huntley, of 65, Union Road, Ul-
timo, Sydney, N.S.W., discover that Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup is the one sure remedy
for biliousness. ‘‘From 19 until 23 I was
not well for a day,” writes Mrs. Huntley,
on June 19th, 1902. ' “My ailment was
chronic biliousness. Ordinary food was poison
to me, and I was obliged to exist on soda-
water, milk, plain biscuits, and dry toast. Even
that fare would sometimes distress me to an in-
tolerable degree. My skin turned sallow, my eyes
sank and were surrounded by dark hollow circles.
I slept only by fits and starts, and there was a dull,
continuous pain between my shoulders and in my
right side. Then there were frequent fits of vomit-
ing. During four years of this misery I was treated
by three doctors, and took numerous medicines;
but nothing availed me until I was induced to try
Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup. Within a few days
of my first dose of that remedy, I began to mend,
and before a month had elapsed I could eat ordi-
nary food without fear. I continued to take it for
two months, and by the end of that time was
thoroughly restored to health. My long illness
and rapid recovery due to' Mother Seigel’s Cura-
tive Syrup excited much attention at the time in
Ultimo —where I was born, and my father before
me, and am, therefore, very well known.”*
