i The A*® l “°. urn ® dailies are straining every nerve to
keep their circulations at normal since the Ballarat and
Bendigo dailies launched out to catch the western and
northern army of readers, under the motto that “ ft’s
the early paper that catches the pennies ” ! The provin
cial dailies have their city offices and staffs and theh-
literary and mechanical arrangements have been brouirht
up-to-date, particularly the latter-regardless of expense
(considering the outlook). The Argus has corresnon!
dents in the rural centres only, and as a rule only press
men connected with the local organ are appointed-
it pays one shilling for each weather report received and
eaters particularly for pastoral tastes. supples
its country corres. with addressed envelopes, stationery
and printed forms on which to keep tally of what thev
get printed—a simplified invoice-foim J On the other
hand, the Age has a representative in almost every
tin-pot hamlet in the State, pays 6d for a weather
report, and caters to suit the' cookies' palates in
regard to rural happenings. The Bendig-o press records
every blade of grass that makes its appearance between
Pannoomilloo and Carag Carag-and pays one penny a
line as against three-halfpence .paid by the Melb taffies
but many of its “ Out-Back ” scribblers are paid so much
weekly. When things look brighter, it is quite on the
boards all the dailies will reduce their literary expenses
considerably on the principle that anything sefis when
money is plentiful. Then woe to the army of unem
ployed-ahem !-country journalists who revel in beer-
money these dry times !
* * * *
And still they go ! Melb. Age has now lost four good
men since beginning of year—M Oulloch (late Evening
News, Sydney). A. Buchanan (late S M.hU), Pratt
(now of Sydney D.T.), and Bnckhill, a Tassy. So far, none
of these has been replaced, and two of the departed, at
least, wili take a, lot of beating. Only change on Argus
staff is withdrawal of F. Magona (late Adelaide ’Tiser)
through ill-health. The Age office, always a nigger-
driving shop, has lately been a perfect Gehenna for its
over-worked, and now excessively under-manned, staff,
Just at present the Age is doing a large re-shuffling of its
literary hand. G. F. H. Schuler, who for two years has
acted as editor, has been superseded, the “ Old Chief ”
Windsor, being now again in full command. Schuler was
not literary enough, though astute and, in politics (David’s
politics), perfect. During his time the Age wobbled so
badly at times that if the Argus had not been similarly
afflicted all along, that many-opticked paper might have
taken its 10-years-lost lead once more. It is a strange
coincidence that both the Oollins-st. dailies have had the
same experience with their helmsmen—Haddon, after
“ retiring," is back again, while Willoughby is definitely
done (ill-health). It seems probable that Schuler will
carry out his oft-talked-of retirement at last (he has
thousands of golden reasons therefor) and that when
Windsor, who is not anxious to carry on after all these
years, decides that his own age and David Syme’s Age
form too great a burden, that brilliant, kindly, inde-
fatigable pressman, J. S. Stephens, will occupy the chair,
* * * *
Re the emotional side of Justice Williams (Yic.).
It was displayed in the early days of his judgeship
when sentencing to a term of imprisonment a young
man who had abortively cut the throat of a Flossie
living at May Day Villa. She had rooked and jilted him
after the manner of her kind. Said Flossie wrote a
letter to the judge begging him to be lenient to the
man she had ruined. It was said that an Age reporter
obtained a copy of the letter (a scoop for his paper), by
passing an evening at May Day Villa, and that when
David Syme heard of this, he sacked the scribe instanter.

An international trust of all the linotype co’s., embrac-
ing, too, the Monoline Co., is threatened. Competition
In the trade cut the price of a linotype from £650 to £2OO
In France. Hence the trust proposals.
What newspaper office in the Commonwealth
has not been honored with a visit from the itinerant
comp. Humorous to the brim (of his seedy old hat)
his failings o’ercome him—then, perforce, he walks
Bohemia! He can hold his own with any man at the
case, when he likes, which is not often while the price
of a draught by which he falls” is burning a hole in his
pocket. At the word “go 1” he takes up his stick, faces
the case, and goes ahead as though he had served a life-
time in his new billet. In an hour he is familiar with all
tne type, in two he has the name of every “pub.” in
Shantyville, in three hours he reckons he is an indis-
pensable addition to the staff of the Shantyville Grunter !
He works well, as a rule, till first publishing night—then
s i vo^ . s fy a( 'ks of “pi.” When he spits at his stick of
stuff, his aim is untrue—and when he lands by mistake
on the eye of the proprietor-cum-editor-cum-manager-
cum-everything who must stay to see the edition through,
hege? his walking ticket ana the balance of his wages.
Why snobs ” and comps, run to drink is a puzzle. The
snob owns up to his pet failing; the travelling comp,
puts his misfortune down to the linotype. “ Put off on
account of machines, sir,” he pleads unblushingly. But he
has lots of sympathy—the old type-slinger. His unreliabi-
lity gives him a sort of charm. His undeniable cleverness
at unexpected times, when a clean proof is a longed-for
desideratum, and the knowledge that the copy is well nigh
undecipherable is abroad, leads one to shift the blame
from his shoulders when the “ hash up ” comes. For
as he comes he goes—like a shadow and a memory—
only to turn up next year again like a mushroom. Then
another job, another “ bust up,” and away on the wallaby
again.
* * » *
The brilliant and lamented Dekker, of Sydney
Evening News, was a singularly tired man. Once,
when writing an article on the Australian’s sad want
of enterprise and vigor, the draught from an open
window annoyed him. Being too tired to shift, he rang
nis bell and, pointing to the window, languidly said
down” to the boy, and went on writing. As the
window was easily within the editor’s reach, the boy
didn’t quite take in the tiredness of his chief. Also,
he thought the editor said “jammed,” and informed the
foreman to that effect. Dekker was slightly amazed to see a
muscular “hand,” and two weaker men, heavily laden
with tools of the screw-jack variety, bustle into the room.
After a noisy and critical inspection the myrmidons got
their shoulders under the window, and, with a cheery
“ All together, boys 1 ” sent the frame nearly through the
roof and themselves amongst the editor and inkpots. For
a time, the den was a wilderness of astonished curses,
and the editor tacked another paragraph on his article —
about the Australian’s waste of energy this time.
* * * *
. One of the best proofs of the excellence of the organisa-
tion amongst the Vic. railway men was the way in which
the Melbourne daily press was kept in absolute ignorance
as to the engine-drivers’ intentions right up to the actual
delivery of their ultimatum on May 8. That was at 9.15
a.m., and in their issues of that morning both Age and
Argus discounted the possibility of a strike, the former
distinguishing itself by printing a couple of columns of
testimony from “well-informed” sources in support of
its own vehement declaration, as set forth in a staring
headline, that a strike was out of the question. An
Argus rep., who has been since boyhood a friend of one of
the strikers’ executive, walked home with the latter
after the final meeting on the night of May 7, when the
last touches had been put to the strike campaign arrange-
ments, but in spite of persistent and more or less adroit
questioning he could not extract even a hint of an
intended strike. Another man on the same staff nearly
tumbled on to the well-kept secret through overhearing a
telephone message from a railway man in which, explain-
ing his all-night absence from home, he said “ The fat’s in
the fire”; but, through inability to follow up the clue
and obtain information, the chance was missed of making
one of the biggest “ scoops ” in Australian journalism.
A few country happenings, the reporting of which
brings a crust to the way-back correspondents of the city
and provincial dailies : dust-storms, loeu> ts, hail-storms,
cockatoos, sparrows, parrots, galahs, foxes, eaglehawks,
hot fly, codlin moth, caterpillars, poisonous weeds, mag-
pies, crows, Cape weed, Bathurst burr, thistles, wild
dogs, frosts, &c., down the ladder to Gehenna.

Zangwill on his former profession :
“ Journalism is the art of disguising one’s own ignor-
ance in order to add to other people’s.”
“ Every question is like a sheet of paper—much may be
said on bothjsides. But for journalistic purposes it may
only be said on one side.”

An American announcement: “ Arthur Stringer asks
us to say that a line in his poem in last month’s Ainslie’s,
‘ Bad-scented April evenings,’ was written, with purely
poetical intent, * Bud-scented April evenings,’ which
sounds more cheering.”
* * * *
The following notice has been posted up in a room of a
Victorian newspaper office, and not before it was time :
“ (1). Constables have to ‘ go ’ to where they are wanted.
They are not to ‘ proceed ’ to the place of the mishap, fire
or disorder. (2). No person fished out of the river in
time is to be ‘no werse for the immersion.’ .(3). ‘ Happy
pair’is to be given a rest in reporting weddings. (4).
No song is to be ‘pleasingly rendered’ in this district.
(5). Nothing is to ‘ bid fair ’to be popular. (6). No acci-
dent ‘which might have eventuated in serious conse-
quences ’ is to be so described without serious conse-
quences to the writer ; and (7). A ‘ representative gather-
ing ’ is to be cut out.”
* * * *
English Institute of Journalists is gradually shoving
ahead its scheme for making the press a “close pro-
fession.” Latest move is a qualifying examinaiion with,
for chief subjects, English language and English litera-
ture, modern English constitutional and political history,
modern political and physical geography, a couple of
foreign languages, and newpaper libel and copyright
law. What English journalists, and the Australian
variety, too, require is a solid union which will not be
afraid to hit out at sweating proprietors.

The Age and Argus, the twin brethren of Shekels and
Shoddy, have been belaboring each other over Malcolm
M'Eacharn’s revelations of the damage done to Vic.
manufactures by the miserably inefficient Federal tariff.
The Age got home heavily on the Argus’s imported ear,
hence this reply from the paper with the hundred green
spectacles :
“ The COMMENTS of the Age on our exposure of the
figures so carelessly adopted by the president of the
Chamber of Commerce from its columns IS characteristic
and WAS to be expected. . . The results of these move-
ments ARE published monthly, and it isindeed marvellous
that THIS could have been passing under the eyes of the
President of the Chamber of Commerce without his
being aware of the facts THEY establish.”
It are indeed —or, rather, they am.

AS AN INVESTMENT.
READ WHAT
AN AUTHORITY SAYS.
PEOPLE cannot understand why Diamonds do not come down m price.
Unless some new Diamond fields are discovered they will never come
down, and I think that proportionately they will go as much higher
in the next ten years as they have in the last ten years. The reason
is this • Ten years ago it took less than one-third of the effort to produce
Diamonds as it does to-day. In other words, the strata of blue ground m the
Kimberley Fields that were then worked were so prolific that Diamonds
were found practically on the top of the earth. After the very prolific strata
were worked out and it was necessary to go down a little deeper into the
earth Diamonds were not quite so plentiful, and to-day it takes at least three
times the effort to produce the same amount of stones it did ten years ago.
Pearls are also going up in value at a rapid rate.
A
UNIQUE
INVESTMENT.
AS an investment, Diamonds afford advantages offered by nothing else.
;p or years they have increased in value at about the rate of 10 per
cent, a year, which makes them as an investment about three times as
good as any bank. When you put your money in the bank someone
else has the use of it; when vou put it in Diamonds you yourself have the use
of it and the pleasure and "satisfaction of wearing Diamonds are worth as
much as the returns from the investment. This is no idle statement, written
only with the view of selling our Diamond goods, but actual facts which you
can easily prove to be correct.
We are the only Australian firm having a Warehouse in Hatton Garden,
which place, as everyone knows, is the Diamond Mart of the world. Our
long and careful study of the Stone Market has placed us in the unique posi-
tion of being able to buy more favorably than almost any firm. These
advantages are shared with our customers, while others, if they could buy as
cheaply, would probably retain them as extra profit.

Joe and the Dook.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bill and me were sittin’ in the Half Way pub.
cooling our internals, when Bill looks out the
winder and spots Joe. Ses he: “Here comes
Joe, back from Ballarat. I s’pose he’ll come
spongin’ around for a booze.” So when Joe comes
in we were busy arguing whether Australia
should be loyal to Hingland.
I ses, It s this way. Say if Hingland gets
into a war with the Younited States ”
“He’s having a wet with Jerry, and Joe’s
payin’,” ses Bill.
As I said, sez I, “if England goes to war
with the Younited States ”
Joe s payin again, and a sovereign, too !
What ho ! Joe ! Come and settle this argument ”
ses Bill.
Joe came over. “What’s the row?” ses he.
We told him.
“Well,” ’e ses, “ let’s have a wet first. Hurry
up, Missus Public House. What’s yours’ Now
blokes, you knows I was always against Royality’
but Ira goin to arst you to drink to the health of
the Dook of York, and Gorblessim.”
We drank it, astonished like, 'an’ asked Joe
what ad happened.
“ Well,” ’e ses, “you know I finished that con-
tract of splitting in the forest, and I went down
to Ballarat with fifty-five quid nine and sixpence
all on my lonesome. I had a look about, and
after a while trots into a place they call the Ex-
change. Well, there was a lot of blokes sitting
about, so I sits down too. I saw a little parcel
tied up carefully—looked like joolery, so I edges
to it and sneaks down and picks it up. I opened
it and, straight wire, there was nothin’ in it at all.
All the blokes began larfing like a lot of kids.
The chap sittin’ next to me was quite wild, and
ses ’e, ‘ It’s a damn shame.’
“Well, him and me got talkin’, and presently
’e says ‘ Do you want to make a heap of money ’ *
ses ’e. ‘My oath,’ ses I. He whispered, ‘ Well!
buy Dook of York.’
“‘Chuck it,’ses I; ‘ where can I buy him ?He
larfed a bit, and ses ‘ You go into that orfice and
arsk him for Dook of York shares and he’ll sell
them quick enough,’ ses ’e. So off I trots and ses
‘ ’Ow many Dook of Yorks can I get for fifty
quid.’ Ses’e, ‘They’re one and three, and you
can have 800 for £5O. ’ ‘ Orlright, ’ ses I. He gets
some papers out of his iron safe and gives them to
me and I gives him my fifty quid. I got a bit
suspicious, and I ses ‘ These yours ? ’ ‘ No,’ ses ’e,
‘ Whaffor ? ’ ‘ Well, why are you sellin’ me some-
thing that doesn’t belong to yon ? ’ ses I. ‘ That’s
my bizness,’ ses he, larfin’. I was getting wet.
‘lsit ?’ ses I; ‘an’ it’s my bizness, too.’ ‘ Don’t
get oxcited, ’ -ses ’e, ‘ I sell shares for other people.’
‘ Well, trot the other people out,’ ses I. ‘Orl-
right,’ ses ’e, larfin’ like a kid, and out he trots
and brings in the very bloke wot kidded me to
buy. ‘ Oh,’ ses I, ‘ it’s ’im, is it ? That’s orlright.
’E’s a friend of mine. Why, ’e’s the bloke wot
told me to buyjthem,’ so we all went and had a
wet.
“I had five quid left, and, somehow or other,
I had a row with a cop. I found myself doin’
fourteen days in quod.
“ Well, I was makin’ for the pub, when I corned
out, when the bloke wot kidded me to buy the
shares lines up and, ses ’e, ‘ ’Ow de do? I was
lookin’ for you. 1 want to buy back them there
shares.’ Sol thinks to myself, ‘Now, ’oo’s this
bloke wot sells shares and wants to buy them back
again ? ’ While we was havin’ a wet, ’e ses, ‘ I’ll
give you three bob each for them there shares.’
You see, blokes, I’d told him I’d just come out of
quod. ‘Wot,’ ses I, ‘you sold them to me for
one and thrippence, and you’ll give me three bob
for them ? Why, there was a bloke like you up
our way wot was givin’ suvrins away for six-
pences. Some of your pals, I suppose?’ ‘’Ow
dare you, you low potato-man,’ ses ’e ; so, to stop
the argument, I plugged him in the jaw an’ ’ooked
it. Even as ’e was failin’ down an’ was sort o’
arf-way to the ground, I ’eard ’im say ’e’d give me
four bob for them shares.
“ I got 14 days for pluggin’ ’im. When I comes
out I thinks to myself ‘ Perhaps the old geezer in
the orfice will give me my fifty quid back for them
there shares, so I trots down.’ Ses I ‘What’ll
you give me for these Dook of Yorks ?’ Ses ’e,
‘ They smell of beer —phew ! I’ll give you six-
and-two for them.’ ‘Oh, h I,’ ses I, ‘take
them.’
“So he antes up—blokes, what do you think —
over two hundred and forty bloomin’ quid. So fill
’em up again, and we’ll drink ‘ the Dook of York,
Gorblessim.’ ”
GENUNG.
A Letter From the Bush.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dear Bulletin, —You have printed one or two
“little letters from London,” and now I think it
is “up to you” to print one from Way-back.
lam “ full ”of bush life. Nothing but dust,
crows, and dying beasts. I can stand dust and
crows, and one poor meal a day, but the beseech-
ing looks of our old cows, that we had made pets
of, as they lay unable to rise, and dying of slow
starvation, haunt me day and night. I know
they appealed to me to knock them on the head ;
but Dad trusts in Providence to send the rain, so
they must needs suffer to the end. And the poor
sheep that die as their baby lambs are born—
baa-ing their last breath to scare off the waiting
crows—and the bareness, and eternal same-
ness, and the oft-proved uselessness of it all—
and—and—but I mustn’t dwell upon it.
I cribbed the paper I am writing on from the
school-teacher who boards on our farm —we
haven’t had such a luxury since Xmas—or a
stamp to spare. I got a fox scalp to-day while
out wood-oarting for Dad, so our luck is on the
turn. It means 2s 6d. Then I will post this on,
and spend the rest on a present for my mother
a cake of soap, or something useful.
I wish to get a billet down your way—only for
a living-wage, with a trifle over for tram fares.
I can plough, scarify, roll, work the reaper and
binder, combined harvester, cream separator,
erect a windmill, sink a well, milk, manage
horses, doctor beasts, stack wheat, make a hay-
stack, thatch a roof, prune trees, class wool,
shear, break in horses —but any fool can do these
things.
I come of a family of fools. Dad is a grafter,
straight, honest, sober and real good (when
there’s rain about) to mother and the kids but
he’s a fool. He wouldn’t be “cockying”on the
fringe of eternal debt and starvation if he wasn t.
Mother’s another, for allowing Dad to be a fool.
Every Jackass on the land is a fool—to himself.
All our neighbors are alike—of the soil, soily.
They have heads (so have we) like our cows and
horses, and not half the sense, or they would trust
more to themselves and less to Providence for
their food and water. Our school teacher is a
conundrum. He stays at our house, puts up with
the same fare as ourselves—and pays his board
regularly —in fact, mother is waiting for it on the
verandah on the first of every month. It carries
us on—for there has not been a penny of butter-
money this season. The teacher trusts to the
Department for his income and not to Providence.
Dad is going to make a big dam when the rain
comes. Why he doesn’t start to work straightaway,
instead of mooning round looking for the rain,
licks me. He was going to sell Bluegown and
Dimple—the two cows that died ; but he stuck out
for £8 2s. 6d. the pair when the dealer offered
even money. Dad wouldn’t budge because his
left leg felt rheumaticky. And, in the end, he
was glad to get 6s. for their hides.
Mother toils on, but is getting greyer every
day. Sis is only 17 next month. The census man
thought she was 40. All the younger kids look
old, and are skinny—dreadfully skinny—all
through nothing but the drought.
But I want to sneak away from here. I want
to see the sea; to see black clouds; to get wet
through and get a real cold, just to feel what it is
like. To get my feet soaking wet, to work—to
graft like sin at anything off the land. I won’t
mind working machinery if it isn’t mortgaged.
I’ll be glad to dig, deep and well, if there is a
hose fixed handy to water what I plant. I ’ll
drive horses and make them love me; milk cows;
shear and tend sheep—but I must have a contract
duly attested that they will be well-fed. I’ll
work the old hours —dawn till dusk, Sundays in ;
but no crows must caw within earshot. All the
birds I want to see are a few willie-wagtails—
they’re gay; and sparrows—they’re cheeky; and
a magpie or two —they ’re useful and plucky. But
let me get out of this. We ’ll go next if the rain
doesn’t come soon—then the crows will triumph.
The country can’t afford to lose Dad’s breed, fools
though they are.
RITA S.
A Sewing-Machine.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My wife reckons her sewing-machine is a bit out
of date because it doesn’t mend socks, hem
trousers, sew on buttons, rock the baby to
sleep, and cook dinner, so she called on the Jigger
Sewing-Machine Co. to have a look at their much-
advertised modern implements. The young lady
in the shop assured her that they could perform
- all the above operations and play hymn tunes on
Sunday in addition. My wife made her inspec-
tion, and said she would think about the matter.
The young lady, however, froze on hard. “Do
you know,” she said, “your face is so familiar.
I’m sure I have met you somewhere ; are you not
Mrs. Samuel Jones, of Montmorency-street,
Waterloo ? ”
“No,” said my wife, innocently, “I’m Mrs.
Galvo, of Bondi.”
Profuse apologies from the young lady.
Next day, just as we were sitting down to
dinner, the door-bell rang and my wife answered.
I heard a scuffle, and went to see what was hap-
pening. A man with a sewing machine in his
arms was trying to thrust the same past my wife
into the hall against her vehement protests.
“ Doesn’t Mrs. Galvo live here ? ” he asked.
“Yes!”
“ Then I was told to leave this machine here
on approval for three months, and I’m going to.”
It seemed about time for me to take a hand, so
I sent my wife inside and addressed the man in
my best engine-erecting style: “If ycu don’t
take that crimson, spindle-legged, bone-shaking
abortion of a barrel-organ off these premises at
once, I’ll kick it and you down the steps and sool
the dog on to you ! ”
He went, sadly.
Next day, while I was out, a man called and
said he represented the Jigger Sewing Machine
Co., and had heard we wanted a machine.
He could assure us that their style possessed all
the latest improvements, with noiseless running,
ball-bearing silver griller attachments, and could
be worked by a child. My wife said she didn’t
want a machine, but he stuck his foot in the door
and held forth for ten minutes without effect.
Next day a woman came, who said she tra-
velled for the Jigger Sewing Machine Co., and
had been told we wanted a machine. My wife
said she was a hussie, and slammed the door in
the middle of her peroration. Following morning
another man stood on the step and delivered him-
self of the same rigmarole. My wife told him
she would complain to the manager if any more
travellers called, and he replied that he would be
most happy to transmit her complaint. He evi-
dently did so, for the next afternoon a fat, red-
whiskered man, smelling strongly of whisky,
arrived, and said he had called to apologise for
the excessive zeal of his travellers. He would not
like anything done which would prejudice my
wife against their patent Jigger sewing machines,
which he could assure her were the best in the
market, and would make Bayeux tapestry and
boil eggs. My wife indignantly told him that
she had intended buying one of his machines, but
was so disgusted at the treatment she had received
that she had now decided to obtain one from the
opposition. He smiled, bowed politely, and left,
and we thought the siege raised.
Two days later another traveller materialised,
and nearly sold us the same old Jigger machine
by professing to represent a rival firm and abus-
ing the Jigger people with fearful intensity.
Then an alleged third firm turned up, and abused
both the previous firms, and tried to sell us the
Jigger under another name. But I have nearly
completed the training of my bull-pup to bite
viciously whenever the word machine is men-
tioned, and when that is effected and he has taken
up his station at the front doorstep, we look for
such remnants of peace as may be left by the
bottle-oh man, the sauce-hawker, the buy-a-cheap-
load-of-firewood fiend, and the barrel-organist.
GALVO.
“K.K.”: Was padding the hoof at the
back of Wanganui (where the lunatics come from)
when, turning a sharp bend in the road, nearly
collided with a man running as if for his life and
a little farther saw a great smoke. Asked the
fellow where he was going and whether the smoke
was a house on fire. “No,” he said, “that’s a
neighbor of mine burning weeds. She’s a widow,”
he said, beginning to get a move on again, “and
when I see a widow burning her weeds I’m off to
a safer place,” and with a wild howl he galloped
away.

“ I gradually sank into a state of ill-health,” says Elizabeth Corkran,
of 8 Milton Place, Richmond, Melbourne. “My blood became impoverished,
and unsightly pimples and blotches
appeared on my face and neck. The
doctors whom I consulted pronounced
it to be an acute form of Eczema, and,
although they treated me for some
time, my health gradually grew worse.
I could scarcely eat any food, and
what I did eat disagreed with me, and
the suffering I endured at times was
dreadful. I also tried various medi-
cines advertised, but without result,
and I was altogether in a very des-
pondent mood, until, one day, I re-
ceived a pamphlet wherein I noticed
where fellow-sufferers had been cured of similar complaints to my own by
the judicious use of Bile Beans and Zam-Buk Ointment, and I decided to
give them a fair trial. I underwent a course of the Beans, and at the same
time applied Zam-Buk Ointment to the blemishes on my face and neck.
After a week of this treatment I noticed some improvement, which became
more pronounced week after week, until now—just two months since I
began the treatment—lndigestion has entirely left me, and my skin is now
quite clear, and, as you may imagine, such a perfect and speedy cure came
as a great surprise to me, after so many other treatments had proved
unavailing.”

Andrew Barr, of Doodlekine.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Andrew Barr, farmer, of Doodlekine (Westralia), clauus
to have invented a flying machine that will travel at tne
rate of 100 miles an hour, and carry a weight up to louu
tons. It is to be driven by a gasoline motor. He states
that he has been experimenting with flying machines for
over 20 years.
This is the ballad of Andrew Barr,
Farmer, and sage who brought a sign ;
Inventor bold of the flying car—
Pride and glory of Doodlekine.
Greece had Athens, and Italy Home —
Names that in history’s pages shine —
London’s the boast of the folk at home,
But Australia rejoices in Doodlekine.
Twenty years on flying machines
Andrew worked with his humble means.
While others were growing turnips and beans,
Potatoes, parsnips, and curly greens,
And their days were heavy with sordid toil,
And their souls were planted deep in the soil,
Andrew walked with his head in air,
And his soul in the clouds, and his eyes aglare
With the glare of genius that strange things sees,
Wonderful visions and mysteries.
Little for turnips did Andrew care,
With his soul in the clouds and his head in the air.
Wonderful are the ways of Fate ;
Long does she keep her chosen hid ;
Silent for years are the truly great —
Till suddenly opens the casket lid,
And winged wonders spring upward then,
And dazzle the world of the common men.
Who were the people that garnered Fame ?
Who were the people that made a name,
Trumpeting round the afflicted land
The tale of their deeds and intentions grand,
Till even the deaf, perforce, took heed ?
Barton, High Pontiff, and rollicking Reid,
Forrest the rugged-, and Deakin the fine,
Kingston courageous, and lumbering Lyne.
Melbourne, and Sydney, and Adelaide,
Famous thrice over by them were made,
While nobody heard, or could divine,
Of Andrew Barr, or of Doodlekine.
Maxim, and Dumont, and Zeppelin—
All of the aeronaut kith and kin—
Each puts up his shutters, and burns his car,
And uncovers his head to Andrew Barr.
In the years to come, as men will see,
People will travel to Doodlekine
As pilgrims go to the holy shrine;
Sacred to Science the place will be,
As Stratford-on-Avon to Poetry.
Amurrikan citizens will come,
And the queer Land of the Chrysanthemum
Will send her contingent of pilgrims rum,
And the place will then like a bee-hive hum
With praises, in many a tongue bizarre,
Of Doodlekine, and of Andrew Barr.
O Andrew Barr, take gude Scotch heed,
And make this prophecy true indeed !
CREEVE ROE.
An Episode.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The white, straight road made by white men
runs through the middle, and on either side lies
the Older World. That is why the Englishman
riding on the white road looks at the Maori pah
with no understanding ; for there is a matter of
several centuries between, and to the door of
these centuries the Englishman has found no key,
because he does not know that the door is locked.
By law of Nature, therefore, each caste should
hold to itself ; by law of man, it consequently does
not.
There was a Maori girl once belonging to this
pah, and she was beautiful in a way low-grade
Maoris rarely are. The pah was proud of her, and
the white men who came down the road stared at
her supple figure, and her delicate hair, and her
brown eyes with the gold lights; and in many
cases they came back and stared again.
One day she went away, and the old women
nodded their heads together, and the young
women whispered, half envious, and one or two
of the men were angry, for they loved her. But
all this part of the story belongs to two years ago.
The meeting-house of the pah stood back from
the road, under giant trees that had grown over
the holes trenched out in the Maori \\ ar. Ihe
pakeha said that the carvings in it were very
wonderful, and very hideous, and marvelled be-
cause they did not know that the Maori idea of
beauty is ugliness.
It had been a sacred place once, before the
Maori began to wear European clothing and get
drunk. Now all those families who had not gone
away and pretended to be pakeha slept in it on
mats laid round the walls, and bought a couple of
iron bedsteads at an auction mart to keep their
boots on. There were nine families in it al-
together, and it. was thirty feet long by fourteen
wide, with one window that wouldn t open.
It ’ was the very hottest evening of a hot
summer, and all the men were asleep on the
burnt grass before the meeting-house, and all the
women were smoking and talking a little, and
laughing over the fat naked babies that rolled
with the dogs. The white, straight road made by
the white nien after their kind reeled under the
dying sun, and pretended that it stretched right
round the world. It was indubitably twenty-two
miles long.
A black speck had walked down it for fifteen
miles, and ten miles of a by-road before that--but
at the broken gate of the pah it stopped, and, the
dust falling away, proved it to be a Maori girl,
with a child on her back. The child’s legs hung
down limply below the thick shawl, and its head
rolled limply on its neck. It gazed with blue
eyes at its mother’s back, and its hair was fair,
like that of the pakeha.
Slowly the girl came through the gate, and
slowly up to the meeting-house. There was a
rough carving of Maui pulling up the North Island
on the front beam, and she dropped her eyes from
it. For who was she to look upon a god ?
At the closed-in end of the verandah-place stood
a carved white man among the lesser gods. He
was one of the few quite good men on record, and
the girl did not want to look at him either. She
paused before the women, but did not lift her eyes.
For she had been too long with English ways to
meet them face to face, and—knowing English
ways—she was afraid.
“Tena, koutou,” she said, softly, and the old
women looked up. Some were her aunts and
grandmothers ; one was her mother.
“ Tena koe,” said they, and the mother got up,
waddling across the grass.
‘‘ He tamaiti! ” she cried, and pulled the child
out of the shawl, grunting in admiration.
“I have come back,” said the girl, un-
emotionally. “I am weary. Can I sleep 1 ”
“ Koia! My bed is near empty these days,”
and the mother chuckled with her lips to the pale
baby-skin.
Then she followed the girl into the meeting-
house, and squatted on the earth.
“ He is go marry a white wahine ? ” she said, in
the careful English that was meant as compliment
to her daughter.
The girl nodded, with all the carven gods of her
ancestors frowning down on her as they had
frowned in her childhood.
“He aha!”
The girl spread her hands in speechless gesture.
They who say that the Maori is excitable speak
truth. But they who say that he is phlegmatic
speak truth also.
“I know,” said the old woman with serene
certainty; “I know why. Our skin shrivels
early, and when our teeth go we are not good to
see. The pakeha think that our souls change
with our skins, and they marry to a white girl.
They are all fools, and of a tribe of fools. But
the child’s skin will not shrivel.”
The girl laid the boy on the mat and went to
sleep beside him. And thereafter she went about
her old work in the pah, and there was none made
comment, nor showed any concern. For they are
a wise people, the Maoris, and they quite under-
stand the white man’s limitations, and grant him
license therefor.
But the girl understood a little more—which in
her case totalled-up to so much less—and so the
side which the pakeha made English went in
shame and self-condemnation and all the other
things that are the sign of an enlightened intellect.
And the white straight man who made the
white straight road —this pakeha said to the men
of his caste when they spoke of the Outer World
that lay by the roadside : “ Savages, and ignorant
to the last degree, every one of them, I give you
my word.” Which was quite natural, because
the pakeha did not know any better.
There is very little that matters in this story
when you come to think of it, because no one made
talk of it —not even when the boy grew up to
despise his people.
For—having white blood in him—that was quite
natural too.
G. B. LANCASTER.
“’Hoppers.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We were cutting scrub for sheep on Carson’s, and
our camp contained some really competent liars,
but all had to bow before the cook—a weird-
looking little object with a face as expressionless
as a copper dinner-plate, and an eye like a dead
mussel. Black Bill, a huge roach-backed mass of
humanity, with a figure like a badly-made S, had
been holding forth at some length upon the dis-
astrous effect of the grasshoppers during the
previous spring, when the cook chipped in
thusly:—
“Grasshoppers! Last spring! You should
have been with us two years ago, when we was
tank-sinkin’ on Yalgogrin. We’d been gettin’ on
first-rate, an’ was within a week of finishin’, when
one mornin’ the boss comes out to see how we’re
progressin’, and casually mentions that the
’hoppers was pretty bad at the homestead.
Well, of course, we couldn’t see how they was
goin’ to affect us, but we soon found out. A
couple of days later, as we was goin’ to work,
Jimmy, the ploughman, calls our attention to the
fac’ that the ’hoppers was cornin’ in earnest; and
by dinner-time they was six inches deep in the
tank, and the horses slippin’ and failin’ on the
batters. After dinner they was upwards of three
feet of ’hopper in the tank, an’ altho’ we worked
like blazes we couldn’t make no head against ’em ;
so after a bit we give ’em best, an’ started won-
derin’ what was the nex’ move.
“Presently the ole man suggests buildin’ a
brush brake about ten feet high all round the
tank Outside the dirt tip. We started at once,
an’ by sundown had the job finished. In the
mornin’ the ’hoppers hadn’t riz any, an’ we felt
better accordin’. After leadin’ the horses in we
closed up the gateway an’ started scoopin’, but
about the same time the ’hoppers got on the
move agen outside. In a couple of hours they
was flowin’ in over the brake a sight faster’n we
could scoop ’em out, so we had to give ’em best
again.
“For two days the air was so full of ’em that
we couldn’t breathe comfortable outside the tent,
but by the third mornin’ the most of ’em was gone
an’ things began to clear up a bit. Of course we
goes to have a look at the tank first thing, but
be’old, there wasn’t any tank ! It was full of
’hoppers right up to the top of the brake, an’
speakin’ moderately, there must have been at
least twenty thousand cubic yards of ’em packed
in that tank. We was all broke-up, an’ the ole
cove fairly wept.
“Presently, though, he bounds up with a yell,
an’ grabs a firestick an’ rushes at the brake an’
starts firin’ it all round. We joined in, an’ as
there was a lolj of dry Stuff in it, it was soon
blazin’. Presently the hoppers began to heave
and stir, an’ all at once with one vast, almighty,
concentrated kick they scattered that brake all
over the landscape.
“Of course there was a fire, an’ we was away
beltin’ at it for three days. When we got back
the ’hoppers was all dead an’ had subsided a good
deal, but the tank was still half full and smelt bad.
But we wired in, an’ although we had to plough
the last couple of feet on account of the horses
treadin’ ’em down solid, we shifted that mass of
’hopper an’ finished the tank inside a week.” .
Then we retired silently to our bunks.
THE WASTER.
The re-arming of the British Army with a
new rifle is to be carried out at once. The
weapon is called the Lee-Enfield Modified, and
is five inches shorter than the old one. The bore
is slightly enlarged from ten inches back to the
muzzle, doing away with a lot of friction, and so
giving greater range. The mechanism altogether
is very much, simpler than the old pattern, and
the loading will be done with clips, as with the
Mauser. Also, Australia will be expected to buy
up the old discarded rifles as usual.

“X. ” : In a Ballarat Star of 1874 I came across an
astounding case of an aetor paying off his old debts. The
paragraph gratefully records that Mr. R. Lawrence
(father of ; Ada Lawrence), who came to grief at the
Theatre Royal, Ballarat, some three years previously',
had been remitting money from India in settlement of
his old liabilities, “ the final payment of a small account
due to this office, the existence of which had been almost
forgotten, having been made to-day.” That was 30
years ago. Nowadays, actors and managers don’t trouble
to disturb the forgetfulness of newspapers re their
ancient advt. accounts.

Melbourne Harbinger op Light is still harping on the
subject of two Italian mediums, sons of a certain
Baroness Bordonaro, who, not being at all musical them-
selves, have been selected by some spirits to take down
an operatic score from dictation. Spirit Felix started to
convey the libretto of an opera to the younger of the
Bordonaro brethren on Dec. 10, 1901, and completed it at
subsequent sittings up to May 20 last year. The music
was dictated simultaneously by Jo (another spirit). It
seems that at the outset of this wondrous task another
spirit named Assova chipped in and made himself un-
pleasant. He was discovered to have been a former
friend of the Bondonaro family, who died in a lunatic
asylum. His interruptions ceased after a time, so it is
probable that the sane spirits gagged him. The spirit
title given to this opera was, “ I Travolti,” and the excel-
lence of the first act drew a testimonial, says the Har-
binger, from Signor Sapio, conductor of the orchestra
at the Urso-Sapio concerts long ago. For startling musi-
cal items one must go to the spiritualistic press.
Charley Brookfield’s just issued volume of reminis-
cences is worth glancing over. He was a popular London
comedian not long ago, and his yarns are mostly good.
Here is one : A Guardsman went to a London Music Hall
and persuaded the chairman that he was Signor Vesuvio,
the great Italian conjuror. He went on the stage to
oblige with a turn. Borrowed a nice new belltopper,
a sharp knife, and a shawl from three different people.
Then he cut off the rim of the hat with the knife, put
them under the shawl, and said, “ I will leave these for
two minutes, and you shall see what you shall see.” The
band struck up the “ Blue Danube ’’ as he went off at the
vying. He hurried to the stage door and took a hansom.
The May number of the New Idea contains an appre-
ciation of the late Charles Clark by his old friend and
manager, R. S. Smythe. “ People have talked about the
large audiences drawn by Madame Melba,” says R.S.S.,
“but the audiences that went to hear the diva sing
could not be compared with the crowds that flocked to
hear the Rev. Chas. Clark.” That was during his first
lecture tour, and he subsequently made four tours with
slowly diminishing success. Clark’s friendship with
Smythe extended over 25 years. Had Clark paid a sixth
visit to the land of Smythe he might have saved his life.
Or he might have died on Smythe’s hands, which would
have been inconvenient for both parties.
“Beer-puller”: Re “Matchbox’s” statement (8.,
31/5/’O3) as to mummers being as plentiful as starving
stock and mostly in a worse condition ! The average
mummer (and I will admit their name is legion, as
mummers, not as actors or artists) has the cast-iron
cheek of a penny-in-the-slot machine out of order,
coupled with the lying, hypnotic duplicity of Yankee
confidence salesmen. Ask any pubkeeper on the main
routes patronised by swindling mummers, and he
will tell you the same story of hall rents unpaid,
meals and beds (the best in the house), and not a
cent forthcoming. All the mummer (not the actor) wants
to do, is to live from day to day. Women are taking to
this confidence game, and are the keenest at it. Ask
any pressman, printer, publican, bellman, carrier, hall-
keeper, etc., etc., and if “Matchbox” can bring one
of these Vic. individuals (sober, mind) to say the average
mummer does not live on the fat of the land, then I’ll
shout champagne for “(Matchbox.” Why, mumming, so-
called, is the greatest confidence game abroad on the
roads to-day, and the worst of it is that all the satisfaction
you can get out of the presumptuous swindlers is to
seize their carpet-bag of bricks (for they never bring their
duplicate kit to their diggings), while they skip with
their properties worn under their knock-about-the-streets
garb. There are honest companies, of course, but they
are actors—not mummers. I know some of the honest
ones—Dan Barry’s, for a shining example.
Thackeray and his friend Higgins, known as Jacob
Omnium, went to see a giant at a fair. Thackeray was
6ft. 4in., and Higgins 6ft. 6in. The proprietor said,
“ Gents, if you’re in the profession, there’s no charge.”
An amateur actor recently had a high compliment paid
him. He wag the comedian of a company playing before
the patients of a North London lunatic asylum. The
performance was going splendidly', as he thought,
and he grimaced and clowned, and the audience shrieked
with laughter. Finally a gmall voice came from the
back of the room—“ You’d better stay ’ere, sir ! ”
Melb. Age dramatic critic let lbose his pent-up feelings
over Musgrove’s production of “ As You Like It,” and
poured forth a quarter-column of ancient English
geography and natural history thusly : “ Wolves were to
be found in the highlands of Scotland, herds of wild cattle
in Northumberland, beavers in Wales, eagles in Shrop-
shire, and badgers everywhere.” If he had also added
that the cockroach was reasonably numerous, his in-
formation would have left nothing to be desired.
How they enjoy the play up at Charters Towers (Q.).
Thus the local Telegraph, describing What happened
after a woman in the theatre audience had fainted “ when
Nemo, the Vulture, was shaking his long claws at all and
sundry ” :—“The curtain dropped and the crowd pressed
round the female in distress. ‘ Twist her beak,’ said one,
4 that’s the thing to revive her.’ Then another shouted :
‘ Stand back you silly fools and give ’er plenty of hair.
It’s hair she wants.’ • Smoke her out,’said another, and
forthwith a dozen smokers who were looking on com-
menced to puff smoke into her face. She revived. But
when the curtain rolled up on a new act things were only
seen.through a dim mist of blue smoke.”
The London correspondent of the Argus tells the
candid truth about Miss Bertha Bird, a Melbourne g rl
who journeyed to England awhile ago on the strength of
an absurdly abnormal top note. Miss Bird, it seems,
gave a “successful concert at St. James’ Hall” —the
same successful concert that all aspirants give if funds
and friends will run to it—and repeated her oft sung
“ cadenza terminating in Ain alt.” Melbourne remem-
bers Miss Bird and her A in alt without the least
enthusiasm. The Argus man, for a wonder, admits that
“ except for this wonderful highness Miss Bird’s voice is
not remarkable ”; also that in an air from “ Ernani ” “ the
singer was clearly overweighted and her intonation was
faulty.” All the same, Miss Bird is likely to appear
occasionally in some more cable messages, and her
supposed triumphs will unsettle the minds .of other
girls who haven’t gone to Europe yet, but are pardonably
convinced that they ought to be able to equal Bertha's
“success.”
John Prouse, the ex-Maoriland timber merchant, who
went to the United States on a concert tour recently,
was 15 years ago a choir baritone of the common or
garden variety. After a little tuition, he went to the
Big Smoke, and, some 10 months after his arrival there,
was classed as a second Santley. Since his return, Mr.
Prouse has been called upon to sing with many big com-
panies touring Maoriland. Two years ago he and his
daughter travelled all over Australia with Mme. Dolores,
and now they are off to join her in an extended tour of
the United States and British Columbia.
